☕ Java Interview Mastery

1100 Questions for Indian MNC Interviews — 11 Topics × Top 100 — Easy → Conceptual

☕ Core Java — Top 100

ℹ️ Q1–30 fundamentals, Q31–75 intermediate, Q76–100 deep conceptual. Click any question to reveal the answer.

JVM is the virtual machine that executes bytecode (platform-specific). JRE = JVM + core libraries needed to run Java programs. JDK = JRE + development tools (javac, jar, javadoc, debugger) needed to build programs. JDK ⊃ JRE ⊃ JVM.

Java source compiles to bytecode, which is the same on every OS ("write once, run anywhere"). The JVM itself is a native program written separately for each OS/architecture — it translates that universal bytecode into platform-specific machine instructions.

public — JVM must call it from outside the class. static — JVM calls it without creating an object of the class. void — Java doesn't return an exit code via return value (use System.exit(n)). String[] args carries command-line arguments.

static means the member belongs to the class, not an instance — one copy shared by all objects. It applies to: variables (shared state), methods (callable without object), blocks (run once at class loading), and nested classes (don't need outer instance).

A static method runs without any object — there is no this reference. Instance variables live inside objects, so the compiler can't know which object's variable you mean. Instance methods can access static members because the class is always loaded.

Static blocks run once when the class is loaded, before main and before any object creation, in the order they appear. Instance initializer blocks run every time an object is created, after the super constructor but before the class constructor body.

class Demo {
    static { System.out.println("1. static block"); }
    { System.out.println("2. instance block"); }
    Demo() { System.out.println("3. constructor"); }
}
// new Demo() twice → 1 prints once; 2,3 print twice

Overload — yes; the JVM only ever invokes main(String[]), but other overloads can exist and be called manually. Override — no; static methods are never overridden, only hidden in a subclass.

final variable — value/reference can't be reassigned (the referenced object can still mutate). final method — can't be overridden. final class — can't be extended (e.g. String, Integer). Final fields must be assigned exactly once, by declaration or in every constructor.

final — keyword: restricts reassignment/overriding/inheritance. finally — block: always executes after try/catch (cleanup). finalize() — method called by GC before reclaiming an object; unreliable and deprecated since Java 9 (use try-with-resources or Cleaner).

Key reasons: string pool sharing is only safe if values never change; security (file paths, class names, URLs can't be altered after checks); thread safety for free; and hashCode caching (safe to use as HashMap key). Internally the char[]/byte[] is final and never exposed.

A special area (in the heap since Java 7) where string literals are cached. Identical literals share one object. new String("a") always creates a new heap object outside the pool; intern() returns the pooled instance.

String a = "java";           // pool
String b = "java";           // same pooled object
String c = new String("java"); // new object
a == b  // true
a == c  // false; a.equals(c) → true

== compares references (same object in memory) for objects, and values for primitives. equals() compares logical content — its default in Object is same as ==, but classes like String/Integer override it for value equality. Always use equals() for object comparison.

If a.equals(b) is true, then a.hashCode() == b.hashCode() must hold. Unequal objects may share a hash (collision). Breaking the contract corrupts hash-based collections: a HashMap may store "equal" keys in different buckets, so get() can't find them. Always override both together.

Automatic conversion between primitives and wrappers: int → Integer (autoboxing) and Integer → int (unboxing). Watch out: unboxing a null wrapper throws NullPointerException, and boxing in hot loops creates garbage.

The Integer cache: autoboxing reuses cached objects for values -128 to 127 (via Integer.valueOf). Inside that range both references point to the same cached object; outside it, new objects are created. Moral: compare wrappers with equals(), never ==.

  • Encapsulation — bundle data + behavior, hide state behind private fields and public methods.
  • Abstraction — expose what something does, hide how (interfaces, abstract classes).
  • Inheritance — reuse and extend behavior via is-a relationships (extends).
  • Polymorphism — one interface, many implementations; calls resolved by actual object type at runtime.

Overloading — same name, different parameter list, same class (or inherited); resolved at compile time (static polymorphism). Overriding — subclass redefines a superclass method with the same signature; resolved at runtime by the actual object type (dynamic polymorphism). Return type can differ for overloading; overriding allows only covariant returns.

  • Same name and parameter list; return type same or covariant (subtype).
  • Access modifier can't be more restrictive (protected → public ✔, public → private ✘).
  • Can't throw new/broader checked exceptions; unchecked are fine.
  • private, static, final methods can't be overridden.
  • Use @Override to let the compiler verify.

Abstraction is design-level: hide implementation complexity, expose a contract (e.g. List interface — you don't care if it's array-backed). Encapsulation is implementation-level: hide the data itself behind access control (private fields + getters). Abstraction hides complexity; encapsulation hides and protects state.

  • Abstract class: can hold state (instance fields), constructors, any access modifiers; single inheritance. Use for is-a hierarchies sharing code/state (e.g. AbstractList).
  • Interface: contract only — no instance state; multiple inheritance of type; methods are implicitly public; default/static/private methods allowed (Java 8/9+). Use for capabilities (Comparable, Serializable).
  • Rule of thumb: interface first; abstract class only when shared state/protected helpers are needed.

Private — yes (singletons, factory-only classes, utility classes). final/static/abstract — no; constructors are not inherited and never overridden, so those modifiers are meaningless and illegal. A subclass constructor always invokes a superclass constructor (implicitly super() if not written).

Calling one constructor from another. this(...) calls another constructor of the same class; super(...) calls the parent constructor. Either must be the first statement, so you can't use both in one constructor. If neither is written, the compiler inserts super().

Always pass-by-value. For objects, the value of the reference is copied — so a method can mutate the object the reference points to, but reassigning the parameter to a new object has no effect on the caller's variable.

void change(List<Integer> list, StringBuilder sb) {
    list.add(99);             // visible to caller (same object)
    sb = new StringBuilder(); // NOT visible (local copy of reference)
}
  • private — same class only.
  • default (no modifier) — same package only.
  • protected — same package + subclasses anywhere.
  • public — everywhere.

Top-level classes can only be public or default.

String — immutable; every "modification" creates a new object. StringBuilder — mutable, not thread-safe, fastest; default choice for concatenation in loops. StringBuffer — mutable, synchronized (thread-safe), slower; rarely needed today. Note: the compiler already converts simple a + b + c into StringBuilder/invokedynamic — the problem is only concatenation inside loops.

An enum is a special class representing a fixed set of instances (e.g. days, states). Unlike public static final int constants, enums are type-safe (can't pass an invalid value), have a namespace, can carry fields/methods, work in switch, and are safely singleton per constant.

values() returns all constants as an array (in declaration order). valueOf("NAME") returns the constant with that exact name or throws IllegalArgumentException. ordinal() returns the declaration index — never persist ordinal (reordering constants breaks data); store name() or a custom code instead.

Throwable is the root → Error (serious JVM problems: OutOfMemoryError, StackOverflowError — don't catch) and Exception. Exception splits into checked (IOException, SQLException — must be declared/handled) and RuntimeException (unchecked: NullPointerException, IllegalArgumentException, ClassCastException).

Checked: recoverable, external conditions the caller should anticipate (file missing, network down) — compiler forces handling. Unchecked: programming bugs or violated preconditions (null, bad argument) — fix the code, don't catch. Modern style (incl. Spring) prefers unchecked for most custom exceptions to avoid throws-clause pollution.

throw — statement that actually raises one exception object: throw new IllegalStateException("..."). throws — method-signature clause that declares which checked exceptions may propagate: void read() throws IOException.

Yes — the constructor is implicitly private and runs once per constant. Each constant can even override methods (constant-specific bodies).

enum Planet {
    EARTH(5.97e24), MARS(6.42e23);
    private final double mass;
    Planet(double mass) { this.mass = mass; }
    double mass() { return mass; }
}

enum Op {
    ADD { int apply(int a, int b) { return a + b; } },
    MUL { int apply(int a, int b) { return a * b; } };
    abstract int apply(int a, int b);
}

The JVM guarantees an enum constant is created once, thread-safely, at class load. Enums are immune to the two classic singleton breakers: reflection (constructor calls on enums throw) and serialization (deserialization returns the same constant, no readResolve needed).

enum AppConfig {
    INSTANCE;
    void load() { /* ... */ }
}

Specialized collections for enum keys/elements. EnumSet is backed by a bit vector — far faster and smaller than HashSet. EnumMap is backed by an array indexed by ordinal — faster than HashMap and iterates in natural (declaration) order. Use them whenever keys/elements are enums.

Resources declared in try (...) are closed automatically in reverse order when the block exits — even on exception. Any class implementing AutoCloseable (one close() method) qualifies. It eliminates leak-prone finally blocks.

try (var in = new FileInputStream("a.txt");
     var out = new FileOutputStream("b.txt")) {
    in.transferTo(out);
}   // out closed first, then in — guaranteed

In try-with-resources, if the try block throws and close() also throws, the close-exception doesn't replace the original — it is attached as suppressed and retrievable via throwable.getSuppressed(). With a manual finally block the original would be silently lost — a key advantage of try-with-resources.

The finally return wins — it overwrites any value or even swallows the exception in flight, which is why returning from finally is a code smell (most linters flag it). Similarly, finally doesn't run only if the JVM dies (System.exit, crash, kill).

int test() {
    try { return 1; }
    finally { return 2; }  // returns 2 — and would even hide an exception
}

Extend RuntimeException (or Exception for checked) and provide standard constructors — especially one taking a cause so stack traces chain properly.

public class PaymentFailedException extends RuntimeException {
    public PaymentFailedException(String message) { super(message); }
    public PaymentFailedException(String message, Throwable cause) {
        super(message, cause);   // never lose the root cause
    }
}

Best practices: meaningful name ending in Exception, keep them immutable, add context fields (orderId etc.), don't use exceptions for flow control.

catch (IOException | SQLException e) handles multiple types in one block (Java 7+). Restrictions: the types must not be in a subclass relationship with each other, and e is implicitly final — you can't reassign it.

  • Declare the class final (or make constructors private + static factories).
  • All fields private final; no setters.
  • Initialize everything in the constructor.
  • Defensive-copy mutable inputs (Date, collections) on the way in AND out (or wrap with List.copyOf).
public final class Money {
    private final BigDecimal amount;
    private final List<String> tags;
    public Money(BigDecimal amount, List<String> tags) {
        this.amount = amount;
        this.tags = List.copyOf(tags);   // defensive copy
    }
    public List<String> getTags() { return tags; } // already unmodifiable
}

Shallow copy duplicates the object but shares nested object references; deep copy recursively duplicates everything. Object.clone() does a shallow field-by-field copy and requires implementing the marker Cloneable (else CloneNotSupportedException). clone() is widely considered broken — prefer copy constructors, static factory methods, or serialization-based copying.

Serialization converts an object graph to a byte stream (Serializable marker + ObjectOutputStream) for storage or network transfer. serialVersionUID is a version stamp checked during deserialization — if the class changed and IDs don't match, InvalidClassException is thrown. Always declare it explicitly; otherwise the compiler generates one that changes with any class edit.

transient fields are skipped during serialization (passwords, caches, non-serializable members) and come back as default values (null/0/false). static fields are never serialized either — they belong to the class, not the instance.

A static nested class doesn't hold a reference to the outer instance — instantiate with new Outer.Nested(). A (non-static) inner class implicitly carries an outer-instance reference (Outer.this) and needs one to exist: outer.new Inner(). Prefer static nested classes — inner classes can cause memory leaks by keeping the outer object alive (classic with anonymous listeners).

An unnamed class declared and instantiated in one expression — implements an interface or extends a class on the spot. Differences vs lambda: anonymous classes get their own this (lambda's this is the enclosing instance), can have state/multiple methods, can extend classes, and compile to separate class files. Lambdas only work for functional interfaces and use invokedynamic.

Generics add compile-time type parameters to classes/methods (List<String>), introduced in Java 5 to provide type safety (no ClassCastException at runtime) and eliminate manual casting. Before generics, collections held raw Objects and errors surfaced only at runtime.

Generic type info is removed at compile time — at runtime List<String> and List<Integer> are both just List (backward compatibility with pre-Java-5 bytecode). Consequences: no new T(), no T.class, no instanceof List<String>, no generic arrays (new T[10]), and overloads differing only in type parameter clash. Workaround: pass Class<T> tokens explicitly.

PECS = Producer Extends, Consumer Super. Use ? extends T when you only read T's from the structure (it produces); use ? super T when you only write T's into it (it consumes).

// reads from src (producer), writes to dst (consumer)
static <T> void copy(List<? super T> dst, List<? extends T> src)

You can't add to a List<? extends T> (except null) because the actual element type is unknown.

An interface with no methods used to tag a class with a capability: Serializable, Cloneable, RandomAccess. The JVM/libraries check it with instanceof. Annotations can do the same job with metadata, but a marker interface participates in the type system — you can write void save(Serializable s) and get compile-time checking.

"abc" creates (or reuses) one pooled object. new String("abc") creates a new heap object every call, plus the pooled literal if not already present — so up to two objects for the famous interview phrasing. s.intern() returns the canonical pooled instance.

Wrappers (Integer, Long…) are immutable for safe caching (the valueOf pools), thread safety, and reliable use as map keys. char is 2 bytes because Java adopted UTF-16 — a char is a UTF-16 code unit; characters outside the BMP (emoji etc.) need two chars (a surrogate pair), which is why String.length() can differ from the real character count (codePointCount).

ArrayList: backed by a resizable array — O(1) random access, amortized O(1) append, O(n) middle insert/remove (shifts elements). LinkedList: doubly-linked nodes — O(1) insert/remove at a known node, but O(n) to reach it, poor cache locality, more memory per element. In practice ArrayList wins almost always; LinkedList only when you mostly add/remove at both ends — and even then ArrayDeque is better.

  • put(k,v): compute hash(k) (key's hashCode spread with (h ^ h>>>16)), index = hash & (capacity-1) → bucket.
  • Bucket holds a linked list of nodes; equal hash → walk list comparing with equals(); replace value or append node.
  • When size exceeds capacity × loadFactor (default 16 × 0.75), the table doubles and rehashes.
  • Since Java 8: a bucket with > 8 nodes (and table ≥ 64) converts to a red-black tree → worst case O(log n) instead of O(n).

Power-of-two capacity lets the index be computed with a cheap bit mask hash & (n-1) instead of modulo, and guarantees even distribution of the spread hash bits. Load factor (default 0.75) is the fill ratio that triggers resize — lower = fewer collisions but more memory; higher = denser but slower lookups.

The entry becomes unreachable: it was stored in the bucket computed from the old hashCode, but lookups now compute a different bucket. get() returns null, yet the entry still occupies memory — a subtle leak. That's why keys should be immutable (String, Integer, records).

  • HashMap — not thread-safe, allows one null key and null values; fastest single-threaded.
  • Hashtable — legacy, every method synchronized (one big lock), no nulls; never use in new code.
  • ConcurrentHashMap — thread-safe with fine-grained locking (per-bin CAS/synchronized since Java 8), no null keys/values, no locking for reads, atomic helpers (computeIfAbsent, merge). The correct concurrent choice.

HashSet is literally a HashMap in disguise — each element is stored as a key with a shared dummy object (PRESENT) as the value. So uniqueness, performance, and the equals/hashCode requirements are all inherited from HashMap. add() returns false when the key already existed.

LinkedHashMap maintains a doubly-linked list of entries. Construct it with accessOrder=true so every get moves the entry to the tail, then override the eviction hook:

Map<K, V> lru = new LinkedHashMap<>(16, 0.75f, true) {
    protected boolean removeEldestEntry(Map.Entry<K, V> eldest) {
        return size() > MAX_ENTRIES;   // evict least-recently-used
    }
};

TreeMap is a red-black tree keeping keys sorted — O(log n) operations vs HashMap's O(1), but it gives ordered iteration and navigation methods: firstKey, floorKey, ceilingKey, headMap, tailMap, subMap. Use it for range queries, sorted views, or "nearest key" lookups. Keys must be Comparable or you must pass a Comparator; it uses compareTo, not equals.

Comparable — the class defines its own natural order via compareTo (one per class, e.g. String alphabetical). Comparator — external strategy object; define unlimited orderings without touching the class.

employees.sort(Comparator.comparing(Employee::getSalary).reversed()
                          .thenComparing(Employee::getName));

Fail-fast (ArrayList, HashMap iterators): detect structural modification during iteration via a modCount check and throw ConcurrentModificationException — best effort, not guaranteed. Fail-safe (CopyOnWriteArrayList, ConcurrentHashMap): iterate over a snapshot or weakly-consistent view — no exception, but may not reflect concurrent changes. To remove while iterating a normal collection use iterator.remove() or removeIf().

It returns a fixed-size view backed by the array — add/remove throw UnsupportedOperationException, while set writes through to the original array. Also, passing an int[] gives a List<int[]> of size 1 (primitives aren't autoboxed element-wise). For a real list: new ArrayList<>(Arrays.asList(...)).

List.of() (Java 9+) is fully immutable (even set throws), rejects null elements, and is not backed by the input array. Arrays.asList() is a mutable-by-set, fixed-size, null-tolerant view. Prefer List.of for constants; List.copyOf to defensively copy.

A binary min-heap on an array: offer/poll are O(log n), peek O(1). Only the head is guaranteed smallest — iteration order is NOT sorted (common trap). For max-heap pass Comparator.reverseOrder(). Nulls are not allowed; it's not thread-safe (use PriorityBlockingQueue).

Stack extends Vector — every method synchronized, legacy design. ArrayDeque is a resizable circular array: faster than both for stack (push/pop) and queue (offer/poll) operations, cache-friendly, no per-node allocation. It disallows null (null is the "empty" sentinel for poll/peek).

A map whose keys are held by weak references — when a key has no strong references elsewhere, GC reclaims it and the entry vanishes automatically. Classic use: metadata caches keyed by objects you don't own (e.g. Class → computed info), so the cache never prevents garbage collection.

A map that compares keys with == (reference identity) instead of equals(), and uses System.identityHashCode. Used in frameworks for object-graph traversal (serialization, deep-copy cycle detection) where two equal-but-distinct objects must be tracked separately.

equals, hashCode, toString, clone, finalize, getClass, wait, notify, notifyAll. Commonly overridden: equals + hashCode (value semantics) and toString (logging). wait/notify/notifyAll are final (can't override); finalize is deprecated. Records auto-generate equals/hashCode/toString.

The subclass override runs before the subclass constructor and field initializers execute — it can observe uninitialized (null/0) state.

class Parent { Parent() { init(); } void init() {} }
class Child extends Parent {
    private String name = "set";
    void init() { System.out.println(name); }  // prints null!
}

Rule: constructors should call only private/final/static methods.

String[] IS-A Object[] (covariant) — a pre-generics design that pushes type errors to runtime: storing an Integer into a String[] through an Object[] reference throws ArrayStoreException. Generics are invariant (List<String> is NOT a List<Object>) so the same mistake fails at compile time; wildcards (? extends/super) restore controlled flexibility.

void log(String fmt, Object... args) — the compiler wraps trailing arguments into an array. Rules: at most one varargs parameter, and it must be last. Risks: ambiguity with overloads, passing an array vs single element confusion, and heap pollution with generic varargs (hence @SafeVarargs).

instanceof tests runtime type (null is never an instance of anything → false). Java 16 added pattern matching: if (obj instanceof String s) tests and binds the cast in one step, eliminating the test-then-cast boilerplate and its mismatch bugs.

Collections.unmodifiableList(list) is a read-only window onto the original — if the underlying list changes, the view changes too. List.copyOf(list) / List.of(...) create independent immutable snapshots. Returning an unmodifiable view of internal state is fine for encapsulation, but it is not a defensive copy.

intern() returns the canonical instance from the string pool, adding the string if absent — enabling == comparison and deduplication. Dangers: the pool is a fixed-size hashtable (-XX:StringTableSize); interning huge numbers of unique strings degrades performance and retains memory. Prefer G1's automatic -XX:+UseStringDeduplication for heap savings.

If a class implements two interfaces that both define the same default method, the compiler forces the class to resolve the conflict by overriding it (optionally delegating with InterfaceA.super.method()). Class-wins rule: a method inherited from a superclass always beats interface defaults.

class C implements A, B {
    public void hello() { A.super.hello(); }   // explicit choice
}

A static method with the same signature in a subclass hides (not overrides) the parent's. Resolution is by reference type at compile time, not the object's runtime type — no polymorphism.

class A { static String who() { return "A"; } }
class B extends A { static String who() { return "B"; } }
A obj = new B();
obj.who();   // "A" — decided by reference type, not the actual object
  • Loading — read .class bytes, create a Class object.
  • Linking — verification (bytecode validity), preparation (static fields get default values), resolution (symbolic refs → direct refs, often lazy).
  • Initialization — run static initializers and static field assignments (<clinit>), exactly once, thread-safely.

Three built-in loaders: Bootstrap (core java.* classes) → Platform/Extension → Application (classpath). On a load request, each loader first delegates to its parent; only if the parent can't find the class does it try itself. This prevents spoofing core classes (your evil java.lang.String never loads) and guarantees one shared definition of JDK classes. Web containers (Tomcat) deliberately invert this per webapp for isolation.

Yes — if loaded by different classloaders. A class's runtime identity is (classloader, FQN). The two versions are unrelated types: casting between them throws ClassCastException ("X cannot be cast to X" — the classic app-server error). This is how containers isolate webapps and how plugins load different library versions.

Initialization is lazy — triggered by first instance creation, static method call, non-constant static field access, reflection, or subclass init. Accessing a compile-time constant (static final primitive/String with constant initializer) does NOT trigger init: the value is inlined into the caller at compile time. That inlining is also why changing a constant in a library requires recompiling its clients.

31 is an odd prime — primes spread bits well and reduce collisions; odd avoids losing information on overflow (multiplying by an even number shifts bits out). Bonus: 31 * i == (i << 5) - i, which JVMs optimize to shift-and-subtract. Objects.hash(...) uses the same scheme.

If Point.equals uses instanceof and subclass ColorPoint adds a field, then point.equals(colorPoint) can be true while colorPoint.equals(point) is false — symmetry violated. Using getClass() == o.getClass() restores symmetry but breaks substitutability (a subclass that adds no state is never equal). Effective Java's verdict: you cannot extend an instantiable class with a new value component while preserving the contract — prefer composition.

Sorted collections use compareTo/compare exclusively — two objects comparing to 0 are treated as duplicates even if !equals(). Example: new BigDecimal("1.0") and new BigDecimal("1.00") — unequal by equals, but a TreeSet keeps only one, while a HashSet keeps both. Such a collection silently violates the Set/Map contract; the Comparable javadoc strongly recommends consistency.

Synthetic methods the compiler generates to make generics (after erasure) and covariant returns work polymorphically. If class MyComparator implements Comparator<String> defines compare(String,String), the compiler also emits a hidden compare(Object,Object) that casts and delegates — that's what erased call sites actually invoke. Visible via reflection (method.isBridge()), occasionally causing duplicate-method confusion.

Heap pollution = a variable of parameterized type pointing at an object of the wrong type parameter (possible because of erasure) — the ClassCastException then explodes far from the actual bug. Generic varargs create an Object[] that can be misused this way, hence the compiler warning. @SafeVarargs is the author's promise that the method only reads the array and never stores into it or leaks it — it suppresses the warning at call sites; allowed only on static/final/private methods (non-overridable).

The compiler generates a two-stage switch: first switch on s.hashCode() to a candidate case, then confirm with equals() (hash collisions between case labels are handled), then a second switch on a computed index runs the actual case body. Consequence: switching on a null String throws NullPointerException at the hashCode() call.

Since Java 9, a + b compiles to a single invokedynamic call bootstrapped by StringConcatFactory — the JVM picks the optimal concat strategy at runtime (often building the exact-size byte[] directly), instead of the Java-8 hardcoded StringBuilder chain. Benefit: faster concatenation without recompiling, and future strategies apply automatically. Loops are still the exception — reuse an explicit StringBuilder there.

Constant folding: concatenation of compile-time constants is computed by the compiler and stored as the pooled literal "ab" — same reference. With variables, concatenation happens at runtime producing a fresh heap object outside the pool. Making s1, s2 final with constant initializers turns them back into compile-time constants → true again.

Arithmetic silently wraps around two's-complement: Integer.MAX_VALUE + 1 == Integer.MIN_VALUE — no exception. Famous victim: binary search's (low + high) / 2; fix with low + (high - low) / 2 or >>> 1. To fail loudly use Math.addExact / multiplyExact (throws ArithmeticException), or use long/BigInteger for bigger ranges.

Doubles are binary (IEEE 754) — 0.1 and 0.2 have no exact binary representation, so the sum is 0.30000000000000004. Never compare doubles with ==; use an epsilon. For money use BigDecimal created from String (new BigDecimal("0.1") — the double constructor captures the inexact value!) with explicit scale/RoundingMode, or store paise/cents as long. Also: BigDecimal's equals compares scale too (1.0 ≠ 1.00) — compare with compareTo.

NaN != NaN (only value not equal to itself; test with Double.isNaN), yet Double.valueOf(NaN).equals(NaN) is true — equals/compareTo deliberately diverge from IEEE so sorting and hashing work. Similarly 0.0 == -0.0 is true but Double.valueOf(0.0).equals(-0.0) is false. Collections use the equals semantics.

Runtime inspection/manipulation of classes, fields, and methods (Class, Method, Field) — the engine behind Spring DI, Hibernate, Jackson, JUnit. Costs: slower than direct calls (though caching + JIT help), no compile-time safety, breaks encapsulation via setAccessible(true). Since Java 9/16, the module system blocks reflective access to JDK internals (InaccessibleObjectException) unless opened with --add-opens — a top migration pain point.

Proxy.newProxyInstance(loader, interfaces, handler) generates a class at runtime implementing the given interfaces; every call is routed to InvocationHandler.invoke(proxy, method, args) where you add behavior around delegation. This is how Spring AOP proxies interface-based beans (CGLIB subclassing covers classes without interfaces) and how Feign/MyBatis turn interfaces into implementations.

Service proxy = (Service) Proxy.newProxyInstance(
    cl, new Class[]{Service.class},
    (p, method, args) -> {
        log.info("before {}", method.getName());
        return method.invoke(target, args);
    });

SOURCE — discarded by compiler (@Override, Lombok). CLASS — in the .class file but not loaded at runtime (default; bytecode tools). RUNTIME — readable via reflection (@Autowired, @Test, @Entity). Frameworks scan the classpath, load classes, and call isAnnotationPresent / getAnnotation to drive behavior. Custom annotations also declare @Target (where they may appear).

Java's plugin pattern: an API defines an interface; providers ship implementations declared in META-INF/services/<interface-FQN> (or module provides...with); ServiceLoader.load(Iface.class) discovers and instantiates them at runtime. Used by JDBC drivers (no more Class.forName), logging backends (SLF4J), charset providers — and how Spring Boot's old spring.factories concept is modeled.

Deserialization creates a new instance bypassing constructors — two "singletons" now exist. Fix: implement readResolve() returning the existing INSTANCE; the deserialized copy is discarded. The enum singleton avoids the issue entirely (enum serialization is by name).

private Object readResolve() { return INSTANCE; }

constructor.setAccessible(true); constructor.newInstance() creates a second instance despite the private constructor. Defenses: throw from the constructor if INSTANCE already exists; or use an enum — the JVM itself forbids reflective instantiation of enums (Constructor.newInstance throws IllegalArgumentException for enum types).

Arrays don't override equals/hashCode — they use identity, so two arrays with the same contents are different keys, and a re-created array never finds the old entry. Compare with Arrays.equals (1-D) or Arrays.deepEquals (nested); hash with Arrays.hashCode / deepHashCode. As map keys, use List.of(...) or a record wrapping the values instead.

  1. Parent static fields + static blocks (once, at class load)
  2. Child static fields + static blocks (once)
  3. Parent instance fields + instance blocks
  4. Parent constructor body
  5. Child instance fields + instance blocks
  6. Child constructor body

Statics top-down once; then per object: parent's instance init + constructor fully completes before the child's instance initializers run.

It produces the 4-char string "null" — string conversion of a null reference is defined as the literal "null" (via String.valueOf), so no NPE. + is the only "overloaded" operator in Java, and only for String, handled by the compiler (constant folding or invokedynamic concat). Corollary trap: System.out.println('a' + 1) prints 98 (int arithmetic), while "" + 'a' + 1 prints a1.

Inheritance couples the subclass to the parent's implementation: a harmless-looking change in the base (e.g. addAll internally calling add) silently breaks overriding subclasses — the fragile base class problem (classic example: extending HashSet to count insertions double-counts via addAll). Composition (hold a reference, delegate, implement an interface) couples only to the contract, allows swapping implementations, and works at runtime. Use inheritance only for genuine is-a with designed-for-extension classes; otherwise wrap (Decorator) — and that's exactly why Effective Java says "favor composition over inheritance."

🗑️ Garbage Collection & Memory Management — Top 100

ℹ️ Q1–30 fundamentals, Q31–75 collectors & tuning, Q76–100 deep internals & troubleshooting.

Automatic memory management: the JVM finds objects that are no longer reachable from live code and reclaims their heap memory. Developers don't free memory manually (no delete), eliminating whole bug classes (dangling pointers, double-free) at the cost of GC pause overhead.

  • Heap — all objects and arrays; shared; GC-managed.
  • Stack (per thread) — frames with local variables, operand stack, return addresses.
  • Metaspace — class metadata (native memory, since Java 8).
  • PC register (per thread) — current instruction pointer.
  • Native method stacks — for JNI code.
  • Plus the code cache for JIT-compiled machine code.

Stack: per-thread, stores method frames and local variables, LIFO, freed automatically on method return, small (~512KB–1MB default), overflow → StackOverflowError. Heap: shared by all threads, stores objects, freed by GC, large, exhaustion → OutOfMemoryError. Object references can live on the stack; the objects themselves are on the heap.

The reference variable p lives in the method's stack frame; the Person object (its fields, header) lives on the heap; the Person class metadata lives in Metaspace. When the method returns, p disappears — the object becomes garbage if nothing else references it.

When it is unreachable from any GC root — no chain of strong references leads to it. Typical ways: reference goes out of scope, reference reassigned, reference set to null, object removed from a collection, or an "island of isolation" forms. Eligibility ≠ immediate collection — GC runs when the JVM decides.

The starting points of reachability analysis: local variables on thread stacks, active threads themselves, static fields of loaded classes, JNI references, and system classes/monitors. Anything reachable by following references from these roots is live; everything else is garbage.

The heap is split generationally. Young generation — where new objects are allocated; collected frequently and cheaply (minor GC). Old (tenured) generation — objects that survived enough young collections get promoted here; collected rarely and more expensively. This works because of the generational hypothesis: most objects die young.

The young gen subdivides into Eden (new allocations) and two Survivor spaces, S0/S1. On a minor GC, live objects from Eden + the active survivor space are copied to the other survivor space (or promoted); Eden is then empty. Each survival increments the object's age; reaching the tenuring threshold (default ~15) promotes it to the old gen.

Minor GC — collects young gen only; frequent, fast. Major GC — collects the old gen. Full GC — collects the entire heap (young + old + metaspace); usually the longest stop-the-world pause and the one to watch for in production logs.

No. System.gc() (and Runtime.gc()) is only a suggestion — the JVM may ignore it, and when honored it typically triggers an expensive Full GC. Production guidance: never call it (some teams even disable it with -XX:+DisableExplicitGC).

finalize() has no timing guarantee (may never run), resurrects objects, slows GC, swallows exceptions, and enables attacks. Deprecated in Java 9, removed-for-good path in recent JDKs. Replacements: try-with-resources / AutoCloseable for deterministic cleanup, and java.lang.ref.Cleaner as a safety net for native resources.

PermGen was a fixed-size heap region for class metadata — its exhaustion caused the infamous OutOfMemoryError: PermGen space on redeploys. Java 8 replaced it with Metaspace in native memory, which grows automatically (capped only by -XX:MaxMetaspaceSize). Interned strings and static fields had already moved to the heap in Java 7.

Static fields are part of the Class object's state, stored on the heap (since Java 7/8 era JVMs), referenced from the class metadata in Metaspace. They act as GC roots — objects referenced by static fields stay alive as long as the class (and its classloader) is loaded, which is why static collections are leak suspects #1.

In the heap (moved from PermGen in Java 7), implemented as a native hashtable of references to String objects. Being heap-resident means pooled strings are garbage-collectable when unreferenced, and the pool size is tunable via -XX:StringTableSize.

-Xms = initial heap size, -Xmx = maximum heap size. Setting them equal avoids runtime heap resizing (which can trigger Full GCs and OS-level page faults), giving predictable memory footprint and latency — standard practice for server deployments and containers.

  • Java heap space — heap full; leak or undersized heap.
  • GC overhead limit exceeded — >98% time in GC reclaiming <2%; heap nearly full.
  • Metaspace — too many classes (classloader leaks, heavy proxy generation).
  • unable to create new native thread — OS thread limit hit, not heap.
  • Direct buffer memory — off-heap NIO buffers exhausted.

A thread's stack is exhausted — almost always unbounded/deep recursion (missing base case, accidental cyclic calls like A.toString → B.toString → A.toString). Fixes: correct the recursion, convert to iteration, or as a last resort raise stack size with -Xss (e.g. -Xss2m). Note it's an Error, not an Exception — don't catch it.

Yes — GC only frees unreachable objects. A leak in Java is keeping references to objects you no longer need: ever-growing static maps/caches, unremoved listeners, ThreadLocals in thread pools, unclosed resources, inner classes pinning outer instances. The memory is "reachable garbage" — GC can't touch it.

A phase where the JVM suspends all application threads so the GC can work on a consistent heap snapshot. Every collector has some STW phases (even ZGC has sub-millisecond ones); the goal of modern collectors is to shrink them by doing most work concurrently with the application.

G1 (Garbage-First) has been the default since Java 9, replacing Parallel GC (the Java 8 default). Selection flags: -XX:+UseG1GC, -XX:+UseParallelGC, -XX:+UseZGC, -XX:+UseSerialGC, -XX:+UseShenandoahGC.

Usually no — local variables die with the stack frame, and the JIT's scope analysis already knows when they're dead. It helps only for long-lived containers that hold references past their usefulness: the canonical example is elements[size] = null when popping from a hand-rolled stack, or evicting cache entries. Clean code beats null-ing.

Yes. Java uses reachability tracing from GC roots, not reference counting — a cycle of objects (A↔B) that no root can reach is an island of isolation and gets collected as a unit. (Reference-counting systems like old CPython needed special cycle detectors; the JVM doesn't.)

The empirical observation that most objects die young (temporaries, request-scoped data) while objects that survive a while tend to live long. Generational GCs exploit this: collect the young region frequently with cheap copying collection, touch the old region rarely — dramatically less work than scanning the whole heap every time.

Each object's header tracks how many minor GCs it has survived. When age reaches the tenuring threshold (-XX:MaxTenuringThreshold, default 15; adjusted dynamically), the object is promoted to the old generation. Objects too big for survivor spaces get promoted early; very large objects may skip young gen entirely.

Objects getting pushed to the old generation before they would naturally die — caused by undersized young gen/survivor spaces or allocation bursts. Bad because short-lived objects then die in the old gen, filling it with garbage that only an expensive major/Full GC can reclaim. Fix: bigger young gen, tuned survivor ratio, lower allocation rate.

From strongest to weakest: Strong (normal references — never collected while reachable), Soft (collected only under memory pressure), Weak (collected at the next GC once only weakly reachable), Phantom (enqueued after finalization; for cleanup actions — get() always returns null).

Yes — GC runs on JVM-internal daemon threads, so it never prevents JVM shutdown. When the last non-daemon (user) thread finishes, the JVM exits regardless of GC state — which is also why finalizers/cleaners may never run.

A snapshot of every object in the heap with references and sizes (.hprof file). Take one when investigating memory leaks or OOMs: jmap -dump:live,format=b,file=heap.hprof <pid>, jcmd <pid> GC.heap_dump, or automatically with -XX:+HeapDumpOnOutOfMemoryError. Analyze with Eclipse MAT or VisualVM.

  • jcmd — Swiss army knife (heap dump, GC.run, VM.flags, thread dump).
  • jstat -gcutil <pid> 1s — live GC region utilization and counts.
  • jmap — heap dump, histogram (-histo).
  • jconsole / VisualVM — GUI monitoring.
  • JDK Flight Recorder + Mission Control — low-overhead production profiling.

The amount of heap allocated per second (MB/s). High allocation rate → Eden fills fast → frequent minor GCs → more promotion pressure and CPU spent on GC. Reducing allocations (object reuse, primitives over wrappers, avoiding accidental garbage in hot loops) is often more effective than GC tuning.

  • Mark-sweep — mark live objects from roots, sweep dead ones; fast but leaves fragmentation.
  • Mark-compact — mark, then slide live objects together; no fragmentation but more expensive moves.
  • Copying — copy live objects to a fresh space, abandon the old space wholesale; cost ∝ live data only — ideal for young gen where most objects are dead.
CollectorThreadsGoalUse case
Serial1Smallest footprintTiny heaps, single CPU, containers with 1 core
ParallelMany (STW)ThroughputBatch jobs where pauses don't matter
G1Concurrent + parallelBalanced, target pause timeDefault; most services
ZGCAlmost fully concurrentSub-ms pauses, huge heaps (TB)Latency-critical, large-heap services

Concurrent Mark Sweep was the original low-pause collector (concurrent marking, no compaction). Its weaknesses — heap fragmentation, concurrent-mode failures falling back to long Full GCs, and high maintenance cost — led to deprecation in Java 9 and removal in Java 14. G1 is its designated successor; ZGC/Shenandoah for stricter latency.

G1 divides the heap into ~2048 equal regions (1–32MB each); any region can be Eden, Survivor, Old, or Humongous at a given time. It concurrently marks liveness, then collects the regions with the most garbage first (hence the name) in incremental, size-bounded evacuation pauses, aiming at the target set by -XX:MaxGCPauseMillis (default 200ms). Young collections are fully STW but small; "mixed" collections add selected old regions.

Objects larger than half a region size — allocated directly into contiguous "humongous" regions of the old generation, bypassing Eden. Many humongous allocations (huge byte[]s, giant collections) cause fragmentation and can trigger Full GCs. Mitigations: increase region size (-XX:G1HeapRegionSize), or chunk the data into smaller pieces.

After a concurrent marking cycle completes, G1 runs collections that evacuate all young regions plus a few of the garbage-richest old regions per pause — spreading old-gen cleanup across several short pauses instead of one big major GC. Controlled by flags like -XX:G1MixedGCCountTarget and -XX:G1HeapWastePercent.

ZGC does marking, relocation, and reference fixing concurrently with the application using colored pointers (metadata bits inside 64-bit pointers) and load barriers (tiny checks on every reference load that can fix up a stale pointer on the fly). Only trivial root-scanning work is STW. Generational ZGC (Java 21) adds young/old separation for better throughput.

Shenandoah (Red Hat) is another concurrent low-pause collector with goals similar to ZGC — pauses independent of heap size. Implementation differs: historically Brooks forwarding pointers, now load barriers like ZGC. Practically: both target latency-sensitive workloads; ZGC ships with Oracle/OpenJDK mainline, Shenandoah with Red Hat builds and OpenJDK. Choice usually comes down to JDK distribution and benchmarking.

Epsilon (-XX:+UseEpsilonGC, Java 11) allocates but never reclaims — when the heap fills, the JVM dies. Uses: performance testing without GC noise, measuring an app's true allocation footprint, ultra-short-lived jobs, and latency experiments where you size the heap to outlive the process.

The eternal GC triangle — optimize two, sacrifice one. Throughput (max work done; pauses OK) → Parallel GC. Latency (consistent low pauses) → ZGC/Shenandoah, G1 in between. Footprint (minimal memory/CPU) → Serial. Default answer for services: start with G1, set a pause goal, measure with GC logs, escalate to ZGC only if p99 latency demands it.

Java 9+ unified logging: -Xlog:gc*:file=gc.log:time,uptime,level,tags:filecount=5,filesize=20M. Key things to read: pause type (Young/Mixed/Full), pause duration, heap before→after, promotion volume, and frequency. Tools like GCeasy or GCViewer turn logs into throughput/pause percentile reports. Java 8 legacy: -XX:+PrintGCDetails -XX:+PrintGCDateStamps.

  • -Xms / -Xmx — heap min/max; -Xss — thread stack size.
  • -XX:MaxMetaspaceSize — cap class metadata.
  • -XX:MaxGCPauseMillis — G1 pause goal.
  • -XX:NewRatio / -Xmn / -XX:SurvivorRatio — young gen sizing (mostly pre-G1).
  • -XX:+HeapDumpOnOutOfMemoryError -XX:HeapDumpPath=... — always on in prod.
  • -XX:MaxRAMPercentage — heap as % of container memory.

To collect the young gen without scanning the old gen, the JVM must know which old objects point into young space. A card table divides the heap into 512-byte "cards"; a write barrier marks a card dirty whenever a reference is stored — minor GC scans only dirty cards. G1 generalizes this with per-region remembered sets tracking which outside regions reference into each region.

A tiny piece of code the JIT injects around reference field writes so the GC can track cross-generation/cross-region pointers (card marking) or maintain marking invariants during concurrent GC (e.g. SATB — snapshot-at-the-beginning — in G1). Cost is a few instructions per reference store; concurrent collectors like ZGC additionally use load barriers on reads.

Each thread gets a private chunk of Eden, so allocation is a lock-free pointer bump (~10 instructions) instead of synchronizing on a shared heap pointer. When a TLAB fills, the thread grabs a new one. This is why object allocation in Java is extremely cheap — often cheaper than malloc.

JIT analysis determining whether an object can escape the method/thread that created it. If it never escapes, the JIT can apply: scalar replacement (explode the object into local variables — no allocation at all), stack allocation semantics, and lock elision (remove synchronization on a thread-confined object). Why micro-benchmarks claiming "allocation costs X" are often wrong.

Memory-sensitive caches: a SoftReference<Bitmap> keeps data while memory is plentiful and lets GC reclaim it under pressure. Catches: clearing order/timing is JVM-dependent (-XX:SoftRefLRUPolicyMSPerMB), they can delay OOM diagnosis, and modern advice prefers bounded caches with explicit eviction (Caffeine) over soft-ref caches for predictability.

Create a reference with a queue: new WeakReference<>(obj, queue). When GC clears the referent, the reference object itself is enqueued; a cleanup thread polls the queue to release associated resources (remove map entries, close handles). This is the machinery beneath WeakHashMap, ThreadLocal's stale-entry cleanup, and Cleaner.

The Java 9+ replacement for finalizers: register an object with a cleanup Runnable that must not reference the object (use a static nested class holding only the native resource handle). When the object becomes phantom-reachable, the Cleaner's daemon thread runs the action. Still non-deterministic — it's a safety net behind AutoCloseable, not a substitute.

private static final Cleaner CLEANER = Cleaner.create();
private final Cleaner.Cleanable cleanable =
    CLEANER.register(this, new ReleaseNativeMemory(address));

On 64-bit JVMs, object pointers ("oops") are stored as 32-bit offsets scaled by 8 (object alignment), halving reference memory cost. Works for heaps up to ~32GB; beyond that the JVM falls back to full 64-bit pointers — which is why a 33GB heap can hold less data than a 31GB one. Enabled by default (-XX:+UseCompressedOops).

Header (12 bytes with compressed oops: 8-byte mark word — identity hash, GC age, lock state — plus 4-byte class pointer) + instance fields (reordered by JVM to minimize padding) + padding to 8-byte alignment. So new Object() ≈ 16 bytes; an empty String far more than its characters suggest. Inspect with the JOL (Java Object Layout) library.

int = 4 bytes inline. Integer = 16 bytes (header + value + padding) plus a 4–8 byte reference to reach it, plus GC pressure. An ArrayList<Integer> of a million values costs ~5x an int[] and scatters data across the heap (cache misses). Hence: primitive arrays/streams in hot paths; Project Valhalla's value types aim to fix this permanently.

-XX:+UseStringDeduplication (G1 only): during GC, strings with identical char content have their internal byte[] arrays shared (the String objects stay distinct — unlike intern()). Typical enterprise heaps are 10–25% duplicate strings, so this is a cheap win for string-heavy apps. Verify benefit with -Xlog:stringdedup*=debug.

  1. Confirm the OOM type (heap? metaspace? native threads?) from logs.
  2. Get the heap dump (-XX:+HeapDumpOnOutOfMemoryError should already be set).
  3. Open in Eclipse MAT → dominator tree + "Leak Suspects" report → find what retains the memory.
  4. Check GC logs: gradual climb (leak) vs sudden spike (huge allocation/burst)?
  5. Trace the retaining reference chain to a GC root → that's your code location.
  6. Fix, then verify with load tests + jstat/JFR trending.
  • Static collections/caches growing without eviction.
  • ThreadLocal values never removed in thread pools.
  • Listeners/callbacks registered but never unregistered.
  • Inner/anonymous classes pinning their outer instance.
  • Unclosed resources (streams, connections, native handles).
  • Classloader leaks on redeploys (a single static ref keeps the whole app's classes).
  • Mutated HashMap keys — unreachable but retained entries.

Pool threads never die, so their ThreadLocalMaps live forever. The map's key (the ThreadLocal) is weakly referenced, but the value is strongly referenced — once the ThreadLocal object is gone you have unreachable-but-retained values ("stale entries") that only get cleaned opportunistically. Worse, values can leak between requests (security context bleeding). Rule: always threadLocal.remove() in a finally block per request.

If any object loaded by an app's classloader stays reachable after undeploy (a registered JDBC driver, a running thread, a ThreadLocal value, a JDK-level cache holding your class), the classloader can't be collected — and a classloader pins every class it loaded plus all their statics. Result: Metaspace/heap grows each redeploy until OOM. This is why Tomcat has leak-detection warnings and why apps must deregister drivers and stop threads on shutdown.

ByteBuffer.allocateDirect(n) allocates outside the heap in native memory — I/O can use it zero-copy (no heap↔native copying), and it doesn't add GC pressure. Costs: slower to allocate, invisible to heap monitoring, capped by -XX:MaxDirectMemorySize, and freed only when the owning buffer object is GC'd — a classic source of "memory leak but heap looks fine." Netty, Kafka clients use it heavily.

Non-heap consumers: Metaspace (class/proxy generation), direct buffers, thread stacks (thousands of threads × 1MB), JIT code cache, native libraries (zlib, netty epoll), and glibc malloc arena fragmentation. Tooling: -XX:NativeMemoryTracking=summary + jcmd <pid> VM.native_memory summary, pmap, and for malloc issues, switching to jemalloc to profile.

Modern JVMs (8u191+, 10+) are container-aware: they read cgroup limits for CPU and memory. Default heap = 25% of container memory — usually too small; set -XX:MaxRAMPercentage=75 (leave headroom for metaspace, stacks, direct memory, or the kernel OOM-killer kills the container with exit code 137). Also note CPU quota affects GC thread counts and availableProcessors().

A point in execution where a thread's state is fully known to the JVM (references mapped, stack walkable). For STW operations (GC, deoptimization, thread dumps, biased-lock revocation) all threads must reach a safepoint and park. JIT code polls a flag at method returns and loop back-edges. A long-running counted loop with no safepoint poll can delay everyone — visible as high "time-to-safepoint" in -Xlog:safepoint.

A minor GC must move surviving objects, but the destination (survivor/old gen, or G1 free regions) has no room — "to-space exhausted" in G1 logs. The JVM falls back to an expensive single-threaded Full GC. Causes: old gen too full, fragmentation, allocation bursts. Fixes: larger heap, more G1 reserve (-XX:G1ReservePercent), earlier concurrent cycles (-XX:InitiatingHeapOccupancyPercent lower).

  • To-space exhaustion — concurrent cycle started too late → lower IHOP, raise G1ReservePercent.
  • Humongous allocation pressure → bigger regions or smaller objects.
  • Metaspace expansion → raise MaxMetaspaceSize / fix class leaks.
  • System.gc() from libraries → -XX:+ExplicitGCInvokesConcurrent or disable.
  • Plain undersized heap → more memory.

A healthy G1 service should see zero Full GCs.

Columns are % utilization: S0 S1 E O M (survivors, Eden, Old, Metaspace) and counters: YGC/YGCT (young GC count/time), FGC/FGCT (full GC), GCT (total). Diagnosis patterns: O climbing steadily and never dropping → leak or undersized; FGC incrementing → trouble; E cycling fast → high allocation rate.

Native memory where JIT-compiled machine code lives (-XX:ReservedCodeCacheSize, default ~240MB). If it fills, the JVM stops JIT compiling ("CodeCache is full. Compiler has been disabled") and performance silently collapses to interpreter speed for new methods. Causes: huge codebases, excessive proxy/lambda generation. Monitor via JFR or jcmd Compiler.codecache.

A class is unloaded only when its classloader becomes unreachable — no instances of any of its classes, no Class references, no loader reference. Classes from the bootstrap/app loader effectively never unload. Matters for: app servers (redeploys), frameworks generating classes dynamically (lambdas use hidden classes precisely so they can unload individually).

Histograms show what, not who. Take a full heap dump and use MAT's dominator tree — it attributes retained size to the objects that keep others alive. Group by class, follow "path to GC roots" (exclude weak/soft refs) on the biggest retainers. byte[]/char[] are nearly always leaves owned by Strings owned by collections owned by… your leak.

An always-available, in-JVM event recorder (allocations, GC, locks, I/O, exceptions, method samples) with ~1% overhead — designed to run continuously in production. Start with -XX:StartFlightRecording=duration=120s,filename=rec.jfr or jcmd <pid> JFR.start; analyze in JDK Mission Control. Free/open-source since JDK 11. The first tool to reach for on "the service is slow and we don't know why."

Each platform thread reserves stack space (default ~1MB on 64-bit Linux) in native memory, outside -Xmx. 5,000 threads ≈ 5GB of stack reservations — the real reason "thread per request" doesn't scale and unable to create new native thread appears. -Xss512k shrinks per-thread cost (risking StackOverflowError on deep stacks); virtual threads (Java 21) sidestep the whole problem with pay-as-you-go heap-based stacks.

The JVM's self-tuning: based on detected CPUs/memory it picks the collector, heap sizes, GC thread counts, and then adaptively resizes generations/survivors at runtime to meet goals (pause target, throughput target, footprint). Practical takeaway: start with minimal flags (heap size + collector + pause goal) and let ergonomics work; over-specifying flags often fights the adaptive logic.

Healthy — usage climbs as objects are promoted, then drops sharply when an old-gen collection reclaims them. A leak shows a saw-tooth whose valleys keep rising: each collection reclaims less, the baseline ratchets upward until OOM. Always judge the post-GC floor, not the peaks.

A finalizer storing this into a reachable field — the "dead" object becomes reachable again and GC must keep it. The object's finalizer never runs again, object lifecycle becomes incoherent, and security exploits have abused it (resurrect a partially-constructed object). One of the core reasons finalize() was deprecated; Cleaner's design (cleanup action must not reference the object) makes resurrection impossible.

Any VM operation needs a global safepoint: deoptimization, code cache flushes, thread dumps (jstack), biased lock revocation (pre-15), JVMTI/debugger attach, heap inspection (jmap -histo:live actually triggers GC + safepoint). Plus time-to-safepoint delays where one thread in a long counted loop keeps everyone waiting. Diagnose with -Xlog:safepoint — pause time = safepoint sync + operation.

Used heap — live + not-yet-collected objects (what jstat shows). Committed heap — memory the JVM has actually claimed from the OS for the heap (≥ used, ≤ max). RSS — total physical memory of the process: committed heap + metaspace + stacks + code cache + direct buffers + native libs. Container limits apply to RSS — sizing only by -Xmx is the classic OOMKilled mistake.

Historically the JVM rarely shrank. Now: G1 (since JDK 12, JEP 346) uncommits idle heap during concurrent periodic cycles; ZGC has -XX:+ZUncommit; Shenandoah uncommits aggressively. Relevant for cloud cost (pay for what you hold) and for sharing nodes. Requires -Xms < -Xmx — equal sizing deliberately disables shrinking.

Objects are conceptually white (unvisited/garbage), grey (marked, children pending), black (marked, children done). Marking pushes grey→black until no grey remains; whites are garbage. Concurrent hazard: the application stores a reference to a white object into a black object and erases the old path — the live object would be missed (the "lost object problem"). Write barriers prevent it.

Two fixes for the lost-object problem. SATB (G1): logically snapshot the object graph at marking start — the write barrier records the old value of overwritten references, so anything live at the snapshot stays marked; floating garbage is the trade-off. Incremental update (CMS): barrier records the new reference written into scanned objects, requiring a re-mark of dirtied cards at the end — shorter-lived garbage, but a longer final remark pause.

Objects that die during a concurrent marking cycle but were already marked live (or protected by the SATB snapshot) — they survive this cycle and are reclaimed only in the next one. It's the inherent cost of concurrent GC: the collector works against a slightly stale view. Practical effect: heap occupancy needs headroom beyond the true live set.

ZGC steals ~4 metadata bits inside each 64-bit reference (marked0/marked1/remapped/finalizable). Every reference load passes a barrier that checks the color against the current GC phase; a "bad" color triggers self-healing: the barrier looks up the forwarding table, fixes the reference in place, and returns the new address. Result: objects can be relocated while the app runs — threads never wait for relocation to finish, hence pauses stay sub-millisecond regardless of heap size.

Original ZGC was single-generation — every cycle traced the whole heap, so high allocation rates forced frequent full traces and big CPU cost ("allocation stalls"). Generational ZGC adds young/old separation: cheap frequent young collections absorb the die-young majority, full traces become rare. Outcome: same sub-ms pauses with ~4x lower allocation-stall risk and notably less CPU — made the default ZGC mode in JDK 23.

The 64-bit mark word is multiplexed: it stores GC age + identity hash in the neutral state, or a pointer to a lock record/monitor when locked. Consequences: calling System.identityHashCode() on an object forces the hash to be materialized, which historically disabled biased locking for it; when an object is locked, its hash/age live in the displaced header. This packing is why biased locking was removed (JDK 15) — the complexity outweighed gains.

Initiating Heap Occupancy Percent — the old-gen occupancy at which G1 starts a concurrent marking cycle (-XX:InitiatingHeapOccupancyPercent, default 45). Too high → marking finishes late → to-space exhaustion → Full GC; too low → wasted CPU on premature cycles. Modern G1 adjusts IHOP adaptively from measured promotion rate and marking duration; you can pin it (-XX:-G1UseAdaptiveIHOP) for predictability.

  1. Check what dominates the pause in -Xlog:gc+phases: object copy time → too many regions per pause.
  2. Lower -XX:MaxGCPauseMillis (G1 then picks fewer old regions per mixed GC) and raise -XX:G1MixedGCCountTarget to spread work.
  3. Reduce promotion: bigger young gen, fix allocation bursts, check humongous allocations.
  4. If reference processing is the culprit → -XX:+ParallelRefProcEnabled.
  5. Still failing the SLO → evaluate ZGC and re-measure throughput cost.

Shallow size — the object's own bytes (header + fields). Retained size — everything that would become garbage if this object were collected, i.e. all memory it exclusively dominates. Leak hunting uses retained size: a 40-byte HashMap with 2GB retained is your problem. MAT's dominator tree is precisely this computation.

Object A dominates B if every path from GC roots to B goes through A — so collecting A frees B. The dominator tree arranges the heap by this relation, rolling up retained sizes. It answers "who is responsible for this memory" in one view, cutting through the rats' nest of shared references that makes raw reference graphs unreadable.

The kernel kills on total RSS, not heap. Audit the full memory budget: heap (committed, not used!) + Metaspace + thread stacks (count threads!) + code cache + direct/native buffers + native libs + malloc fragmentation. Actions: enable NativeMemoryTracking and compare to the cgroup limit; cap direct memory; reduce thread count or -Xss; set MaxRAMPercentage≈70 so the JVM leaves room. Remember committed heap can be far above used.

Workloads with mid-lived objects: LRU caches, session stores, batching/buffering pipelines, message accumulation — objects survive long enough to be promoted, then die in old gen. Effect: constant promotion, old gen churn, frequent mixed/major GCs ("nepotism" effects too). Mitigations: larger young gen so they die before promotion, higher tenuring threshold, off-heap caches, or low-pause collectors that don't care (ZGC).

  • Profile first — JFR's TLAB allocation events show exactly which call sites allocate.
  • Primitives/primitive arrays over wrappers; IntStream over Stream<Integer>.
  • Avoid hidden garbage: string concat in loops, iterator allocation, autoboxing, varargs arrays, captured-lambda allocation.
  • Reuse buffers (ThreadLocal byte[]), pre-size collections, consider object pooling only for expensive objects.
  • Let escape analysis help: keep objects method-local and small.

Native code can create NewGlobalRef handles that act as GC roots until explicitly deleted with DeleteGlobalRef. Forgetting deletion pins Java objects forever — invisible to normal heap analysis except as "GC root: JNI global" paths in MAT. Common with sloppy native libraries; diagnose with -Xcheck:jni and JFR JNI events.

STW operations wait for all threads to reach safepoints, and JIT-compiled counted loops (int index) may omit safepoint polls for speed. One thread crunching a billion-iteration loop delays the safepoint for everyone — GC can't start, jstack hangs. Visible as huge "time-to-safepoint" in safepoint logs. Fixes: loop strip-mining does this automatically in modern JDKs; -XX:+UseCountedLoopSafepoints; or restructure with long/early exits.

  1. jstat -gcutil trend → confirm the old-gen floor rises after collections.
  2. jmap -histo <pid> twice, 30 min apart → diff instance counts (cheap, brief pause).
  3. Start a JFR recording → OldObjectSample events point at allocation sites of long-lived objects.
  4. If allowed, jcmd GC.heap_dump during low traffic → MAT dominator tree.
  5. Correlate with deploys/feature flags; fix and verify the new floor is flat.

Free memory exists but in scattered small holes, so a large allocation fails — OOM with free space. Non-compacting collectors suffer: CMS famously fragmented old gen (its downfall). Compacting/copying collectors (Parallel, G1 regions, ZGC relocation) defragment as a side effect, though G1 can still fragment at the region level with humongous objects.

Two flavors: (1) GetPrimitiveArrayCritical hands native code a direct pointer into the heap — collectors must either pin that object (region pinning, JEP 423 in G1) or block GC, historically causing latency spikes. (2) Virtual threads pinned to carriers by synchronized/native frames — different "pinning" but a common interview confusion; it blocks unmounting, not GC.

The string table is a fixed-bucket native hashtable (default 65536 buckets). Interning millions of unique strings (e.g. interning every CSV cell) degrades it to long chains — O(n) lookups inside GC (the table is cleaned during GC) and slow interning. Symptoms: rising GC "string table" times. Fix: don't intern dynamic data; use a bounded map if dedup is needed; for pure memory savings prefer G1 string deduplication.

JFR samples a tiny fraction of allocations and tracks those objects; ones still alive at recording dump are emitted with their allocation stack trace, time, and reference chain. Because it samples (not instruments) the overhead stays ~1%, yet a leak's allocation site appears with high probability over time — often enough to skip the heap-dump step entirely. Enable with path-to-gc-roots=true for chains.

Memory barriers/fences — CPU instructions ordering loads/stores for visibility between threads (the JMM machinery behind volatile, synchronized). GC barriers (read/write barriers) — compiler-injected snippets around reference accesses that maintain GC bookkeeping (card marking, SATB logging, ZGC load checks). Same word, unrelated mechanisms — one is about concurrency correctness, the other about collector correctness.

G1's region count and mixed-pause copying struggle at this scale, so: ZGC (generational) — pauses stay sub-ms independent of heap. Plan: compressed oops are gone above ~32GB (accept ~30% reference bloat — or shard into smaller JVMs); reserve CPU headroom (concurrent GC steals cores); set -Xmx=-Xms, enable NUMA interleaving (-XX:+UseNUMA); watch allocation stalls in -Xlog:gc; load test for throughput regression vs G1 and validate p99.9, not averages.

With throughput collectors, a bigger heap means GC runs less often but longer — each pause scans/copies more live data, hurting p99 latency. It can also push the heap past compressed-oops (32GB cliff), increase page-cache pressure, and merely delay a leak's OOM, making diagnosis slower. Right-size to live-set × 2–4 with the collector matched to the latency goal, rather than maximizing.

A dead old-gen object still sits in a remembered set/card pointing at young objects — minor GC, which doesn't know the old object is dead, treats the reference as live and keeps (even promotes) the young garbage. The promoted garbage can in turn keep more young objects alive next cycle — dead objects "sponsoring" their relatives. Self-heals after the next old-gen collection; mitigated by clearing references in node-based structures (why LinkedList-style code nulls next/prev on unlink).

  • Bound it: size/weight-based eviction + TTL (Caffeine) instead of unbounded maps.
  • Reduce object count: store serialized byte[] or primitive structures instead of deep object graphs (millions of small objects = marking cost).
  • Go off-heap for very large caches (Chronicle Map, Ehcache off-heap) — invisible to GC.
  • Or externalize to Redis/Memcached — heap stays small, restarts stop being painful.
  • If it must stay on-heap: ZGC/Shenandoah + generational tuning, and measure the live-set honestly.

🌊 Java 8 — Top 100

ℹ️ Lambdas, Streams, Optional, CompletableFuture, Date/Time API. Q1–30 basics, Q31–75 applied, Q76–100 conceptual depth.
  • Lambda expressions and functional interfaces
  • Stream API for declarative data processing
  • Optional for null-safety
  • Default and static methods in interfaces
  • Method references (String::length)
  • New Date/Time API (java.time)
  • CompletableFuture, Metaspace (PermGen removed), Nashorn JS engine, Base64 API, parallel array sort.

An anonymous function — parameters + body — that can be passed around as a value: (a, b) -> a + b. It replaces verbose anonymous inner classes for behavior parameterization (callbacks, comparators, runnables), enabling functional-style APIs like Streams.

// Before
Collections.sort(list, new Comparator<String>() {
    public int compare(String a, String b) { return a.compareTo(b); }
});
// After
list.sort((a, b) -> a.compareTo(b));   // or String::compareTo

An interface with exactly one abstract method (SAM — single abstract method). It can have any number of default/static methods. Lambdas can only be assigned to functional interface types. Examples: Runnable, Comparator, Callable, Function.

Not mandatory — any SAM interface works with lambdas. The annotation makes the compiler enforce the single-abstract-method rule, so adding a second abstract method later fails compilation instead of silently breaking every lambda user. Good practice on every intentional functional interface.

InterfaceMethodSignature
Predicate<T>testT → boolean
Function<T,R>applyT → R
Consumer<T>acceptT → void
Supplier<T>get() → T
UnaryOperator<T>applyT → T
BiFunction<T,U,R>apply(T,U) → R
BinaryOperator<T>apply(T,T) → T
  • Static: Integer::parseInt ≡ s -> Integer.parseInt(s)
  • Bound instance: System.out::println ≡ x -> System.out.println(x)
  • Unbound instance: String::length ≡ s -> s.length() (receiver is the first param)
  • Constructor: ArrayList::new ≡ () -> new ArrayList()

A pipeline for declarative processing of data: source → intermediate ops (filter, map) → terminal op (collect, reduce). Differences from a collection: streams store nothing, are lazily evaluated, can be consumed only once, can be infinite, and use internal iteration (the library drives the loop).

Intermediate — return a Stream, lazy, chainable: filter, map, flatMap, sorted, distinct, limit, skip, peek. Terminal — trigger execution, produce a result/side-effect, consume the stream: collect, forEach, reduce, count, min, max, anyMatch, findFirst, toArray. Without a terminal op, nothing runs at all.

List<String> names = employees.stream()
    .filter(e -> e.getSalary() > 50000)
    .map(Employee::getName)
    .sorted()
    .collect(Collectors.toList());

A container that may or may not hold a non-null value — making "no result" explicit in the type system instead of returning null and hoping callers check. It pushes NPE prevention to compile-time API design: a method returning Optional<User> forces the caller to handle absence.

Optional.of(value) — throws NPE if value is null (use when null is a bug). Optional.ofNullable(value) — empty if null (use for possibly-null sources). Optional.empty() — explicitly no value.

orElse(createDefault()) evaluates the default always, even when the Optional has a value. orElseGet(() -> createDefault()) calls the supplier only when empty. For constants either is fine; for anything expensive (DB call, object creation) use orElseGet. Classic interview trap with observable side effects.

Methods with a body in an interface (default void forEach(...)). Added so the JDK could evolve existing interfaces without breaking implementations — e.g. adding forEach, stream(), sort() to Collection/List without breaking every custom implementation on the planet.

Yes (Java 8+). They are called on the interface itself — Comparator.comparing(...), Stream.of(...) — and are NOT inherited by implementing classes or sub-interfaces. Typical use: factory/utility methods that belong with the type (killing the need for companion classes like Collections).

Old Date/Calendar problems: mutable (not thread-safe), months 0-indexed, years offset from 1900, Date is actually a timestamp with no timezone clarity, SimpleDateFormat not thread-safe (classic production bug when made static). java.time (JSR-310, inspired by Joda-Time) is immutable, fluent, clearly typed, and thread-safe.

  • LocalDate — date only (2026-06-13), no time/zone.
  • LocalTime — time only (14:30:00).
  • LocalDateTime — date + time, no zone (ambiguous instant!).
  • ZonedDateTime — date + time + timezone rules (DST aware).
  • Instant — machine timestamp on the UTC line (epoch nanos); use for storage/logs.

Period — date-based amount: years/months/days (Period.between(date1, date2)); calendar-aware ("1 month" varies). Duration — time-based amount: seconds/nanos (Duration.ofMinutes(90)); exact machine time. Use Period with LocalDate, Duration with Instant/LocalTime.

DateTimeFormatter fmt = DateTimeFormatter.ofPattern("dd-MM-yyyy HH:mm");
String s = LocalDateTime.now().format(fmt);        // "13-06-2026 10:30"
LocalDateTime dt = LocalDateTime.parse("13-06-2026 10:30", fmt);

Unlike SimpleDateFormat, DateTimeFormatter is immutable and thread-safe — safe to keep as a static constant.

Defined as a default method on Iterable (and on Stream and Map): list.forEach(System.out::println), map.forEach((k, v) -> ...). Note: on a parallel stream, forEach order is non-deterministic — use forEachOrdered if order matters.

A local variable never reassigned after initialization — final without the keyword. Lambdas capture the variable's value (the stack frame may be gone when the lambda runs later/in another thread); allowing mutation would create the illusion of shared state without the reality. Workarounds for counters: arrays of size 1, AtomicInteger, or proper stream reduction.

filter(Predicate) selects elements (same type, fewer items); map(Function) transforms each element (possibly new type, same count).

List.of(1,2,3,4).stream().filter(n -> n % 2 == 0)  // [2, 4]
List.of(1,2,3,4).stream().map(n -> n * n)           // [1, 4, 9, 16]

A Collector is a recipe for mutable reduction — supplier (create container) + accumulator (add element) + combiner (merge partials, for parallel) + finisher. toList() accumulates into an ArrayList. Other staples: toSet, toMap, joining, groupingBy, partitioningBy, counting, averagingInt.

Map<Integer, String> byId = users.stream()
    .collect(Collectors.toMap(User::getId, User::getName));

If two elements produce the same key → IllegalStateException. Provide a merge function: toMap(User::getId, User::getName, (a, b) -> a).

Both are short-circuiting and return Optional. findFirst() respects encounter order — deterministic. findAny() may return any matching element — faster in parallel streams because threads don't coordinate on order. Sequentially they behave the same in practice.

Classic trick question: anyMatch → false, allMatch → true (vacuous truth — no element violates), noneMatch → true. All three short-circuit on the first decisive element for non-empty streams.

// 1. reduce(BinaryOperator) → Optional<T>
Optional<Integer> sum = nums.stream().reduce(Integer::sum);

// 2. reduce(identity, op) → T  (safe for empty streams)
int sum2 = nums.stream().reduce(0, Integer::sum);

// 3. reduce(identity, accumulator, combiner) — parallel-friendly,
//    accumulator and combiner may differ in type
int totalLen = words.parallelStream()
    .reduce(0, (acc, w) -> acc + w.length(), Integer::sum);
Optional<Employee> top = employees.stream()
    .max(Comparator.comparing(Employee::getSalary));

int maxNum = nums.stream().mapToInt(Integer::intValue).max().orElse(0);
employees.sort(
    Comparator.comparing(Employee::getDept)
              .thenComparing(Employee::getSalary, Comparator.reverseOrder())
              .thenComparing(Employee::getName));

Also useful: Comparator.nullsFirst(...), reversed(), comparingInt (avoids boxing).

distinct() removes duplicates via equals/hashCode (stateful — remembers seen elements). limit(n) truncates (short-circuiting — essential for infinite streams). skip(n) drops the first n (stateful). skip + limit = pagination.

list.stream();  list.parallelStream();
Stream.of("a", "b");  Arrays.stream(arr);
IntStream.rangeClosed(1, 10);
Stream.iterate(0, n -> n + 2);   // infinite
Stream.generate(Math::random);   // infinite
Files.lines(path);  "abc".chars();

map is 1→1; if the mapper returns a Stream/collection you get a stream of streams. flatMap is 1→many: it flattens each produced stream into one.

List<List<Integer>> nested = List.of(List.of(1,2), List.of(3,4));
nested.stream().flatMap(Collection::stream).toList();   // [1,2,3,4]

sentences.stream()
    .flatMap(s -> Arrays.stream(s.split(" ")))           // all words
    .distinct().toList();
// Dept → employees
Map<String, List<Employee>> byDept = employees.stream()
    .collect(Collectors.groupingBy(Employee::getDept));

// Dept → count
