Making an object visible to other threads such that they see it fully constructed. Safe mechanisms: assign to a volatile field or AtomicReference; guard with a lock (synchronized); static initializer; final field set in constructor (freeze semantics); hand off via concurrent collection or BlockingQueue; thread-start/executor-submit edges. Unsafe: plain field assignment read by another thread without any of the above.

If an object is not leaked during construction, any thread that obtains a reference to it sees its final fields correctly initialized — even without synchronization (the "freeze" at constructor end). This is the formal foundation of immutable-object thread safety: String can be shared race-free precisely because its fields are final.

  • tryLock() / tryLock(timeout) — avoid deadlock, fail fast.
  • lockInterruptibly() — cancellable waits.
  • Fairness option (FIFO) — prevent starvation (at a throughput cost).
  • Multiple Condition objects per lock (notEmpty/notFull separately).
  • Lock/unlock in different scopes (hand-over-hand locking).

Otherwise prefer synchronized: simpler, can't forget unlock, JVM-optimized. ReentrantLock requires the try/finally unlock ritual.

A thread holding a lock can acquire it again without deadlocking — a hold count increments/decrements. Both synchronized and ReentrantLock are reentrant: a synchronized method calling another synchronized method on the same object just works. Non-reentrant locks would self-deadlock on such nesting.

ReadWriteLock rw = new ReentrantReadWriteLock();
rw.readLock().lock();    // many readers concurrently
rw.writeLock().lock();   // exclusive — waits for all readers

Helps for read-mostly data with non-trivial critical sections (config registries, routing tables). Caveats: writer starvation vs reader throughput trade-offs, and for short sections plain synchronized or StampedLock's optimistic mode often beats it. Measure before choosing.

long stamp = sl.tryOptimisticRead();    // NO locking at all
double x = this.x, y = this.y;          // read state
if (!sl.validate(stamp)) {              // did a write intervene?
    stamp = sl.readLock();              // fall back to real read lock
    try { x = this.x; y = this.y; } finally { sl.unlockRead(stamp); }
}

Reads cost almost nothing when writes are rare. Cautions: not reentrant, no Conditions, and you must re-read state after failed validation.

CountDownLatchCyclicBarrier
ReusableNo — one shotYes — resets after release
Who waitsWaiters wait for events (countDown from anywhere)The parties themselves wait for each other
Action on release—Optional barrier Runnable runs once
UseWait for N startup eventsIterative phased computation
Semaphore permits = new Semaphore(10);   // max 10 concurrent calls

Response callDownstream(Request r) throws InterruptedException {
    if (!permits.tryAcquire(200, TimeUnit.MILLISECONDS))
        throw new TooManyRequestsException();   // bulkhead full
    try { return client.send(r); }
    finally { permits.release(); }
}

Counting permits for resource limiting — the core of bulkhead patterns and simple rate limiting. Fair mode prevents starvation.

Segments are gone. Reads are lock-free (volatile reads of the table). Writes: empty bin → CAS the new node in; occupied bin → synchronized on the first node of that bin only (per-bucket locking). Bins treeify like HashMap; resizing is cooperative (multiple threads help transfer). Plus atomic compound ops: computeIfAbsent, merge, compute — and size() is an estimate via CounterCells, not a locked count.

Ambiguity under concurrency: get(k) == null could mean "absent" or "mapped to null", and you can't resolve it with a containsKey check because the map may change between the two calls. HashMap tolerates this only because single-threaded callers can check atomically-enough. Doug Lea banned nulls to keep the API race-free.

Every mutation copies the entire backing array; reads and iteration touch an immutable snapshot — no locking on reads, iterators never throw CME (but don't see later updates, and don't support remove()). Perfect for read-heavy, write-rare data: listener lists, handler registries, config. Disastrous for write-heavy or large lists (O(n) copy per write).

Compare-And-Swap: atomically "if current value == expected, set new value" — one CPU instruction; the basis of all atomics (failed CAS → retry loop). ABA: value goes A→B→A; CAS sees A and succeeds, blind to the intermediate change — dangerous in lock-free structures reusing nodes. Fix: AtomicStampedReference (value + version stamp) so A₁ ≠ A₂; or avoid node reuse (GC mostly saves Java here).

Under heavy contention, AtomicLong threads fight over one CAS cell — retry storms. LongAdder splits the count across striped cells (threads hit different cells), summing on read. Use LongAdder for hot counters/metrics (write-heavy, occasional reads); AtomicLong when you need exact read-modify-write semantics like compareAndSet or frequent precise reads. LongAccumulator generalizes to max/min etc.

Each worker has its own deque: it pushes/pops subtasks at one end (LIFO — cache friendly); idle workers steal from the other end of busy workers' deques (FIFO — biggest chunks). Designed for divide-and-conquer: a ForkJoinTask forks subtasks and joins them, with the framework balancing load automatically. Parallel streams and CompletableFuture defaults run on ForkJoinPool.commonPool().

class SumTask extends RecursiveTask<Long> {
    final long[] arr; final int lo, hi;
    protected Long compute() {
        if (hi - lo <= 10_000) {           // threshold → compute directly
            long s = 0;
            for (int i = lo; i < hi; i++) s += arr[i];
            return s;
        }
        int mid = (lo + hi) >>> 1;
        SumTask left = new SumTask(arr, lo, mid);
        left.fork();                         // async
        long right = new SumTask(arr, mid, hi).compute();  // this thread
        return right + left.join();
    }
}

Pattern: fork one half, compute the other yourself (avoids wasting the current thread), join last.

invokeAll(tasks) — runs all, blocks until every task completes, returns all Futures (check each for exceptions). invokeAny(tasks) — returns the result of the first successful task and cancels the rest; throws if all fail. invokeAny is a natural "query N replicas, take fastest" primitive.

FixedRate: targets a constant period (every 10s by the clock); if a run overlaps its slot, the next fires immediately after — runs can bunch up (no concurrent runs though). FixedDelay: waits N seconds after the previous run finishes — natural pacing for variable-duration jobs. Critical: an uncaught exception silently cancels all future runs — always wrap the task body in try/catch.

submit() — captured into the Future; invisible until get() throws ExecutionException (the classic "swallowed exception" bug). execute() — propagates to the thread's UncaughtExceptionHandler (default prints to stderr). Defenses: always consume Futures, set a logging UncaughtExceptionHandler via ThreadFactory, or wrap task bodies in try/catch with metrics.

ThreadFactory tf = r -> {
    Thread t = new Thread(r);
    t.setName("payment-worker-" + COUNTER.incrementAndGet());
    t.setUncaughtExceptionHandler((th, ex) -> log.error("Task died on {}", th, ex));
    t.setDaemon(false);
    return t;
};

Named threads make thread dumps readable ("pool-3-thread-7" tells you nothing), the handler stops silent task deaths, and you control daemon status. Libraries: Guava's ThreadFactoryBuilder.

  • Take 3 dumps ~10s apart (jstack / jcmd Thread.print) — single dumps lie.
  • BLOCKED threads all waiting on one monitor + the owner doing slow work → lock contention hotspot.
  • "Found one Java-level deadlock" — jstack detects monitor cycles for you.
  • Hundreds of threads in socketRead0 → downstream stuck, no timeouts.
  • Pool threads all RUNNABLE in the same stack across dumps → hot loop or undersized pool.
  • WAITING on Future.get / latch forever → lost completion path.
ThreadMXBean mx = ManagementFactory.getThreadMXBean();
long[] ids = mx.findDeadlockedThreads();     // monitors + ownable syncs
if (ids != null)
    for (ThreadInfo ti : mx.getThreadInfo(ids, true, true))
        log.error("DEADLOCK: {}", ti);

Run periodically from a watchdog; pair with alerting. Note it finds cycles in lock graphs — it can't see logical deadlocks (waiting on a queue that nobody will fill).

Avoiding synchronization by ensuring data is touched by only one thread: stack confinement (local variables only — inherently safe), ThreadLocal confinement (per-thread instances), and architectural confinement (a single dedicated thread owns the state; others send messages — Swing EDT, Netty event loops, actor model). Often the cleanest concurrency strategy: no locks because there's no sharing.

Immutable objects are thread-safe by construction — no writes after publication means no races, no locks, free sharing and caching (JMM final-field guarantee makes even unsafe publication see them whole). Pattern: model state as immutable snapshots; "mutation" = build a new snapshot and CAS/volatile-swap the reference (copy-on-write). This is how CopyOnWriteArrayList, String, and functional designs scale reads infinitely.

Phaser — a reusable, dynamic barrier: parties can register/deregister at runtime, supports multi-phase computations (arriveAndAwaitAdvance per phase) — the flexible successor to CountDownLatch + CyclicBarrier. Exchanger — a two-thread rendezvous where each hands the other an object (V mine = exchanger.exchange(yours)) — niche but elegant for double-buffering pipelines (filler swaps full buffer for the drainer's empty one).

  • ArrayBlockingQueue — bounded, array, single lock; predictable.
  • LinkedBlockingQueue — optionally bounded, separate put/take locks → higher throughput.
  • SynchronousQueue — direct handoff, no storage.
  • PriorityBlockingQueue — unbounded, ordered by comparator.
  • DelayQueue — elements available only after their delay expires (schedulers, TTL caches).
  • LinkedTransferQueue — transfer() blocks until a consumer takes it; hybrid handoff/queue.

The low-level primitives beneath AQS/locks: park() blocks the current thread; unpark(t) grants t a permit (max one, can be given before park — no lost-wakeup race). No monitor required, no IllegalMonitorStateException, permits are per-thread not per-object. You'd use them only when building custom synchronizers; everything in j.u.c is built on them.

The framework underlying ReentrantLock, Semaphore, CountDownLatch, ThreadPoolExecutor's worker control: an atomic int state + a CLH FIFO queue of waiting threads. Subclasses define tryAcquire/tryRelease semantics over the state (lock: 0→1 with owner; semaphore: permits; latch: count); AQS handles queuing, parking, fairness, and interruption. Interview gold: "ReentrantLock is a thin wrapper over AQS where state = hold count."

  • Lock elision — escape analysis proves the object is thread-local → lock removed entirely.
  • Lock coarsening — adjacent lock/unlock on the same object merged into one.
  • Adaptive spinning — briefly spin before parking, betting the lock frees quickly.
  • Biased locking — uncontended re-acquisition by the same thread was nearly free; removed in JDK 15 (complexity > benefit on modern hardware).

Net: uncontended synchronized is a few nanoseconds — "synchronized is slow" is outdated folklore; contention is what's slow.

Blocking (park) costs two context switches (~µs) but frees the CPU; spinning burns CPU but reacts in nanoseconds. Spinning is legitimate when waits are reliably ultra-short and cores are dedicated (low-latency trading, ring buffers like Disruptor, JVM adaptive spin). In application code, spin loops (while(!flag){}) are bugs — at minimum the flag must be volatile, and Thread.onSpinWait() hints the CPU. Default to blocking primitives.

stop() kills a thread at an arbitrary point, releasing all its monitors instantly — other threads then see corrupted, half-updated state (broken invariants with locks "successfully" released). suspend() freezes a thread while it holds locks → instant deadlock risk; resume() may race and never arrive. Replacement: cooperative interruption + volatile shutdown flags. (stop/suspend now throw UnsupportedOperationException in recent JDKs.)

Child threads created by a parent copy its inheritable ThreadLocal values at construction. But pool threads are created once, long before your request — tasks submitted later inherit nothing (or stale values from pool startup). Real fixes: capture context at submit time and restore in the task (Spring's context-propagating decorators, Micrometer context-propagation), or ScopedValues with structured concurrency.

// Decorate tasks: capture at submit, restore inside worker
ExecutorService wrapped = ContextExecutors.wrap(pool, () -> {
    Map<String,String> mdc = MDC.getCopyOfContextMap();      // capture
    SecurityContext sec = SecurityContextHolder.getContext();
    return (Runnable task) -> () -> {
        MDC.setContextMap(mdc); SecurityContextHolder.setContext(sec); // restore
        try { task.run(); }
        finally { MDC.clear(); SecurityContextHolder.clearContext(); }
    };
});

Spring: DelegatingSecurityContextExecutor, TaskDecorator. The finally-cleanup prevents context bleed between unrelated tasks — the other half people forget.

  1. Prefer immutable (final fields, no setters) — done, safe.
  2. Else identify every invariant spanning multiple fields; guard each invariant with one lock (document which!).
  3. No escaping this from constructors; defensive-copy mutable inputs/outputs.
  4. Compound operations (check-then-act, read-modify-write) must be atomic — one synchronized method, or atomics' compose methods.
  5. Delegate where possible (ConcurrentHashMap beats hand-rolled locking).
  6. State the safety contract in Javadoc; test with jcstress for the brave.
// Thread 1        // Thread 2
x = 1;              y = 1;
r1 = y;             r2 = x;
// Can r1 == 0 AND r2 == 0?  YES — both loads may be reordered
// before the stores (store buffers). Make x,y volatile → forbidden.

Compilers and CPUs reorder freely as long as single-threaded semantics hold; only happens-before edges constrain cross-thread observation. This litmus test is the standard demonstration that intuition about "obvious" ordering is wrong.

Two threads write different variables that happen to sit on the same 64-byte CPU cache line — every write invalidates the other core's line, causing ping-ponging that can cost 10–100x. Invisible in code, visible in profilers as cache-miss storms. Fixes: @jdk.internal.vm.annotation.Contended (used by LongAdder cells, Thread), manual padding fields, or restructuring so hot counters aren't adjacent.

Future<?> f = pool.submit(() -> {
    while (!Thread.currentThread().isInterrupted()) {   // cooperative check
        chunk = readChunk();                             // blocking ops throw IE
        process(chunk);
    }
});
f.cancel(true);   // true → interrupt the running thread

cancel(false) only prevents a not-yet-started task. Cancellation works only if the task cooperates: poll the flag in loops, let InterruptedException propagate, and clean up in finally. CompletableFuture.cancel does NOT interrupt its thread — a famous gotcha.

All request threads pile up blocked on the slow dependency (no timeouts), exhausting the shared pool — even endpoints not using that dependency starve: a bulkhead failure. Fixes: aggressive connect/read timeouts; a dedicated bounded pool or semaphore per dependency (bulkhead isolation) so only its budget drowns; circuit breaker to fail fast once it's down; bounded queues with CallerRuns for backpressure. This story is the bridge question to microservices resilience.

class Bounded<T> {
    final Queue<T> q = new ArrayDeque<>();
    final int cap;
    final ReentrantLock lock = new ReentrantLock();
    final Condition notFull = lock.newCondition(), notEmpty = lock.newCondition();

    void put(T t) throws InterruptedException {
        lock.lock();
        try {
            while (q.size() == cap) notFull.await();
            q.add(t);
            notEmpty.signal();
        } finally { lock.unlock(); }
    }
    T take() throws InterruptedException {
        lock.lock();
        try {
            while (q.isEmpty()) notEmpty.await();
            T t = q.remove();
            notFull.signal();
            return t;
        } finally { lock.unlock(); }
    }
}

Two conditions wake exactly the right waiters — with wait/notify on one monitor you'd need notifyAll and waste wakeups.

class OddEven {
    private int n = 1; private final int max = 10;
    synchronized void print(boolean odd) throws InterruptedException {
        while (n <= max) {
            while (n <= max && (n % 2 == 1) != odd) wait();
            if (n > max) break;
            System.out.println(Thread.currentThread().getName() + ": " + n++);
            notifyAll();
        }
    }
}
// new Thread(() -> oe.print(true), "ODD").start();
// new Thread(() -> oe.print(false), "EVEN").start();

The pattern: shared monitor + condition-in-while + notifyAll. Variants: with Semaphores (cleaner) or Lock/Conditions.

class RoundRobin {
    private int turn = 0, n = 1;
    private final int threads = 3, max = 15;
    synchronized void print(int id) throws InterruptedException {
        while (n <= max) {
            while (turn != id && n <= max) wait();
            if (n > max) break;
            System.out.println("T" + (id + 1) + ": " + n++);
            turn = (turn + 1) % threads;
            notifyAll();
        }
    }
}

Generalizes odd-even to k threads with a turn variable. Semaphore-chain version: each thread releases the next thread's semaphore.

Naive code (everyone picks left fork then right) deadlocks when all hold one fork. Standard fixes: resource ordering — always pick the lower-numbered fork first (breaks circular wait); or limit seats to N-1 with a Semaphore; or tryLock both with backoff. Resource-ordering version:

Object first = forks[Math.min(left, right)];
Object second = forks[Math.max(left, right)];
synchronized (first) { synchronized (second) { eat(); } }
class LockFreeStack<T> {
    private final AtomicReference<Node<T>> top = new AtomicReference<>();
    void push(T v) {
        Node<T> n = new Node<>(v);
        do { n.next = top.get(); }
        while (!top.compareAndSet(n.next, n));     // retry on contention
    }
    T pop() {
        Node<T> cur;
        do {
            cur = top.get();
            if (cur == null) return null;
        } while (!top.compareAndSet(cur, cur.next));
        return cur.value;
    }
}

The CAS retry loop is the lock-free idiom: read, compute, CAS, retry on failure. GC sidesteps most ABA issues here.

class TokenBucket {
    private final long capacity, refillPerSec;
    private double tokens; private long last = System.nanoTime();
    synchronized boolean tryAcquire() {
        long now = System.nanoTime();
        tokens = Math.min(capacity, tokens + (now - last) / 1e9 * refillPerSec);
        last = now;
        if (tokens >= 1) { tokens--; return true; }
        return false;
    }
}

Lazy refill on each call — no scheduler thread. Bursts up to capacity, sustained rate = refillPerSec. Production: Resilience4j RateLimiter or Bucket4j (adds fairness, distributed variants).

The mapping function runs while holding the bin lock. If it (a) takes long, it blocks all writers to that bin; (b) recursively touches the same map (computeIfAbsent inside computeIfAbsent on a colliding key) — IllegalStateException ("recursive update") or historically a hang. Rules: mapping functions must be short, pure, and never re-enter the map. For expensive loads, store a Future/Lazy holder instead and compute outside the lock (cache-through pattern).

No — volatile applies to the reference arr, not its elements; arr[i] = x is a plain racy write. Options: AtomicIntegerArray (volatile semantics per element), VarHandle with array element access modes (setVolatile/getVolatile), or lock around element access. Same trap as volatile Point p; p.x = 5 — field writes through a volatile reference are not volatile.

Without a happens-before edge, the JIT may hoist the field read out of the loop (it's allowed to assume no other thread changes it): the loop compiles to if (!stop) while(true) work();. The setter thread's write is never observed. Fix: declare volatile boolean stop, or use interruption (the idiomatic cancellation channel), or AtomicBoolean. This is the most common real-world JMM bug.

Speedup ≤ 1 / (S + (1−S)/N) where S = serial fraction. Even 5% serial work caps speedup at 20× no matter how many cores; 50% serial caps at 2×. Serial fractions hide in: lock contention, shared queues, GC, result merging. Interview application: before scaling the pool, find and shrink the serial part — and USL (adding contention+coherence costs) explains why throughput can drop past the optimum.

A switch costs ~1–10µs directly, but the real damage is cache pollution afterwards (warm L1/L2 evicted). Symptoms of oversubscription: high sys% CPU, throughput dropping as threads rise, vmstat showing huge cs/sec. Heuristics: CPU-bound ≈ cores; thousands of blocked threads also burn memory (1MB stack each) and scheduler time. Beyond ~few hundred OS threads doing real work, redesign: async I/O, or virtual threads where blocking is the bottleneck.

void shutdownGracefully(ExecutorService pool) {
    pool.shutdown();                                    // no new tasks
    try {
        if (!pool.awaitTermination(30, TimeUnit.SECONDS)) {
            pool.shutdownNow();                          // interrupt stragglers
            if (!pool.awaitTermination(10, TimeUnit.SECONDS))
                log.error("Pool did not terminate — tasks ignore interrupts?");
        }
    } catch (InterruptedException ie) {
        pool.shutdownNow();
        Thread.currentThread().interrupt();              // preserve status
    }
}

Hook it to @PreDestroy / shutdown hooks; tasks must be interrupt-aware or the second phase is futile.

A virtual thread runs mounted on a carrier (platform) thread from an internal ForkJoinPool (≈ cores). On a blocking call, the JDK unmounts it — continuation stack chunks move to the heap — freeing the carrier; on wakeup it remounts on any carrier. Consequences: don't pool virtual threads (creation is ~1µs — one per task via newVirtualThreadPerTaskExecutor); pools' role shifts from "thread reuse" to concurrency limiting — use Semaphores for that; ThreadLocal works but per-thread caching of heavy objects becomes an anti-pattern at millions of threads.

Pinning = the virtual thread cannot unmount while blocking, holding its carrier hostage: caused by blocking inside a synchronized block/method or native/JNI frames. Enough pinned threads stall the small carrier pool → mysterious throughput collapse. Detect: -Djdk.tracePinnedThreads=full or JFR jdk.VirtualThreadPinned events. Fix: replace hot synchronized-with-blocking by ReentrantLock; upgrade drivers/libraries; JDK 24 (JEP 491) removes the synchronized limitation entirely.

  • Platform pool: CPU-bound work, modest concurrency — nothing beats simplicity.
  • Virtual threads: high-concurrency blocking I/O (typical CRUD/REST/DB) — thread-per-request style, debuggable stack traces, minimal code change.
  • Reactive (WebFlux/Reactor): streaming, backpressure across stages, or pre-21 constraints — at the cost of stacktrace hell and coloring the whole codebase.

Senior take: with Java 21+, virtual threads cover most of reactive's scalability case; reactive remains for genuine streaming/backpressure semantics.

Parallel streams share one commonPool (≈ cores−1 threads): blocking those threads starves every other parallel stream and async CompletableFuture in the process. ForkJoinPool.ManagedBlocker is the escape hatch — wrap the blocking section and the pool temporarily adds compensation threads while yours blocks. Mostly the right answer is: don't block in commonPool; use CF with a dedicated executor or virtual threads.

class Memoizer<K, V> {
    private final ConcurrentMap<K, Future<V>> cache = new ConcurrentHashMap<>();
    private final Function<K, V> compute;

    V get(K key) throws Exception {
        Future<V> f = cache.get(key);
        if (f == null) {
            FutureTask<V> ft = new FutureTask<>(() -> compute.apply(key));
            f = cache.putIfAbsent(key, ft);          // atomic claim
            if (f == null) { f = ft; ft.run(); }      // we won — compute
        }
        try { return f.get(); }                        // others wait, not recompute
        catch (ExecutionException e) { cache.remove(key, f); throw e; }
    }
}

Storing the Future (not the value) closes the duplicate-computation window; failed computations are evicted so they can retry. Straight from JCiP.

A CF doesn't know which thread (if any) runs its completer — it's a result container, not a task handle — so cancel(true) only marks it CancellationException-completed; the underlying work keeps burning CPU. Workarounds: keep the Future from executor.submit() alongside, and on cancel call its cancel(true); or have the task poll cf.isCancelled(); or use orTimeout + interrupt-aware tasks. Loom's structured scopes fix this properly (scope shutdown interrupts forks).

  • synchronized long next++ — correct, contended lock at scale.
  • AtomicLong.incrementAndGet() — lock-free, great to ~millions/sec; CAS storms beyond.
  • Striped/block allocation — each thread grabs a range of 1000 IDs at a time (one Atomic op per 1000) — near-linear scaling, IDs not globally ordered.
  • Snowflake-style — timestamp + nodeId + sequence — distributed-safe, sortable.

LongAdder is wrong here — it can't give you unique values, only aggregate counts. Trade-off to articulate: uniqueness vs strict ordering vs throughput.

Striping: split one lock into N locks by key partition (old ConcurrentHashMap segments; Guava Striped) — contention drops ~N-fold while preserving per-key atomicity. Hand-over-hand (lock coupling) on a list: lock node A, then lock A.next, then release A, advance — writers traverse safely while other threads work on distant parts of the list concurrently. Both trade memory/complexity for parallelism within one structure.

while (true) {
    if (from.lock.tryLock()) {
        try {
            if (to.lock.tryLock()) {
                try { transfer(from, to, amount); return; }
                finally { to.lock.unlock(); }
            }
        } finally { from.lock.unlock(); }
    }
    Thread.sleep(ThreadLocalRandom.current().nextInt(1, 5)); // random backoff
}

tryLock both; on failure release everything and retry with jittered backoff (prevents livelock). Alternative orderings: lock by System.identityHashCode with a tie-breaker lock — the JCiP account-transfer answer.

  • jcstress — OpenJDK's harness that hammers interleavings and checks observed outcomes against the JMM (the only honest race detector).
  • Stress tests: many threads × CountDownLatch-synchronized start × invariant assertions afterward.
  • Awaitility for async assertions instead of sleep(); deterministic executors (same-thread) for logic tests.
  • Make schedules controllable: inject executors/clocks; avoid Thread.sleep coordination (flaky).
  • Accept the limit: tests show presence of bugs, not absence — pair with reviews focused on invariants + happens-before edges.
class Buffer {
    private final Queue<Integer> q = new LinkedList<>();
    private final int CAP = 5;

    public synchronized void produce(int v) throws InterruptedException {
        while (q.size() == CAP) wait();        // buffer full → producer waits
        q.add(v);
        notifyAll();                            // wake consumers
    }
    public synchronized int consume() throws InterruptedException {
        while (q.isEmpty()) wait();             // empty → consumer waits
        int v = q.remove();
        notifyAll();                            // wake producers
        return v;
    }
}

Talking points they probe: why while not if; why notifyAll not notify (mixed waiter types on one monitor); and that BlockingQueue makes all of this one line in real code.

  1. top -H -p <pid> → hottest native thread IDs; convert to hex.
  2. Thread dump (jcmd/jstack) → match hex nid → the exact stack burning CPU.
  3. Usual suspects: infinite/spin loop on a non-volatile flag; regex catastrophic backtracking; unsynchronized HashMap corrupted into a loop (the legendary pre-8 infinite get()); hot deserialization; missing backoff in retry loops.
  4. Confirm with async-profiler/JFR CPU flamegraph.
  5. If GC threads dominate instead → it's a memory problem wearing a CPU costume; pivot to GC logs.

Hashtable / Collections.synchronizedMap: every op takes one global lock — readers block readers; iteration additionally requires manual synchronized(map) around the whole loop or you get CME. ConcurrentHashMap: lock-free reads, striped writes, weakly consistent iterators (no CME, may or may not see concurrent updates — and that's fine for caches), atomic compute/merge for read-modify-write. For the cache use case there is no contest — CHM, plus size bounds via Caffeine if eviction is needed.

  • Storage: ConcurrentHashMap<K, Entry<V>> where Entry = (Future<V> value, long expiresAt).
  • Single-flight: memoizer pattern — putIfAbsent a FutureTask so concurrent misses compute once (Q91).
  • TTL: check expiry on read (lazy) + a scheduled sweeper for cleanup; store expiry, don't schedule per-entry timers.
  • Max size: approximate LRU — access-ordered eviction is costly to do strictly; use size-triggered eviction of oldest/sampled entries, or segment into shards to reduce contention.
  • Metrics: hit/miss via LongAdder.
  • Honest ending: in production use Caffeine — Window-TinyLFU eviction, async loading, refresh-after-write; hand-rolling is for interviews. Explaining why Caffeine's design wins is the senior answer.

🏗️ Java Design Patterns — Top 100

ℹ️ GoF patterns, SOLID, enterprise patterns, patterns inside the JDK & Spring, and scenario-based pattern selection.

Reusable, named solutions to recurring design problems — a shared vocabulary, not copy-paste code. GoF categories: Creational (object creation: Singleton, Factory Method, Abstract Factory, Builder, Prototype), Structural (composition: Adapter, Decorator, Proxy, Facade, Composite, Bridge, Flyweight), Behavioral (interaction: Strategy, Observer, Template Method, Command, State, Chain of Responsibility, Iterator, Mediator, Memento, Visitor, Interpreter).

Ensures one instance with a global access point — for shared resources like configuration or connection registries.

public class AppConfig {
    private static final AppConfig INSTANCE = new AppConfig(); // eager, JVM-safe
    private AppConfig() {}
    public static AppConfig getInstance() { return INSTANCE; }
}

Eager init is thread-safe via class-loading guarantees. Lazy variants: holder idiom, double-checked locking with volatile, or enum.

Define an interface for creating an object, letting subclasses (or a parameterized method) decide which class to instantiate — callers depend on the abstraction, not concrete classes.

interface Notification { void send(String msg); }

class NotificationFactory {
    static Notification create(String channel) {
        return switch (channel) {
            case "EMAIL" -> new EmailNotification();
            case "SMS"   -> new SmsNotification();
            case "PUSH"  -> new PushNotification();
            default -> throw new IllegalArgumentException(channel);
        };
    }
}

Factory Method creates one product via one method. Abstract Factory creates families of related products that must be used together — a factory interface with multiple creation methods, with one concrete factory per family.

interface UIFactory { Button button(); Checkbox checkbox(); }
class DarkThemeFactory implements UIFactory { ... }   // dark family
class LightThemeFactory implements UIFactory { ... }  // light family
// switching theme = swapping ONE factory — products stay consistent

Step-by-step construction of complex objects — the cure for telescoping constructors (4+ params, many optional) and for building immutable objects readably.

User user = User.builder()
    .name("Asha").email("a@x.com")
    .age(30)                      // optional fields only when needed
    .build();                      // validation happens here

JDK examples: StringBuilder, Stream.Builder, HttpRequest.newBuilder(). Lombok @Builder generates it; records reduce (not eliminate) the need.

Create new objects by cloning a pre-configured instance instead of building from scratch — useful when construction is expensive (parsed templates, loaded game entities) or when the concrete type is decided at runtime. In Java: copy constructors or static copy factories are preferred over the broken Cloneable mechanism. Spring's prototype bean scope echoes the idea: a fresh instance per request.

Converts one interface into another that clients expect — the "plug converter" letting incompatible types work together.

// legacy returns XML, new code wants JSON-based Invoice
class LegacyInvoiceAdapter implements InvoiceProvider {
    private final LegacyXmlService legacy;
    public Invoice getInvoice(String id) {
        return parseXmlToInvoice(legacy.fetchXml(id));   // translate
    }
}

JDK: Arrays.asList (array→List), InputStreamReader (byte stream→char stream), Collections.list(Enumeration).

Wrap an object to add behavior without changing its class — wrapper implements the same interface and delegates, adding its feature around the call. Stackable at runtime.

InputStream in = new GZIPInputStream(
                   new BufferedInputStream(
                     new FileInputStream("data.gz")));   // 3 decorators stacked

Also: Collections.unmodifiableList, synchronizedList. Decorator adds behavior; Adapter changes interface; Proxy controls access — same shape, different intent.

A simple front door over a complex subsystem — one class exposing the few operations clients actually need, hiding the orchestration of many internal classes.

class OrderFacade {
    PlacedOrder placeOrder(Cart cart, Payment p) {
        inventory.reserve(cart);
        var receipt = payments.charge(p);
        var shipment = shipping.schedule(cart);
        notifications.confirm(receipt, shipment);
        return new PlacedOrder(receipt, shipment);
    }
}

Your typical @Service orchestrating repositories and clients is a facade. JDK: Executors over ThreadPoolExecutor configs.

A stand-in object controlling access to the real one, same interface. Variants: virtual (lazy-load expensive objects — Hibernate lazy entities), protection (security checks — Spring Security method guards), remote (local stub for a remote service — Feign clients), logging/caching proxies (Spring AOP @Transactional, @Cacheable). Spring wraps your beans in proxies — the single most important pattern for understanding Spring behavior.

One-to-many dependency: when the subject's state changes, all registered observers are notified — decoupling publisher from subscribers.

interface OrderListener { void onPlaced(Order o); }
class OrderService {
    private final List<OrderListener> listeners = new CopyOnWriteArrayList<>();
    void place(Order o) {
        repo.save(o);
        listeners.forEach(l -> l.onPlaced(o));   // notify all
    }
}

Seen as: Swing listeners, Spring ApplicationEvents, Kafka consumers (distributed observer), reactive subscribers.

Encapsulate interchangeable algorithms behind one interface, selected at runtime — kills if/else chains over behavior.

interface PricingStrategy { BigDecimal price(Order o); }

Map<CustomerType, PricingStrategy> strategies = Map.of(
    REGULAR,  o -> o.total(),
    PREMIUM,  o -> o.total().multiply(new BigDecimal("0.90")),
    WHOLESALE,o -> o.total().multiply(new BigDecimal("0.75")));

BigDecimal price = strategies.get(customer.type()).price(order);

JDK: Comparator is a strategy. Functional interfaces made Strategy nearly boilerplate-free.

A base class defines the algorithm's skeleton; subclasses fill in specific steps — "don't call us, we'll call you" (Hollywood principle).

abstract class DataImporter {
    public final void run() {        // fixed skeleton — final!
        var raw = read();
        var rows = parse(raw);
        validate(rows);
        save(rows);
    }
    protected abstract String read();
    protected abstract List<Row> parse(String raw);
    protected void validate(List<Row> r) {}   // optional hook
    protected abstract void save(List<Row> r);
}

Spring's JdbcTemplate/RestTemplate are the spiritual cousins (template + callbacks).

A class should have one reason to change. Violation: an InvoiceService that calculates totals, formats PDF, sends email, and writes to DB — four unrelated reasons to change, four owners colliding in one file. Split into calculator, renderer, notifier, repository — each independently testable and changeable.

Open for extension, closed for modification — add behavior by adding code, not editing tested code.

// ✘ every new type edits this method
double area(Object s) { if (s instanceof Circle c) ...; else if (...) ... }

// ✔ new shape = new class, area() untouched
interface Shape { double area(); }
record Circle(double r) implements Shape { public double area() { return Math.PI*r*r; } }

Strategy, Template Method, and polymorphism are OCP's main tools.

Subtypes must be usable wherever the base type is, without breaking expectations. Square extends Rectangle fails: setWidth(4); setHeight(5) on a Square gives area 25, violating the Rectangle contract (width and height independent) — code valid for Rectangle breaks for Square. LSP violations show as: overridden methods throwing UnsupportedOperationException, strengthened preconditions, type-checks before calling. Fix: model differently (immutable shapes, composition).

Clients shouldn't be forced to depend on methods they don't use — prefer several small role interfaces over one fat one. Violation symptom: implementations full of throw new UnsupportedOperationException() (java.util.Collection's optional ops are the JDK's own confession). Fix: Readable/Writable instead of one giant FileSystemItem with 20 methods.

DIP (principle): high-level policy depends on abstractions, not concrete details — OrderService depends on a PaymentGateway interface, not on RazorpayClient. DI (technique): someone else (Spring container, constructor caller) supplies the concrete implementation. DI is the mechanism that makes DIP practical: swap implementations in config/tests without touching business code.

  • DRY — one authoritative representation of each piece of knowledge. Caution: deduplicating coincidentally similar code couples unrelated features (wrong abstraction > duplication).
  • KISS — simplest design that works. Caution: simple ≠ simplistic; don't ignore real requirements.
  • YAGNI — don't build for imagined future needs. Caution: doesn't excuse skipping necessary design seams (interfaces at module boundaries).

Sequential access to a collection's elements without exposing its internals — hasNext()/next(). Java bakes it in: Iterable + the for-each loop (compiles to iterator calls), Iterator.remove() for safe removal during traversal, and Spliterator as the parallel evolution. Writing a custom Iterable lets your own data structures work in for-each.

Reify a request as an object — decoupling "what to do" from "when/who executes it" — enabling queues, undo, retry, and logging of operations.

interface Command { void execute(); void undo(); }
record InsertText(Document d, String text) implements Command {
    public void execute() { d.append(text); }
    public void undo()    { d.removeLast(text.length()); }
}
// editor keeps Deque<Command> history → undo = pop().undo()

Runnable/Callable are commands; task queues and transactional outbox entries are commands at scale.

Pass a request along a chain of handlers; each handles it, enriches it, or passes it on — sender doesn't know which handler acts.

// Servlet filters ARE the pattern:
public void doFilter(ServletRequest req, ServletResponse res, FilterChain chain) {
    if (!authenticated(req)) { reject(res); return; }  // handle & stop
    chain.doFilter(req, res);                           // pass along
}

Also: Spring Security filter chain, interceptor chains, logging frameworks' appender chains, exception handler resolution.

DAO (Data Access Object) — encapsulates persistence operations for an entity (findById, save) — a behavioral boundary to the database; Spring Data repositories are generated DAOs. DTO (Data Transfer Object) — a dumb data carrier shaped for transport across boundaries (API responses), decoupling wire format from entities. DAO is about operations; DTO is about data shape. Exposing JPA entities directly in REST responses instead of DTOs is the classic violation.

Separate Model (data + business rules), View (presentation), Controller (handles input, coordinates). In Spring: @RestController methods (controller) ↔ services/entities (model) ↔ JSON serialization or Thymeleaf templates (view), with DispatcherServlet as the Front Controller routing every request. Benefit: each layer changes and tests independently.

Instead of returning null, return a do-nothing implementation of the interface — eliminating null checks at every call site.

interface DiscountPolicy { BigDecimal apply(BigDecimal amt); }
class NoDiscount implements DiscountPolicy {
    public BigDecimal apply(BigDecimal amt) { return amt; }   // harmless default
}
// callers always have a policy — code reads linearly, no if (policy != null)

JDK flavors: empty collections (Collections.emptyList) instead of null returns; Optional is the type-level cousin.

State lets an object alter its behavior when its internal state changes — it appears to change class. Each state is an object; the context delegates to the current state, which can transition the context to another state.

interface OrderState { OrderState next(Order o); void cancel(Order o); }
class Placed implements OrderState { public OrderState next(Order o){ return new Shipped(); } ... }
class Shipped implements OrderState { ... }

Difference: Strategy's algorithms are independent and chosen by the client; State's objects know about each other and drive transitions. Same structure, opposite intent — Strategy = "how", State = "what stage".

Treat individual objects and compositions of objects uniformly through a common interface — model part-whole tree hierarchies.

interface FileNode { long size(); }
record File(String name, long bytes) implements FileNode {
    public long size() { return bytes; }
}
class Folder implements FileNode {
    private final List<FileNode> children = new ArrayList<>();
    public long size() { return children.stream().mapToLong(FileNode::size).sum(); }
}

JDK/UI: Swing containers, the DOM, abstract syntax trees. Client code recurses without caring whether a node is a leaf or a branch.

Decouple an abstraction from its implementation so the two vary independently — composition instead of an exploding inheritance matrix.

// Without bridge: RedCircle, BlueCircle, RedSquare... = shapes × colors classes
abstract class Shape {
    protected final Renderer renderer;   // the bridge
    Shape(Renderer r) { this.renderer = r; }
    abstract void draw();
}
interface Renderer { void render(String shape); }   // implementation hierarchy

Now N shapes + M renderers = N+M classes, not N×M. JDBC is the canonical example: the JDBC API (abstraction) bridges to driver implementations.

Share fine-grained objects to save memory by separating intrinsic state (shared, immutable) from extrinsic state (passed in by the client). Use when you have millions of similar objects.

JDK examples: the Integer cache (-128..127), String pool, Character cache. A text editor sharing one glyph object per character, with position passed externally, is the textbook case. The key insight interviewers want: flyweights must be immutable since they're shared.

Centralize complex many-to-many communication into a mediator object, so components talk to the mediator instead of each other — reducing the spaghetti of mutual references.

// Air traffic control: planes don't coordinate with each other directly;
// they all talk to the tower (mediator), which coordinates.
interface ChatRoom { void send(String from, String msg); }

Spring's ApplicationEventPublisher acts as a mediator. Trade-off: the mediator can become a god object if overloaded.

Capture and externalize an object's internal state so it can be restored later, without violating encapsulation. Three roles: Originator (creates/restores mementos), Memento (immutable snapshot), Caretaker (stores mementos, never inspects them).

class Editor {
    private String content;
    Memento save() { return new Memento(content); }   // snapshot
    void restore(Memento m) { this.content = m.state(); }
    record Memento(String state) {}                    // opaque to caretaker
}

Powers undo/redo, transaction rollback, and game save-states.

Separate an algorithm from the object structure it operates on — add new operations over a fixed hierarchy without modifying the element classes.

interface Visitor { void visit(Circle c); void visit(Square s); }
interface Shape { void accept(Visitor v); }
class Circle implements Shape { public void accept(Visitor v){ v.visit(this); } }

Double dispatch: the actual method called depends on two runtime types — the element (via accept) and the visitor (via visit overload). Modern Java alternative: sealed interfaces + pattern-matching switch, which is exhaustive and far less boilerplate.

Object construction (instance = new Singleton()) is not atomic: allocate memory, run constructor, assign reference. The JIT may reorder the assignment before the constructor completes. Another thread sees a non-null but partially constructed object and uses it → corruption.

private static volatile Singleton instance;  // volatile is mandatory
static Singleton get() {
    if (instance == null) {
        synchronized (Singleton.class) {
            if (instance == null) instance = new Singleton();
        }
    }
    return instance;
}

volatile establishes a happens-before edge that forbids the reorder. Better: use the holder idiom or enum.

  1. Eager: static final INSTANCE = new X() — simple, thread-safe, but no lazy loading.
  2. Synchronized method: lazy but locks on every call (slow).
  3. Double-checked locking: lazy + fast, needs volatile, verbose.
  4. Holder idiom (Bill Pugh): lazy via class-loading, no sync — clean.
  5. Enum singleton: the best per Effective Java — thread-safe, serialization-safe, reflection-proof, one line.
public enum DatabasePool { INSTANCE; public Connection get() {...} }
  • Reflection: setAccessible(true) on the private constructor → throw from constructor if instance exists.
  • Serialization: deserialization creates a new object → implement readResolve() returning INSTANCE.
  • Cloning: override clone() to throw.
  • Multiple classloaders: each loader = separate instance (rarely defensible).

Enum singletons are immune to all of these by design — which is exactly why they're recommended.

Static utility class (private constructor + static methods): stateless helpers (Collections, Math). Singleton: a single object with state that can implement interfaces, be passed as a dependency, support lazy init, and be mocked. Key advantage of singleton: it participates in polymorphism and DI; a static class cannot implement an interface or be injected, making it untestable. Prefer singleton-as-injected-bean over static for anything stateful.

Spring beans are singletons by default, but it's a "singleton per container", not the GoF JVM-wide singleton — different ApplicationContexts hold different instances. Spring manages the lifecycle and injects it, avoiding the GoF singleton's downsides (global static state, untestability, hidden dependencies). The critical caveat: a singleton bean must be stateless or thread-safe, since one instance serves all concurrent requests.

PatternInterfaceIntent
AdapterChanges itMake incompatible interfaces work together
DecoratorSameAdd responsibilities dynamically (stackable)
ProxySameControl access (lazy, security, remote, cache)

Structurally all three wrap an object. The exam answer: Adapter converts, Decorator enhances, Proxy guards. Decorator is typically client-stacked; Proxy usually controls a single subject transparently.

Spring wraps your bean in a proxy that intercepts method calls to apply cross-cutting concerns (@Transactional, @Cacheable, @Async). JDK dynamic proxy — used when the bean implements an interface; generates a proxy implementing that interface. CGLIB — used for concrete classes; generates a subclass at runtime (so the class/methods can't be final). This explains the classic bug: self-invocation (calling this.transactionalMethod()) bypasses the proxy, so the annotation does nothing.

  • Singleton: Runtime.getRuntime()
  • Factory Method: Calendar.getInstance(), valueOf()
  • Builder: StringBuilder, Stream.Builder, HttpRequest.newBuilder()
  • Decorator: java.io streams, Collections.unmodifiable*
  • Adapter: Arrays.asList, InputStreamReader
  • Observer: java.util.Observer (deprecated), Swing listeners
  • Iterator: Iterator, Enumeration
  • Strategy: Comparator; Template Method: AbstractList

A DTO is a flat, serialization-friendly object shaped for a specific API contract, decoupling the wire format from the persistence model. Map entities → DTOs when: exposing data over REST (never leak entities — avoids over-fetching, lazy-loading exceptions, and accidental field exposure), aggregating data from multiple entities, or versioning APIs independently of the schema. Tools: MapStruct (compile-time, fast), ModelMapper, or manual mapping. The interview red flag is returning JPA entities straight from controllers.

Repository mediates between the domain and data-mapping layers, presenting a collection-like interface for accessing aggregates — the domain code never sees SQL/JPQL. Spring Data takes it further: you declare an interface extending JpaRepository, and Spring generates the implementation at runtime (a dynamic proxy) from method-name conventions (findByEmailAndActiveTrue) or @Query. This is Repository + Proxy + Strategy combined.

The service layer holds business logic and defines transaction boundaries, sitting between controllers (HTTP concerns) and repositories (persistence). Keep controllers thin because: business logic in controllers can't be reused across entry points (REST + messaging + scheduled jobs), is hard to unit-test without the web layer, and mixes HTTP details with domain rules. The controller's job: validate input, call a service, map the result to a response.

Encapsulate a business rule/query criterion as a reusable, combinable object — composed with and(), or(), not().

Specification<Product> cheap = (root, q, cb) -> cb.lt(root.get("price"), 100);
Specification<Product> inStock = (root, q, cb) -> cb.gt(root.get("qty"), 0);
repo.findAll(cheap.and(inStock));   // Spring Data JPA Specifications

Lets you build dynamic queries from optional filters without writing N query methods or string-concatenating JPQL.

Reuse a pool of pre-created expensive objects instead of allocating/destroying repeatedly — borrow, use, return. Worth it for objects costly to create with limited supply: DB connections (HikariCP), thread pools, large buffers. Not worth it for cheap objects — modern JVM allocation is extremely fast, and pooling lightweight objects adds contention, leak risk (forgetting to return), and stale-state bugs. The classic anti-pattern is pooling ordinary domain objects "for performance".

Inheritance is rigid (fixed at compile time, single parent, exposes the parent's implementation = fragile base class problem). Composition assembles behavior from parts at runtime, swappable and testable. Strategy is composition in action: instead of subclassing to vary an algorithm, you compose the object with a strategy interface and inject the variant. Effective Java Item 18: "Favor composition over inheritance." Use inheritance only for true is-a relationships in classes designed for it.

  • God Object: one class doing everything (violates SRP).
  • Spaghetti code: no structure, tangled control flow.
  • Golden Hammer: applying one favorite pattern/tool everywhere.
  • Singleton abuse: global mutable state masquerading as a pattern.
  • Anemic domain model: entities with only getters/setters, all logic in services.
  • Premature optimization and over-engineering (patterns for imagined needs).

Facade provides a simplified one-directional entry point to a subsystem — the subsystem doesn't know the facade exists, and components don't call back. Mediator coordinates bidirectional, many-to-many interaction — the components know and use the mediator to communicate with each other. Facade simplifies access; Mediator centralizes interaction. A Facade reduces coupling between client and subsystem; a Mediator reduces coupling among peers.

Replace conditional with polymorphism. Options by situation:

  • Strategy + Map: Map<Type, Handler> — O(1) dispatch, open for extension, each handler testable.
  • Polymorphism: move behavior into the type hierarchy (each subclass implements the method).
  • Enum with abstract methods: when the set of types is fixed and small.
  • Sealed interface + pattern switch (Java 17+): exhaustive, compiler-checked.

Each new case becomes a new class/entry rather than another branch edited into tested code.

Several patterns collapse to a lambda or method reference, eliminating boilerplate classes:

  • Strategy → a Function/custom functional interface passed directly.
  • Command → a Runnable.
  • Template Method → higher-order function taking the variable step as a lambda.
  • Observer → list of Consumer callbacks.
  • Factory → a Supplier or constructor reference (ArrayList::new).

The patterns' intent remains; the verbose class scaffolding largely disappears.

The builder accumulates fields in a mutable temporary object, then build() constructs the immutable target in one shot — and is the single place to validate cross-field invariants before the object exists.

public Reservation build() {
    if (checkOut.isBefore(checkIn))
        throw new IllegalStateException("checkOut before checkIn");
    return new Reservation(this);   // immutable, fully validated
}

Benefits over constructor: readable named params, optional fields without overload explosion, and no invalid intermediate state escapes.

Methods return this (or a new instance) so calls chain readably: query.select("x").from("t").where("y").build(). Pitfalls: returning this on an immutable type is wrong (must return a new instance); chaining hides where exceptions occur (poor stack traces mid-chain); and inheritance breaks fluent APIs (subclass methods return the parent type — solved with self-bounded generics <T extends Builder<T>>).

Template Method uses inheritance: the skeleton is fixed in a base class, subclasses override steps — compile-time binding, tight coupling, one variation axis. Strategy uses composition: the algorithm is injected — runtime swappable, looser coupling, testable in isolation. Prefer Strategy when steps vary independently or you need runtime selection; Template Method when the overall flow is genuinely fixed and you want to enforce it (the skeleton method is final).

A single entry point handles all requests, centralizing cross-cutting concerns (routing, auth, logging) before dispatching to handlers. Spring MVC's DispatcherServlet is the front controller: every request hits it, then it consults HandlerMapping to find the controller, applies interceptors, invokes the handler, and resolves the view/response. Centralization means consistent behavior and one place to add filters.

Pluggable handlers that wrap request processing (a Chain of Responsibility). Servlet Filters operate at the container level (before Spring) — good for encoding, CORS, security. Spring HandlerInterceptor operates within Spring MVC with access to the handler and model (preHandle, postHandle, afterCompletion) — good for auth checks, timing, MDC setup. Both let you add behavior without touching controllers.

IoC is the broad principle that a framework controls the flow and lifecycle, calling your code (Hollywood principle) rather than your code driving everything. Dependency Injection is one form of IoC — the container creates and wires dependencies instead of objects instantiating their own collaborators. Other IoC forms: template methods, callbacks, event-driven dispatch. DI specifically inverts object construction control.

Constructor injection is preferred: dependencies are final (immutable, guaranteed non-null), the object is fully initialized after construction, it's trivially testable without a container (just new it with mocks), and it surfaces "too many dependencies" as an obvious code smell. Field injection (@Autowired on a field) hides dependencies, allows nulls, can't be final, requires reflection to test, and enables circular dependencies to sneak in. Spring recommends constructor injection.

Publishers emit events; subscribers consume them via an intermediary (event bus/broker) — publishers and subscribers don't reference each other at all. Difference from Observer: Observer has the subject directly hold and call its observers (in-process, synchronous, tightly bound); Pub-Sub adds a broker decoupling them entirely, enabling async and cross-process delivery (Kafka, Spring's ApplicationEventPublisher, message queues). Pub-Sub scales Observer to distributed systems.

A way to maintain data consistency across services without distributed transactions: a business transaction becomes a sequence of local transactions, each publishing an event that triggers the next; failures trigger compensating transactions that semantically undo prior steps. Two flavors: choreography (services react to each other's events — decentralized) and orchestration (a central saga coordinator drives the steps). Trades ACID for eventual consistency.

Command Query Responsibility Segregation — separate the model that writes data (commands) from the model that reads it (queries), often with separate data stores optimized for each. Benefits: independent scaling of reads vs writes, read models denormalized for fast queries, simpler write models. Cost: complexity and eventual consistency between write and read sides. Pairs naturally with Event Sourcing. Use only when read/write loads genuinely diverge — overkill for CRUD.

Persist state as an immutable, append-only sequence of events rather than current state; rebuild current state by replaying events. Benefits: full audit trail, temporal queries ("state as of last Tuesday"), and you can derive new read models retroactively. Costs: event schema evolution is hard, replaying long streams needs snapshots, and querying current state requires projections. Often combined with CQRS (events feed read models).

Prevents cascading failures by "tripping" when a downstream dependency keeps failing, failing fast instead of waiting on timeouts. Three states:

  • Closed: calls pass through; failures counted.
  • Open: failure threshold exceeded → calls fail immediately (fallback) for a cooldown.
  • Half-Open: after cooldown, allow a few trial calls; success → Closed, failure → Open again.

Implemented by Resilience4j (or legacy Hystrix). Combine with retries, timeouts, and bulkheads.

Deploy a helper component in its own process/container alongside the main application (same pod/host), handling cross-cutting concerns — logging, monitoring, configuration, TLS, service-discovery — without the app needing that code. The service mesh (Envoy proxy in Istio) is the canonical sidecar: it intercepts all network traffic for observability, retries, and mTLS, keeping the application language-agnostic and focused on business logic.

A single entry point in front of microservices that handles routing, authentication, rate limiting, request aggregation, protocol translation, and response caching — a Facade + Front Controller for a distributed system. Clients talk to one endpoint instead of dozens of services; the gateway shields them from the internal topology. Tools: Spring Cloud Gateway, Kong, AWS API Gateway. Risk: it can become a bottleneck or a god component if business logic creeps in.

An incremental migration strategy: gradually replace a legacy system by routing specific features to new services while the old system still handles the rest, until the legacy is "strangled" and removed. A facade/proxy/gateway sits in front, deciding old-vs-new per request. Lets you modernize without a risky big-bang rewrite, delivering and validating value continuously. Named after the strangler fig vine that grows around and eventually replaces its host tree.

Isolate resources (thread pools, connection pools) per dependency so a failure in one doesn't sink the whole application — like a ship's watertight compartments. If service A is slow, calls to A exhaust only A's dedicated pool; service B's pool is untouched, so B's traffic keeps flowing. Implemented via separate thread pools or semaphore-limited concurrency per downstream (Resilience4j Bulkhead). Pairs with circuit breakers for resilient microservices.

Subclassing to combine N optional features needs 2^N classes (BufferedGzipEncryptedStream...) — a combinatorial explosion fixed at compile time. Decorators compose features at runtime in any order and combination, each a single small class, following the Single Responsibility Principle. You also avoid the fragile base class problem. The cost: many small objects and harder debugging through deep wrapper stacks.

It produces a family of related objects guaranteed to be compatible. For multi-DB support, a PostgresFactory creates Postgres-specific connection, dialect, and query builder; a MySqlFactory creates the MySQL family. Client code depends only on the abstract factory interface, so switching databases means swapping one factory — and you can never accidentally mix a Postgres dialect with a MySQL connection. Hibernate's dialect system embodies this idea.

Library: you call its code — you're in control (e.g. Apache Commons). Framework: it calls your code — Inversion of Control, the Hollywood Principle (e.g. Spring calls your beans, JUnit calls your test methods). A framework defines the skeleton (Template Method at architecture scale) and you fill in the extension points. This is why frameworks impose structure while libraries are à la carte.

The listener list is the shared mutable state. Use CopyOnWriteArrayList so notification iterates a stable snapshot without locking, while add/remove are safe — and a listener removing itself during notification won't throw ConcurrentModificationException.

private final List<Listener> listeners = new CopyOnWriteArrayList<>();
void notifyAll(Event e) {
    for (Listener l : listeners) l.onEvent(e);  // snapshot iteration, no lock
}

Also consider: notify outside any lock you hold (avoid deadlock), and handle listener exceptions so one bad listener doesn't break the rest.

Defer creating an expensive object until first use. Risks center on concurrency: naive lazy init has a race (two threads both see null and both create). Safe approaches: holder idiom, double-checked locking with volatile, or ConcurrentHashMap.computeIfAbsent. Other risks: hidden latency on first access (cold-start), and exceptions during init surfacing unexpectedly later. For beans, prefer eager init unless startup cost is provably a problem.

Default methods let interfaces evolve and provide shared behavior without state and with multiple inheritance of behavior — but they can't hold fields or enforce invariants, and diamond conflicts must be resolved manually. Abstract classes carry state and constructors but burn the single-inheritance slot and create tighter coupling. Rule: use interfaces (with defaults) for capabilities and to keep types flexible; use abstract classes only when shared mutable state or template-method skeletons with protected helpers are genuinely needed.

A well-known object that other objects use to find common services/handlers by key — a managed alternative to scattering singletons or globals. Spring's ApplicationContext is effectively a bean registry. Risks mirror Singleton's: it's global mutable state, can hide dependencies, and complicates testing if overused. Prefer DI (inject what you need) over reaching into a registry, but registries are useful for dynamic, plugin-style lookup where the set of services isn't known at compile time.

Spring Security is a FilterChainProxy wrapping an ordered list of security filters — each filter (authentication, CSRF, authorization, exception translation) either handles the request, modifies the security context, or passes it down the chain. A filter can short-circuit (e.g. reject unauthenticated access) or delegate via chain.doFilter(). This is the textbook Chain of Responsibility, and understanding the filter order is key to debugging security behavior.

Object adapter (preferred in Java): the adapter holds a reference to the adaptee and delegates — uses composition, works with any subclass of the adaptee, doesn't burn the inheritance slot. Class adapter: the adapter extends the adaptee and implements the target interface — relies on multiple inheritance, which Java doesn't allow for classes, so it's only possible when adapting via an interface. Java idiomatically uses the object adapter because of single inheritance and the composition-over-inheritance principle.

Strategy for the interchangeable PaymentGateway interface (each gateway an implementation). Factory/Abstract Factory to instantiate the right gateway by config/region. Adapter to wrap each vendor SDK's incompatible API behind your interface. Facade (PaymentService) orchestrating gateway + retry + ledger. Circuit Breaker + Retry for resilience per gateway. Adding a new gateway = new adapter + factory entry, zero changes to existing code (OCP). Inject the map of strategies via Spring so the set is extensible.

Command as the backbone: each edit is a Command with execute() and undo(); two stacks (undo/redo) hold history. For coarse state, Memento snapshots the document and the caretaker stores them (simpler but memory-heavy). Combine: Command for fine-grained operations + Memento for periodic snapshots to bound replay cost. Add Composite commands (a macro = list of commands) so grouped edits undo atomically. Trade-off discussion (memory vs replay) is what interviewers reward.

State pattern: each status is a state object encapsulating which transitions are legal and what happens on each event; illegal transitions throw or are no-ops — no giant switch scattered across the service. For Java 17+, model states as a sealed interface + records and use exhaustive pattern-matching switches for transition logic (compiler enforces completeness). Add Observer/events so each transition publishes a domain event (email, analytics). Persist the state and guard transitions in one transactional method to avoid races.

Strategy for each channel behind a NotificationChannel interface; Factory to resolve channels by user preference. To send via multiple channels, use Composite (a CompositeChannel iterating children) — clients treat one or many uniformly. Decorator for cross-cutting add-ons (rate-limit, retry, audit) wrapping any channel. Template Method for the common send flow (render template → format → dispatch → log). Adding WhatsApp = one new Strategy class registered in the factory.

Specification pattern for composable boolean rules (and/or/not), each rule a unit-testable object. Chain of Responsibility if rules form a pipeline where one can short-circuit. Load rule definitions from config/DB and build the specification tree dynamically (a small Interpreter if rules come as expressions). Strategy + Registry to map rule IDs to implementations. For hot-reload, watch the config source and rebuild the rule set atomically (swap an immutable rule tree via a volatile reference).

All three are the same idea (wrap and intercept). Decorator: explicit, type-safe, you hand-wrap each object — verbose but transparent and debuggable. Dynamic Proxy: generate wrappers at runtime for any interface — less boilerplate, used by frameworks. AOP (Spring): declarative — annotate methods and an aspect applies the concern via proxies/weaving, zero wrapper code. Choose AOP for truly cross-cutting concerns across many classes; Decorator when the enhancement is part of the domain design and you want explicitness. Note AOP's self-invocation caveat.

Patterns are answers to recurring problems — applying them speculatively adds indirection, classes, and cognitive load for no benefit (a FactoryBuilderStrategy to create a POJO). Heuristics: start with the simplest code that works; introduce a pattern only when a real force appears (a second variant, a need for runtime swapping, an actual extension point). Refactor toward patterns when duplication or change-pain emerges, not before. A plain method or a lambda often beats a four-class pattern. Senior signal: justifying the pattern by the concrete pressure it relieves.

Define stable SPI interfaces in the core. Use Java's ServiceLoader (or Spring's component scanning) to discover implementations at runtime via META-INF/services — the core depends only on the interface (DIP). Abstract Factory for plugins that create families of objects; Observer/event bus so plugins hook lifecycle events without the core knowing them. Isolate plugins with separate classloaders for versioning/sandboxing. The core is closed for modification, open for extension via the SPI.

Visitor when the type hierarchy is stable but operations are added often (each operation = one visitor), and you can't use modern Java — but it's verbose (accept/visit boilerplate) and adding a node type breaks every visitor. Sealed interface + pattern switch (Java 17+) is the modern winner for ASTs: exhaustive (compiler flags missing cases), no boilerplate, record patterns destructure nodes, and adding an operation is just another method with a switch. Choose Visitor only for legacy Java or when third parties must add operations externally.

Decorator/Proxy wrapping the call with retry logic; Strategy for the backoff policy (fixed, exponential, exponential-with-jitter). Pitfalls to raise: retrying non-idempotent operations causes duplicates (only retry idempotent or use idempotency keys); retry storms amplify outages (add jitter, cap attempts); always combine with a circuit breaker so you stop hammering a dead service; set per-attempt timeouts. Resilience4j composes these as decorators. The senior point: retries without circuit breaking + idempotency make failures worse.

Make the config an immutable object (record or final fields) built via Builder. For hot-reload, hold it behind a volatile (or AtomicReference) field and atomically swap the whole reference when config changes — readers always see a consistent snapshot, never a half-updated state (copy-on-write at object granularity). Notify dependents via Observer. This avoids locking on every read and avoids torn reads. Spring's @RefreshScope achieves similar semantics by recreating beans.

Pipeline/Chain of processing stages, each a small Strategy implementing a common Stage interface (parse → enrich → filter → aggregate → sink). Compose them as a list built from config (order is data, not code). Decorator for cross-cutting per-stage concerns (metrics, error handling). For 1-to-many transforms, model stages as functions returning streams (flatMap). Backpressure via bounded queues between stages (Producer-Consumer). Adding/reordering a stage = config change. This is essentially how Kafka Streams / Flink topologies are structured.

An abstract step must be implemented by subclasses (the algorithm can't run without it). A hook is a concrete method with an empty or default body that subclasses may override to inject optional behavior at defined points (e.g. beforeSave(), isCachingEnabled() returning false by default). Hooks make the template flexible without forcing every subclass to provide every step. Spring's lifecycle callbacks and HttpServlet.doXxx methods are hooks.

Strategy behind a FeatureFlag interface (boolean per context). A Facade FeatureService hides the flag source (config, DB, LaunchDarkly). Null Object for a default-off flag so callers never null-check. For gradual rollouts, the strategy evaluates percentage/user-segment rules (Specification). Inject the service (DI) so tests stub flags trivially. Avoid scattering if (flag) everywhere — encapsulate flag-gated behavior behind Strategy objects selected by the flag, so removing a flag later is a localized change.

The dual-write problem: you can't atomically update the DB and publish to Kafka — if one succeeds and the other fails, state and events diverge. The Transactional Outbox writes the event to an outbox table in the same DB transaction as the business change; a separate relay (polling or CDC like Debezium) reads the outbox and publishes to the broker, marking rows sent. This guarantees at-least-once delivery consistent with the DB state. Consumers must be idempotent. It's the standard fix for reliable event publishing in microservices.

Generics give type-safe factories (Factory<T>) and inheritable fluent builders. A self-bounded generic lets a subclass builder return its own type from inherited methods (so chaining doesn't downcast):

abstract class Builder<T extends Builder<T>> {
    String name;
    T name(String n) { this.name = n; return self(); }
    abstract T self();
}
class CarBuilder extends Builder<CarBuilder> {
    CarBuilder self() { return this; }   // chain stays CarBuilder
}

This "curiously recurring template pattern" (CRTP) is how hierarchical builders preserve fluency across inheritance.

Strategy behind a Cache interface (get/put/evict) with in-memory and Redis implementations. Decorator to layer features: a MetricsCache, a NullGuardCache, or a Composite/multi-level cache (L1 in-memory in front of L2 Redis, checking L1 first, populating it on L2 hit). Proxy for cache-aside/read-through (transparently load on miss). Template Method for the get-or-load flow. Spring's @Cacheable + CacheManager abstraction is exactly this — Strategy (CacheManager) + Proxy (AOP).

Define a grammar and an interpreter that evaluates sentences in that language — each grammar rule becomes a class, composed into an AST that you evaluate recursively (Composite + Interpreter). Appropriate for small, stable DSLs: simple rule expressions, query filters, calculators. Inappropriate for complex grammars — the class-per-rule approach explodes and performs poorly; use a real parser generator (ANTLR) or an existing expression engine (SpEL, MVEL) instead. Interview answer: great teaching pattern, rarely the right production tool beyond trivial languages.

Orchestration (central coordinator commands each service): explicit, easy to reason about and monitor, clear compensation flow — but the orchestrator is a coupling point and can become complex. Choreography (services react to each other's events, no central brain): loosely coupled, scales well — but the overall flow is implicit, hard to trace/debug, and cyclic event dependencies sneak in. Rule of thumb: few steps + simple flow → choreography; many steps, complex branching, or strong observability needs → orchestration (Camunda, Temporal, Spring StateMachine).

State pattern for the workflow's current step and legal transitions; Command for each step's action + compensation (undo) — enabling Saga rollback. Strategy/Registry mapping step types to executors so new step kinds plug in. Memento/Event Sourcing to persist progress and resume after crashes (the engine replays to current state). Observer to emit step events. The workflow definition is data (JSON/DB), interpreted by the engine — closed for modification, open for new workflows without code changes.

Patterns are refactoring targets, introduced when code smells appear — not designed upfront (Kerievsky's "Refactoring to Patterns"). Examples: (1) a sprawling switch on a type code that keeps growing → Replace Conditional with Polymorphism / Strategy. (2) a constructor with many optional parameters and telescoping overloads → introduce a Builder. (3) duplicated algorithm skeletons across subclasses → Form Template Method. The discipline: let the smell justify the pattern, and remove patterns that no longer earn their complexity.

For REST: require an Idempotency-Key header; store processed keys with their response (a Registry/cache) and on retry return the stored result instead of re-executing. For consumers: track processed message IDs (an idempotent receiver) in a dedup store, or design operations to be naturally idempotent (upsert by business key, set-state-to-X rather than increment). Combine with the Outbox on the producer side. The key insight: at-least-once delivery makes idempotency the consumer's responsibility — dedup table + unique constraints are the workhorse.

A translation layer between your domain and an external/legacy system, preventing the foreign model from leaking into and corrupting yours. It's an Adapter + Facade at a bounded-context boundary: it translates the external system's terms, data shapes, and semantics into your domain's language (and back). Use when integrating with a legacy monolith, a third-party API, or another team's service whose model conflicts with yours — so a messy upstream model can't dictate your design. Common during strangler-fig migrations.

Two varying dimensions → Bridge: separate the exporter abstraction (handles layout/structure) from the format implementation (PDF/Excel/CSV renderer), so N report types × M formats = N+M classes, not N×M. Use Strategy/Factory to pick the format renderer at runtime. Template Method for the common export skeleton (fetch data → transform → render → stream). Builder for complex document assembly. Adding a new format = one renderer; adding a report = one abstraction — both independent (OCP).

  1. Identify the force, not the pattern: what is actually changing or hard to change? (a new variant, runtime selection, an extension point, cross-cutting concern).
  2. Match force → pattern: varying algorithm → Strategy; varying creation → Factory; adding behavior → Decorator; controlling access → Proxy; one-to-many notify → Observer; complex creation → Builder.
  3. Prefer the lightest tool: a lambda, a method, or an interface often beats a multi-class pattern.
  4. Honor SOLID and composition-over-inheritance as the underlying goals patterns serve.
  5. Defer until justified (YAGNI) and refactor toward patterns when smells appear.
  6. Weigh the cost: indirection and class count vs the flexibility gained. If you can't name the pressure, don't add the pattern.

🍃 Spring Boot — Top 100

ℹ️ IoC/DI, auto-configuration, starters, REST, profiles, actuator, testing, and internals. Easy → conceptual.

Spring Framework provides the core (IoC container, DI, AOP, MVC) but requires extensive manual configuration. Spring Boot is an opinionated layer on top that adds auto-configuration, starter dependencies, embedded servers, and production-ready features (Actuator) so you can run a stand-alone app with minimal setup. Slogan: "convention over configuration." Boot doesn't replace Spring — it bootstraps it.

  • Dependency management: starters bundle compatible versions (no version conflicts).
