  • Configuration boilerplate: auto-configuration sets sensible defaults.
  • Deployment: embedded Tomcat/Netty → runnable JAR, no external server.
  • Production readiness: Actuator health/metrics out of the box.
  • Getting started: Spring Initializr scaffolds a project in seconds.

It's a meta-annotation combining three:

  • @Configuration — marks the class as a source of bean definitions.
  • @EnableAutoConfiguration — triggers Boot's auto-configuration mechanism.
  • @ComponentScan — scans the package (and sub-packages) for components.

This is why placing the main class at the root package matters — component scanning starts there.

IoC: the Spring container, not your code, controls object creation and lifecycle. DI: the container injects a bean's dependencies rather than the bean creating them with new. Result: loose coupling (depend on interfaces), easy testing (inject mocks), and centralized configuration. The ApplicationContext is the IoC container.

A bean is any object instantiated, assembled, and managed by the Spring IoC container. The ApplicationContext is that container — it reads configuration (annotations/Java config), creates beans, resolves dependencies, manages lifecycle, and provides them on demand. It extends BeanFactory with enterprise features (events, i18n, AOP integration, environment/profiles).

All are stereotypes that register beans via component scanning. They differ in semantic intent and some add behavior:

  • @Component — generic bean.
  • @Service — business logic layer (semantic only).
  • @Repository — persistence layer; adds exception translation (SQL exceptions → Spring's DataAccessException).
  • @Controller / @RestController — web layer; handles HTTP requests.

@RestController = @Controller + @ResponseBody on every method. A plain @Controller returns view names (resolved to templates like Thymeleaf/JSP); a @RestController serializes return values directly to the response body (JSON/XML) — the standard for REST APIs.

@RequestMapping is the general one; the shortcuts are method-specific: @GetMapping, @PostMapping, @PutMapping, @DeleteMapping, @PatchMapping. Parameter binding: @PathVariable (URI template), @RequestParam (query/form), @RequestBody (deserialize request body), @RequestHeader.

@GetMapping("/users/{id}")
User get(@PathVariable Long id, @RequestParam(defaultValue="false") boolean full) {...}

Curated dependency descriptors that pull in a coherent set of libraries for a capability — one dependency instead of dozens with matched versions. Examples: spring-boot-starter-web (MVC + Tomcat + Jackson), spring-boot-starter-data-jpa (Hibernate + JPA + JDBC), spring-boot-starter-security, spring-boot-starter-test. They eliminate version-conflict hell.

Externalized configuration — server port, datasource URL, logging levels, custom properties — without recompiling. YAML is more readable for nested/hierarchical config and supports multiple documents (profiles) in one file. Both are interchangeable; pick one convention per project.

server:
  port: 8081
spring:
  datasource:
    url: jdbc:postgresql://localhost/app

@Value("${app.timeout}") injects a single property — fine for one-offs. @ConfigurationProperties(prefix="app") binds a whole group of properties to a typed POJO/record — type-safe, supports validation, relaxed binding, and nested objects. Prefer @ConfigurationProperties for groups of related settings.

@ConfigurationProperties("app.mail")
record MailProps(String host, int port, Duration timeout) {}

Profiles let you define environment-specific beans and configuration (dev, test, prod). Activate with spring.profiles.active=prod (env var, JVM arg, or property). Use profile-specific files (application-prod.yml) and @Profile("prod") on beans so different implementations load per environment (e.g. in-memory DB for dev, real DB for prod).

  • singleton (default) — one instance per container.
  • prototype — a new instance every time it's requested.
  • request — one per HTTP request (web).
  • session — one per HTTP session.
  • application — one per ServletContext.

Critical: singleton beans must be stateless/thread-safe since one instance serves all requests.

Boot bundles an embedded servlet container so the app runs as a self-contained JAR with no external server install. Default is Tomcat; you can switch by excluding it and adding spring-boot-starter-jetty or -undertow. For reactive (WebFlux), the default is Netty. The embedded server starts when the application context boots.

A module exposing production-ready operational endpoints over HTTP/JMX: /actuator/health, /metrics, /info, /env, /loggers, /threaddump, /heapdump. It enables monitoring, health checks (for Kubernetes liveness/readiness probes), and runtime introspection. Endpoints are configurable and securable — expose only what's needed in production.

Use @RestControllerAdvice with @ExceptionHandler methods to centralize error handling and return consistent error responses.

@RestControllerAdvice
class GlobalExceptionHandler {
    @ExceptionHandler(EntityNotFoundException.class)
    @ResponseStatus(HttpStatus.NOT_FOUND)
    ErrorResponse handle(EntityNotFoundException e) {
        return new ErrorResponse("NOT_FOUND", e.getMessage());
    }
}

Spring 6 also offers ProblemDetail (RFC 7807) for standardized error bodies.

Use Bean Validation (Jakarta Validation) annotations on the DTO and @Valid on the parameter; Spring throws MethodArgumentNotValidException on failure (handle it in the advice for a 400).

record CreateUser(@NotBlank String name, @Email String email, @Min(18) int age) {}

@PostMapping("/users")
User create(@Valid @RequestBody CreateUser req) {...}
  • 2xx: 200 OK, 201 Created (with Location header), 204 No Content (delete).
  • 4xx (client): 400 Bad Request (validation), 401 Unauthorized, 403 Forbidden, 404 Not Found, 409 Conflict, 422 Unprocessable Entity, 429 Too Many Requests.
  • 5xx (server): 500 Internal Server Error, 502/503/504 (gateway/unavailable/timeout).

Set them via ResponseEntity or @ResponseStatus.

ResponseEntity<T> represents the full HTTP response — status, headers, and body — giving fine-grained control beyond returning a plain object.

@PostMapping("/users")
ResponseEntity<User> create(@RequestBody User u) {
    User saved = service.save(u);
    return ResponseEntity.created(URI.create("/users/" + saved.id())).body(saved);
}

Use it when you must set status/headers dynamically; for simple 200-with-body, returning the object suffices.

Via Jackson (auto-configured with spring-boot-starter-web). HttpMessageConverters convert return values to JSON and request bodies to objects. Customize with annotations (@JsonProperty, @JsonIgnore, @JsonFormat) or a custom ObjectMapper bean / Jackson2ObjectMapperBuilderCustomizer.

Cross-Origin Resource Sharing — the browser policy controlling whether a page from origin A may call your API at origin B. Enable per-controller with @CrossOrigin, or globally via a WebMvcConfigurer overriding addCorsMappings. With Spring Security, configure CORS there too (security filter runs first). Never use a wildcard * with credentials in production.

@PathVariable extracts a value from the URI template (part of the resource path): /users/{id} → identifies a resource. @RequestParam reads query-string or form parameters: /users?active=true&page=2 → filtering/pagination/optional inputs. Rule of thumb: path variables identify what, request params modify how.

mvn package (with the Spring Boot Maven plugin) produces an executable fat/uber JAR containing your code, all dependencies, and an embedded server — run with java -jar app.jar. Boot uses a custom layout (BOOT-INF/) and a launcher (JarLauncher) to load nested JARs. This single artifact is ideal for containers and cloud deployment.

Functional interfaces for running code once after the application context is fully started — for seeding data, warming caches, or startup checks. CommandLineRunner gives raw String[] args; ApplicationRunner gives parsed ApplicationArguments. Multiple runners execute in @Order sequence.

@Bean
CommandLineRunner seed(UserRepo repo) {
    return args -> repo.save(new User("admin"));
}

Boot uses SLF4J as the facade with Logback as the default implementation. Configure levels in properties (logging.level.com.app=DEBUG), output patterns, and file logging without touching XML. You can swap to Log4j2 via the starter. Use @Slf4j (Lombok) or LoggerFactory.getLogger to log.

@EnableAutoConfiguration loads auto-configuration classes listed in META-INF/spring/org.springframework.boot.autoconfigure.AutoConfiguration.imports (was spring.factories pre-2.7). Each class is gated by conditional annotations — they apply only when conditions hold. Example: if DataSource is on the classpath and none is user-defined, DataSourceAutoConfiguration creates one. Auto-config runs last, so your beans always override defaults (@ConditionalOnMissingBean).

  • @ConditionalOnClass / @ConditionalOnMissingClass — classpath presence.
  • @ConditionalOnBean / @ConditionalOnMissingBean — bean presence (enables user override).
  • @ConditionalOnProperty — a property value.
  • @ConditionalOnWebApplication — servlet vs reactive vs none.
  • @ConditionalOnExpression — SpEL.

These power the "configure only if it makes sense" logic behind auto-configuration. Build your own with @Conditional(MyCondition.class).

Run with --debug (or debug=true) to print the Conditions Evaluation Report: which auto-configurations matched (positive matches), which didn't and why (negative matches). Alternatively, the Actuator /actuator/conditions endpoint shows the same. This is the go-to for "why isn't my bean being created" or "why is Spring configuring something I don't want."

@SpringBootApplication(exclude = DataSourceAutoConfiguration.class)
// or in properties:
// spring.autoconfigure.exclude=org.springframework...DataSourceAutoConfiguration

Common when you want to configure something manually, or you have the JPA starter but connect to the DB yourself, avoiding Boot's attempt to auto-create a DataSource (which otherwise fails at startup with "Failed to configure a DataSource").

A → B and B → A. Spring can resolve setter/field circular deps via its three-level cache (early references), but constructor circular deps fail at startup (BeanCurrentlyInCreationException). Fixes, best first: redesign to remove the cycle (extract a third bean / rethink responsibilities); use @Lazy on one injection point; use setter injection; or as a last resort, ApplicationEventPublisher. Since Boot 2.6, circular references are prohibited by default — a signal to fix the design.

Spring throws NoUniqueBeanDefinitionException on ambiguous autowiring. Resolve with: @Primary on the default choice, or @Qualifier("beanName") at the injection point to pick explicitly. You can also inject a List<T> or Map<String,T> to get all of them (useful for Strategy registries).

@Autowired
public PaymentService(@Qualifier("stripe") PaymentGateway gateway) {...}
  1. Instantiation (constructor).
  2. Dependency injection (populate properties).
  3. *Aware callbacks (BeanNameAware, ApplicationContextAware).
  4. BeanPostProcessor.postProcessBeforeInitialization.
  5. @PostConstruct → InitializingBean.afterPropertiesSet → custom init-method.
  6. BeanPostProcessor.postProcessAfterInitialization (where AOP proxies are created).
  7. Bean is ready/in use.
  8. On shutdown: @PreDestroy → DisposableBean.destroy → custom destroy-method.

A hook invoked on every bean during initialization, with two callbacks (before/after init). It can inspect, modify, or wrap beans in proxies — this is how Spring implements AOP, @Autowired (AutowiredAnnotationBeanPostProcessor), @Transactional, validation, etc. Custom BeanPostProcessors let you apply cross-cutting setup to many beans. Note: they themselves are instantiated very early, before most beans.

@Component (and stereotypes) marks your own class for component scanning — you control the class. @Bean is a method in a @Configuration class that returns a bean — use it for third-party classes you can't annotate, or when construction needs custom logic/conditional wiring. @Bean methods give you programmatic control; @Component is declarative auto-detection.

@Configuration classes are proxied (CGLIB) so that calling one @Bean method from another returns the same singleton instance (full mode). In a plain @Component with @Bean methods ("lite mode"), inter-method calls create new instances each time (no proxy), which can silently produce duplicate beans. Always use @Configuration for classes whose @Bean methods call each other.

Spring wraps the bean in a proxy that, around each @Transactional method, begins a transaction, commits on normal return, and rolls back on (by default) unchecked exceptions. Default behavior gotchas: checked exceptions do NOT roll back unless rollbackFor is set; the proxy only intercepts external calls — self-invocation (this.txMethod()) bypasses it; the method must be public. Configure propagation, isolation, readOnly, and timeout via attributes.

  • REQUIRED (default) — join existing tx, or create one.
  • REQUIRES_NEW — always start a new tx, suspending any current one (independent commit — useful for audit logs that must persist even if the outer tx rolls back).
  • NESTED — savepoint within the current tx (partial rollback).
  • SUPPORTS — join if exists, else non-transactional.
  • MANDATORY — must run within an existing tx (else error).
  • NOT_SUPPORTED / NEVER — suspend / forbid transactions.

The transaction is applied by a proxy wrapping the bean. When method A calls this.B() internally, the call goes directly to the target object, not through the proxy — so B's @Transactional is ignored. Private methods can't be proxied at all (not visible/overridable). Fixes: move the transactional method to another bean (so the call crosses a proxy boundary), self-inject the proxy, or use AspectJ weaving (no proxy limitation).

Unit test: plain JUnit + Mockito, no Spring context — fast, test one class with mocked collaborators. Integration test: @SpringBootTest loads the (or a slice of the) application context — slower, tests wiring and real interactions.

@ExtendWith(MockitoExtension.class)   // unit
class ServiceTest { @Mock Repo repo; @InjectMocks Service svc; }

@SpringBootTest                       // integration
class AppIT { @Autowired Service svc; }

Slices load only the relevant part of the context for faster, focused tests. @WebMvcTest loads only the web layer (controllers, MockMvc) — mock the services with @MockBean. @DataJpaTest loads only JPA/repositories with an embedded DB and rolls back each test. @JsonTest, @RestClientTest exist too. They avoid the cost of @SpringBootTest's full context when you only need one layer.

@Mock (Mockito) creates a standalone mock with no Spring involvement. @MockBean (Spring Boot) creates a Mockito mock and replaces the matching bean in the application context — so injected dependencies in the context use your mock. Use @MockBean in slice/integration tests to stub a layer (e.g. mock the service in a @WebMvcTest). Note: it resets the context cache, which can slow tests if overused.

@WebMvcTest(UserController.class)
class UserControllerTest {
    @Autowired MockMvc mvc;
    @MockBean UserService service;

    @Test void getsUser() throws Exception {
        when(service.find(1L)).thenReturn(new User(1L, "Asha"));
        mvc.perform(get("/users/1"))
           .andExpect(status().isOk())
           .andExpect(jsonPath("$.name").value("Asha"));
    }
}

MockMvc tests the web layer without starting a real server. For full-stack tests, use @SpringBootTest(webEnvironment=RANDOM_PORT) + TestRestTemplate/WebTestClient.

  • MOCK (default) — mock servlet environment, use with MockMvc; no real port.
  • RANDOM_PORT — starts the embedded server on a random free port (real HTTP, parallel-safe).
  • DEFINED_PORT — uses the configured port (risk of conflicts).
  • NONE — no web environment (for non-web tests).

RANDOM_PORT + TestRestTemplate/WebTestClient = true end-to-end test.

Most via properties: server.port, server.tomcat.threads.max, server.tomcat.connection-timeout, server.compression.enabled. For programmatic control, implement WebServerFactoryCustomizer<TomcatServletWebServerFactory>. server.port=0 picks a random free port (useful in tests).

@Component
class PaymentGatewayHealth implements HealthIndicator {
    public Health health() {
        return gateway.ping()
            ? Health.up().withDetail("latencyMs", 12).build()
            : Health.down().withDetail("error", "unreachable").build();
    }
}

It auto-registers and contributes to /actuator/health. Kubernetes uses liveness/readiness groups (management.endpoint.health.group.readiness) to gate traffic.

Micrometer is a vendor-neutral metrics facade (think SLF4J for metrics) built into Boot Actuator. You record counters, gauges, timers, and distribution summaries; Micrometer exports to Prometheus, Datadog, CloudWatch, etc. via a registry dependency.

meterRegistry.counter("orders.placed", "type", "online").increment();
Timer.Sample s = Timer.start(registry); /* ... */ s.stop(registry.timer("db.query"));

Add @Timed to methods or use the Prometheus endpoint /actuator/prometheus.

Spring Boot has a defined order (highest wins): command-line args → SPRING_APPLICATION_JSON → OS environment variables → application-{profile}.properties → application.properties → @PropertySource → defaults. This layering lets you set defaults in files and override per-environment via env vars (the 12-factor approach) without rebuilding. Relaxed binding maps APP_MAIL_HOST env var to app.mail.host.

It applies cross-controller logic via three mechanisms: @ExceptionHandler (global error handling), @ModelAttribute (add common model data to all controllers), and @InitBinder (register custom property editors/validators globally). You can scope it to specific packages/annotations/types via @ControllerAdvice(basePackages=...). @RestControllerAdvice adds @ResponseBody for REST.

Enable with @EnableAsync and annotate methods with @Async; they run on a task executor and return void, Future, or CompletableFuture. Configure a dedicated ThreadPoolTaskExecutor — the default SimpleAsyncTaskExecutor creates a new thread per call (no pooling, dangerous). Same proxy caveats as @Transactional: no self-invocation, public methods only. For scheduling, use @EnableScheduling + @Scheduled.

Expose only what's needed: management.endpoints.web.exposure.include=health,info,prometheus. Run management on a separate port (management.server.port) not exposed publicly, and/or secure endpoints with Spring Security (require an admin role). Mask sensitive values, disable /env, /heapdump, /shutdown externally. Health details can be hidden for unauthenticated users (management.endpoint.health.show-details=when-authorized).

  • URI versioning (/api/v1/users) — explicit, cache-friendly, most common; but pollutes URIs.
  • Header versioning (Accept: application/vnd.app.v2+json) — clean URIs, RESTful; harder to test/cache.
  • Query param (?version=2) — simple but un-RESTful.

Pragmatic MNC answer: URI versioning for public APIs (clarity), and minimize breaking changes (additive evolution) so you rarely bump versions. Maintain old versions during a deprecation window.

Accept page, size, sort params and use Spring Data's Pageable; return a Page with content + total count + metadata.

@GetMapping("/users")
Page<UserDto> list(@PageableDefault(size=20, sort="createdAt") Pageable pageable) {
    return repo.findAll(pageable).map(mapper::toDto);
}
// GET /users?page=0&size=20&sort=name,asc

For large datasets, prefer keyset (cursor) pagination over offset — offset gets slow on deep pages and is unstable under concurrent inserts.

Hypermedia As The Engine Of Application State — responses include links to related actions/resources, so clients navigate the API dynamically rather than hardcoding URIs (REST maturity level 3). Spring HATEOAS provides EntityModel/RepresentationModel. Honest take: powerful in theory but adds complexity; most internal/microservice APIs skip it. Worth it for long-lived public APIs where decoupling clients from URI structure pays off.

GET, PUT, DELETE, HEAD are idempotent by spec (same effect if repeated); POST and PATCH generally aren't. To make POST retry-safe (network retries, double-clicks), require an Idempotency-Key header: store the key with its result; on a duplicate key, return the cached response instead of re-creating. This prevents duplicate orders/charges — essential for payment and order APIs.

@PostMapping(value="/upload", consumes=MULTIPART_FORM_DATA_VALUE)
String upload(@RequestParam MultipartFile file) throws IOException {
    file.transferTo(targetPath);  return file.getOriginalFilename();
}
@GetMapping("/download/{id}")
ResponseEntity<Resource> download(@PathVariable Long id) {
    return ResponseEntity.ok()
        .header(CONTENT_DISPOSITION, "attachment; filename=\"x.pdf\"")
        .body(new FileSystemResource(path));
}

Tune spring.servlet.multipart.max-file-size. For large files, stream via StreamingResponseBody to avoid loading into memory.

Servlet Filters run at the container level, before and outside Spring MVC — they see the raw request/response, ideal for encoding, CORS, security, request logging, and can wrap the request. HandlerInterceptors run inside Spring MVC with knowledge of the matched handler and model (preHandle/postHandle/afterCompletion) — ideal for app-level concerns like auth checks and timing. Filters are broader; interceptors are MVC-aware.

The spring-boot-dependencies BOM (Bill of Materials) declares tested-compatible versions for hundreds of libraries. Inheriting spring-boot-starter-parent (or importing the BOM) means you add dependencies without version numbers — Boot supplies them. This eliminates version conflicts. Override a managed version by setting a property (e.g. <jackson.version>) — but do so carefully, as Boot tested specific combinations.

Write a @AutoConfiguration class with @Conditional guards and @ConfigurationProperties, list it in META-INF/spring/org.springframework.boot.autoconfigure.AutoConfiguration.imports, and package it as a library. Convention: name it xyz-spring-boot-starter. Consumers just add the dependency and your beans auto-configure (overridable via @ConditionalOnMissingBean). This is how you share cross-team infrastructure (common security, clients, observability) consistently.

Never commit secrets. Options: environment variables / Kubernetes Secrets, a secrets manager (HashiCorp Vault, AWS Secrets Manager) via Spring Cloud Vault/Config, or encrypted config with Jasypt. Spring Cloud Config Server centralizes config with encryption. For local dev, use .env / profile files that are git-ignored. Principle: config in the environment (12-factor), secrets in a vault, code stays clean.

A centralized configuration server that serves environment/profile-specific config (backed by Git) to many microservices, supporting encryption and dynamic refresh (@RefreshScope + /actuator/refresh or Spring Cloud Bus). You need it when dozens of services share config and you want a single source of truth with auditing and live updates — rather than baking config into each image. For a few services, environment variables/K8s ConfigMaps may suffice.

@EnableCaching   // on a config class
@Cacheable(value="users", key="#id")
public User findUser(Long id) {...}          // result cached by id
@CacheEvict(value="users", key="#user.id")
public void update(User user) {...}          // invalidate on change
@CachePut(value="users", key="#user.id")
public User refresh(User user) {...}         // always run + update cache

Backed by a CacheManager (ConcurrentMap default, or Caffeine/Redis). Caveats: proxy-based (no self-invocation), and choose keys carefully. Use Redis for distributed caching across instances.

HikariCP is Boot's default — fastest, lightest, most reliable pool. Configure via spring.datasource.hikari.*: maximum-pool-size, minimum-idle, connection-timeout, max-lifetime, idle-timeout. Critical sizing rule: pool size should match DB capacity, not app threads — an oversized pool overwhelms the database. A common formula starts around cores × 2; measure under load. Set max-lifetime below the DB's connection timeout.

Use a filter to generate/propagate a correlation ID (from an incoming X-Request-ID header or new UUID), put it in SLF4J's MDC, include it in the log pattern, and forward it on downstream calls — so one request's logs are traceable across services. For full distributed tracing, use Micrometer Tracing (Spring Boot 3) with OpenTelemetry/Zipkin, which auto-propagates trace/span IDs. Clear the MDC in a finally block to avoid leakage across pooled threads.

  • Java 17 baseline (minimum).
  • javax → jakarta namespace migration (Jakarta EE 9+) — the biggest breaking change.
  • Spring Framework 6 under the hood.
  • GraalVM native image support (AOT) for fast startup / low memory.
  • Observability: unified Micrometer Observation API + Micrometer Tracing replacing Spring Cloud Sleuth.
  • ProblemDetail (RFC 7807) for errors.

Boot 3 can compile to a native executable via GraalVM AOT — millisecond startup, low memory footprint, ideal for serverless/scale-to-zero and many small instances. Trade-offs: closed-world assumption breaks runtime reflection/dynamic proxies (needs hints/AOT processing), slower build, no JIT peak throughput (no profile-guided optimization), and harder debugging. Use it where startup/memory matter more than peak throughput; otherwise the JVM remains the safer choice.

Enable server.shutdown=graceful and set spring.lifecycle.timeout-per-shutdown-phase. On SIGTERM, Boot stops accepting new requests and lets in-flight requests finish (up to the timeout) before closing. Combine with Kubernetes preStop hooks and readiness probe flipping to "down" first, so the load balancer drains traffic before shutdown. Use @PreDestroy to release resources (close pools, flush buffers).

Return CompletableFuture, Callable, or DeferredResult from a controller — the servlet thread is released while the work runs on another thread, improving throughput under load. For streaming, use StreamingResponseBody or Server-Sent Events (SseEmitter). For fully reactive non-blocking I/O, use WebFlux. On Java 21, virtual threads (spring.threads.virtual.enabled=true) make blocking controllers scale without rewriting to reactive.

MVC (servlet, thread-per-request, blocking): simpler, mature, great for typical CRUD/DB apps; the default choice. WebFlux (reactive, event-loop, non-blocking, Netty): scales to massive concurrency with few threads, ideal for high-throughput I/O-bound services, streaming, and when the whole stack is reactive — but it has a steep learning curve, harder debugging, and only helps if downstream is non-blocking too. With Java 21 virtual threads, MVC handles high concurrency well, narrowing WebFlux's niche to true streaming/backpressure.

  • RestTemplate — classic synchronous client, in maintenance mode (no new features).
  • WebClient — reactive, non-blocking, also usable in blocking mode (.block()); the recommended choice pre-Boot-3.2 for both styles.
  • RestClient (Boot 3.2+) — modern synchronous fluent client, RestTemplate's successor with WebClient-style API.

Always set connect/read timeouts and wrap with resilience (retry, circuit breaker).

Use Resilience4j (the standard since Hystrix's retirement) with declarative annotations or programmatic decorators:

@Retry(name="inventory", fallbackMethod="fallback")
@CircuitBreaker(name="inventory", fallbackMethod="fallback")
@RateLimiter(name="inventory")
public Stock check(String sku) {...}
public Stock fallback(String sku, Throwable t) { return Stock.unknown(); }

Combine retry (with backoff + jitter), circuit breaker, bulkhead, rate limiter, and time limiter. Only retry idempotent operations. Spring Retry (@Retryable) is a lighter alternative for retry-only needs.

Use @RequestScope (or @Scope("request")). The catch: injecting a request-scoped bean into a singleton fails because the singleton is created once at startup when no request exists. Spring solves this with a scoped proxy (proxyMode = TARGET_CLASS) — it injects a proxy that resolves the real bean from the current request at call time. Same pattern applies to session scope.

@Lazy defers bean creation until first use (instead of at startup). Valid uses: breaking a circular dependency (lazy-inject one side), speeding up startup for rarely-used expensive beans, and avoiding eager initialization of optional integrations. Caveat: lazy beans hide initialization errors until runtime and can cause first-call latency. Don't make everything lazy — fail-fast startup is usually preferable.

// publish
publisher.publishEvent(new OrderPlacedEvent(order));
// listen (sync by default; @Async for async)
@EventListener
void onOrder(OrderPlacedEvent e) { email.confirm(e.order()); }
// run only after DB commit
@TransactionalEventListener(phase = AFTER_COMMIT)
void afterCommit(OrderPlacedEvent e) {...}

Events decouple modules (in-process Observer/Pub-Sub). @TransactionalEventListener is key for "do X only if the transaction committed" (e.g. send email after the order is actually saved).

All run initialization logic after dependency injection, in this order: @PostConstruct (JSR-250, preferred — annotation-based, decoupled from Spring) → InitializingBean.afterPropertiesSet() (couples your class to Spring) → @Bean(initMethod="...") (for third-party classes you can't annotate). Prefer @PostConstruct for your own beans; use initMethod for external classes.

  • Trim dependencies/starters; exclude unused auto-configurations.
  • @Lazy initialization for non-critical beans (spring.main.lazy-initialization=true with care).
  • Reduce component-scan scope (specific base packages).
  • Use AOT processing / GraalVM native image for drastic cold-start wins.
  • Profile with the startup actuator endpoint / BufferingApplicationStartup to find slow beans.
  • Class Data Sharing (CDS) to speed class loading.
  1. Create SpringApplication; deduce web application type (servlet/reactive/none) from classpath.
  2. Load ApplicationContextInitializers and ApplicationListeners from spring.factories.
  3. Prepare the Environment (property sources, profiles); fire ApplicationEnvironmentPreparedEvent.
  4. Print banner; create the appropriate ApplicationContext.
  5. Load bean definitions (component scan, configs).
  6. refresh() the context: instantiate singletons, run BeanPostProcessors, apply auto-configuration, start the embedded server.
  7. Fire ApplicationReadyEvent; run CommandLineRunner/ApplicationRunner beans.

Cause: transactions are applied by a proxy; an internal this.method() call doesn't pass through the proxy, so the inner method's transaction attributes (e.g. REQUIRES_NEW) are never applied. Fixes: (1) extract the inner method into a separate bean so the call crosses a proxy boundary; (2) self-inject the proxy (@Autowired private MyService self;) and call self.method(); (3) switch from proxy-based to AspectJ load-time/compile-time weaving (@EnableTransactionManagement(mode=ASPECTJ)), which weaves into the bytecode and has no self-invocation limitation.

Both have a scoped-lifetime mismatch with the singleton (created once). Solutions: (1) Scoped proxy — annotate the narrower bean with proxyMode=TARGET_CLASS so a proxy resolves the correct instance per request; (2) ObjectProvider/Provider — inject ObjectProvider<T> and call getObject() on demand to fetch a fresh prototype instance each time; (3) Lookup method injection (@Lookup) for prototypes. Plain injection caches one instance forever — the classic "my prototype bean isn't new each time" bug.

Auto-configuration found a JDBC/JPA starter on the classpath but no datasource URL/driver was resolvable in the prod environment. Checklist: are spring.datasource.* properties set (env vars/secrets) in prod? Is the right profile active? Is the JDBC driver on the classpath? Network/firewall to the DB? Fixes: provide the datasource config via environment, or if the service genuinely needs no DB, exclude DataSourceAutoConfiguration. Use the conditions report (--debug) to confirm what triggered the auto-config.

Investigate: thread dump shows threads blocked on getConnection(); Hikari logs "connection is not available, request timed out"; check Micrometer pool metrics (active vs idle). Root causes: connections not returned (leak — missing close/transaction not committed), slow queries holding connections, pool too small, or a downstream call inside an open transaction. Fixes: fix leaks (ensure transactions close), enable Hikari leakDetectionThreshold, right-size the pool to DB capacity, shorten transactions (don't make remote calls inside them), add query timeouts. Bigger pool is rarely the real fix.

For void-returning @Async methods, the caller has no Future to observe, so exceptions are swallowed (logged only if an exception handler exists). Fixes: return CompletableFuture so the caller can handle failures (exceptionally/handle); or implement AsyncUncaughtExceptionHandler via AsyncConfigurer.getAsyncUncaughtExceptionHandler() for void methods. Also configure a real ThreadPoolTaskExecutor — the default creates unbounded threads. And remember @Async is proxy-based (no self-invocation).

If a class has a single constructor, Spring uses it for injection automatically — @Autowired is optional (since Spring 4.3). With multiple constructors, you must mark one with @Autowired (or provide a no-arg default). Spring resolves each parameter as a dependency, applying @Qualifier/@Primary/required semantics. This is why constructor injection on records and Lombok @RequiredArgsConstructor classes "just works" without annotations.

Auto-configurations back off when a user bean exists (@ConditionalOnMissingBean). So define your own bean (e.g. a customized ObjectMapper via Jackson2ObjectMapperBuilderCustomizer) to win deterministically. If two libraries both define non-conditional beans, use @Primary, exclude one auto-configuration, or order them. Use the conditions report to see which auto-config created the bean and why. Principle: explicit user beans always override auto-configuration.

Centralize via a configured client bean with interceptors/filters: for WebClient, an ExchangeFilterFunction (add auth header, log, propagate trace context); for RestClient/RestTemplate, a ClientHttpRequestInterceptor. Register the customization once on a shared builder bean so all clients inherit it. For metrics/tracing, Micrometer auto-instruments WebClient/RestClient when you use the auto-configured builder. This avoids sprinkling header logic at every call site.

Use Spring Cloud Config + @RefreshScope: beans annotated @RefreshScope are re-created on a refresh event (POST /actuator/refresh or Spring Cloud Bus broadcasting to all instances via a message broker). For Kubernetes, mounted ConfigMap changes can trigger reload via Spring Cloud Kubernetes. Caveat: @RefreshScope recreates beans (state loss, brief latency), and some config (server port, datasource pool) can't be hot-reloaded. Design for which properties are truly dynamic.

The endpoint serializes an entity graph, triggering a query per lazy association (1 + N). Detect it via SQL logging (spring.jpa.show-sql / Hibernate statistics / a tool like Hypersistence). Fixes: use JOIN FETCH or an @EntityGraph on the repository method to load associations in one query; project directly into DTOs (constructor projection) so only needed columns load; batch with @BatchSize/hibernate.default_batch_fetch_size; and never serialize lazy entities directly — map to DTOs. This bridges Boot + JPA performance.

  1. Require an Idempotency-Key header from the client.
  2. In a filter/interceptor or service, check a store (Redis/DB) for the key; if present, return the saved response.
  3. Otherwise process within a transaction, persist the order and the idempotency key+response atomically (unique constraint on key prevents races).
  4. Use the Transactional Outbox to publish the "OrderCreated" event in the same transaction.
  5. Return 201 with a Location header; on duplicate key, return the original 201/200.

This makes client retries and double-clicks safe without duplicate orders.

Load test with Gatling/JMeter/k6 against a staging replica; measure p50/p95/p99 latency and error rate, not just averages. Profile with low-overhead tools safe for prod: JDK Flight Recorder + Mission Control, async-profiler (CPU/alloc flamegraphs), and Micrometer/Prometheus dashboards. Watch GC logs, thread pool saturation, DB pool metrics, and downstream latencies. Establish a baseline, change one thing at a time, and validate against SLOs. Avoid heavyweight sampling profilers in prod.

Combine profiles and conditionals: @Profile("prod") on the real implementation, @Profile("dev") or @ConditionalOnMissingBean on an in-memory stub; gate optional features with @ConditionalOnProperty(name="feature.x.enabled", havingValue="true"). Bind the variant's settings via @ConfigurationProperties with validation. For complex selection, a @Configuration with @Bean methods returning the right implementation based on injected properties keeps the logic explicit and testable.

BeanFactory is the basic container with lazy bean instantiation (on first getBean). ApplicationContext extends it with enterprise features (events, i18n, AOP, environment, BeanPostProcessor auto-registration) and eagerly instantiates singletons at startup (fail-fast). Boot always uses an ApplicationContext. Eager init means wiring/config errors surface at startup, not at first request — generally desirable. @Lazy or spring.main.lazy-initialization can defer it.

Add Micrometer Tracing with an OpenTelemetry or Brave bridge plus an exporter (Zipkin/Tempo/Jaeger via OTLP). Boot auto-instruments incoming requests, WebClient/RestClient calls, and messaging, propagating trace and span IDs via W3C traceparent headers. The trace context flows into MDC (so logs carry traceId), and exported spans build an end-to-end timeline across services. Sample appropriately (e.g. 10%) to control overhead. This replaced Spring Cloud Sleuth.

  • Paginate — never return unbounded collections.
  • Project to lean DTOs — return only needed fields (avoid serializing whole entity graphs).
  • Stream large results with StreamingResponseBody or Jackson streaming, or NDJSON, instead of building one giant in-memory object.
  • Enable response compression (server.compression.enabled).
  • Tune Jackson (disable pretty-print in prod, use afterburner/blackbird module).
  • Consider field filtering (GraphQL or @JsonView) so clients fetch only what they need.

A @Configuration class is subclassed by CGLIB; calls between @Bean methods are intercepted to return the already-created singleton from the container (so repo() called from two @Bean methods yields the same instance). In lite mode (@Bean in a plain @Component or a class marked proxyBeanMethods=false), no proxy exists, so inter-method calls execute the method again → duplicate, unmanaged instances. The bite: a shared dependency silently becomes two objects. Use full @Configuration unless you deliberately want lite mode for startup speed (and then don't call @Bean methods from each other).

Resolve the tenant from the request (header/subdomain/JWT claim) in a filter, store it in a ThreadLocal (TenantContext), and use it to choose the datasource/schema (Hibernate multi-tenancy: DATABASE, SCHEMA, or DISCRIMINATOR strategies) and to scope queries. Pitfalls: ThreadLocal must be cleared in a finally (leakage across pooled threads), async/@Async loses the context (propagate it), connection pools per tenant can explode, and cross-tenant data leaks if any query forgets the tenant filter. With virtual threads, prefer ScopedValues; for async, use context-propagation.

Enable BufferingApplicationStartup and read the /actuator/startup endpoint (or use the startup step metrics) to get a timeline of each phase and bean's init time — pinpointing the slow beans. Cross-check the conditions report for unexpected auto-configurations doing work. Then act: lazy-init heavy beans, narrow component scanning, exclude unused auto-config, defer non-critical init to a runner, or adopt AOT/native. Measure before and after — don't guess.

  • Additive changes only within a version — add optional fields/endpoints; never remove or rename or change types.
  • Tolerant reader pattern: clients ignore unknown fields (FAIL_ON_UNKNOWN_PROPERTIES=false).
  • Default values for new required-ish fields server-side.
  • For breaking changes, introduce a new version and run both during a deprecation window (with Deprecation/Sunset headers).
  • Contract testing (Spring Cloud Contract/Pact) to catch breaks in CI.
  • Document and communicate the deprecation timeline.

Layer protections: (1) Timeouts on every outbound call (connect + read). (2) Bulkhead — dedicate a bounded thread pool/semaphore to that dependency so it can't starve the whole app. (3) Circuit breaker — fail fast when it's down, returning a fallback. (4) Retry with backoff + jitter, only for idempotent calls, capped. (5) Rate limiter to protect both sides. (6) Graceful degradation (cached/default response). (7) Monitor with metrics and alerts. Resilience4j composes all of these; the mindset is "isolate and fail fast, never block indefinitely."

AOP proxies are created by a BeanPostProcessor (AnnotationAwareAspectJAutoProxyCreator) in postProcessAfterInitialization — it wraps the fully-initialized bean. For interface-based beans it uses a JDK dynamic proxy; for concrete classes, CGLIB generates a subclass overriding methods to apply advice. Because CGLIB subclasses and overrides, final classes can't be proxied and final/private/static methods can't be advised (can't be overridden) — so @Transactional/@Cacheable on them silently does nothing. This also underlies the self-invocation limitation.

Use the Strangler Fig pattern behind an API gateway: route specific bounded contexts to new services while the monolith serves the rest, migrating feature by feature. Steps: identify seams via domain boundaries (DDD), extract the least-coupled context first, introduce an anti-corruption layer to translate models, use the database-per-service goal (start with shared DB, split tables gradually), adopt async events (Outbox) to decouple. Add observability/tracing early. Avoid the big-bang rewrite; deliver and validate value continuously, retiring monolith pieces as they're replaced.

  • Config: externalized (env/Config Server), secrets in a vault, profiles, typed @ConfigurationProperties with validation.
  • Resilience: timeouts everywhere, circuit breakers + bulkheads + retries (Resilience4j), graceful shutdown, idempotency.
  • Observability: Actuator health (liveness/readiness), Micrometer metrics → Prometheus, distributed tracing, structured logging with correlation IDs.
  • Security: Spring Security (OAuth2/JWT), HTTPS, input validation, secrets management, least-privilege Actuator exposure.
  • Performance: right-sized connection/thread pools, caching, pagination, DTO projections (no N+1), GC tuning.
  • Delivery: containerized (layered/native image), CI with contract + slice + integration tests, blue-green/canary, DB migrations (Flyway/Liquibase).

💾 Spring Data & JPA — Top 100

ℹ️ JPA/Hibernate, repositories, queries, transactions, performance (N+1, fetching, locking), and internals.

JPA is the specification (interfaces/annotations for ORM). Hibernate is the most common JPA implementation (provider). Spring Data JPA sits on top, removing boilerplate — you declare repository interfaces and it generates implementations at runtime. Stack: your code → Spring Data JPA → JPA API → Hibernate → JDBC → database.

@Entity
@Table(name = "users")
public class User {
    @Id @GeneratedValue(strategy = GenerationType.IDENTITY)
    private Long id;
    @Column(nullable = false, length = 100)
    private String name;
}

An entity maps to a table; requires @Entity, an @Id, and a no-arg constructor. @Table, @Column customize mapping. Each instance maps to a row.

  • Repository<T,ID> — marker (no methods).
  • CrudRepository — save, findById, findAll, delete, count.
  • PagingAndSortingRepository — adds paging/sorting.
  • JpaRepository — adds JPA specifics (flush, saveAndFlush, batch delete, getReferenceById); the one most people extend.

Spring Data parses the method name into a query at startup.

List<User> findByLastNameAndActiveTrue(String lastName);
List<User> findByAgeGreaterThanOrderByNameAsc(int age);
Optional<User> findByEmail(String email);
long countByActive(boolean active);
boolean existsByEmail(String email);

Keywords: And, Or, Between, LessThan, Like, In, IgnoreCase, OrderBy, True/False. If the name doesn't parse, you get a startup error.

@Query("SELECT u FROM User u WHERE u.email = :email")
Optional<User> findByEmail(@Param("email") String email);

@Query(value = "SELECT * FROM users WHERE age > ?1", nativeQuery = true)
List<User> olderThan(int age);

Use it for queries too complex for method names. JPQL (object-oriented) by default; nativeQuery=true for raw SQL.

JPQL queries entities (not tables) and is database-portable — prefer it for normal queries. Native SQL uses real table/column names and DB-specific features (window functions, hints, vendor functions) — use when JPQL can't express it or for performance-critical/complex reporting queries. Trade-off: native loses portability and entity mapping niceties.

It defines a transaction boundary — the methods inside run in one DB transaction, committing on success and rolling back on (unchecked) exceptions. Spring Data repository methods are already transactional individually; you put @Transactional on the service layer to group multiple repository calls into one atomic unit of work (and to keep the persistence context open for lazy loading).

  • Transient — new object, not associated with persistence context, no DB row.
  • Managed (persistent) — associated with the context; changes are tracked and auto-flushed.
  • Detached — was managed, but the context closed; changes no longer tracked.
  • Removed — scheduled for deletion.

persist, merge, remove, detach transition between states.

persist() (JPA) makes a transient entity managed; throws if it already has an ID/exists. merge() copies a detached entity's state into a managed instance and returns that managed copy (the argument stays detached). Spring Data's save() is convenience: it calls persist() for new entities (null/unset ID) and merge() for existing ones — and you should use its return value.

EAGER loads the association immediately with the parent. LAZY loads it only when first accessed (via a proxy). Defaults: @OneToMany/@ManyToMany are LAZY; @ManyToOne/@OneToOne are EAGER. Best practice: make everything LAZY and fetch explicitly when needed (JOIN FETCH/entity graph) — EAGER causes hidden joins and the N+1 problem.

@OneToOne, @OneToMany, @ManyToOne, @ManyToMany. The many side typically owns the foreign key. Use mappedBy on the inverse side to avoid a duplicate join, and @JoinColumn/@JoinTable to control the FK/join table.

@ManyToOne @JoinColumn(name = "dept_id")
private Department department;
@OneToMany(mappedBy = "department")
private List<Employee> employees;

Cascade propagates operations from a parent entity to its associations. Types: PERSIST, MERGE, REMOVE, REFRESH, DETACH, ALL. Example: cascade = CascadeType.ALL on an order's items means saving/deleting the order saves/deletes its items. Use carefully — REMOVE/ALL can delete more than intended; never cascade REMOVE on shared references.

orphanRemoval=true deletes a child when it's removed from the parent's collection (the parent-child link is severed) — e.g. order.getItems().remove(item) deletes that item. Cascade REMOVE only deletes children when the parent itself is deleted. Orphan removal models true parent-owns-child composition; use it for items that can't exist without their parent.

  • IDENTITY — DB auto-increment column; simple but disables JDBC batch inserts (ID known only after insert).
  • SEQUENCE — DB sequence; allows batching and pre-fetching; preferred for Postgres/Oracle.
  • TABLE — emulates a sequence via a table; portable but slow (avoid).
  • AUTO — provider chooses (SEQUENCE on most modern DBs).

SEQUENCE with an allocation size is generally best for performance.

Page<User> page = repo.findByActive(true,
    PageRequest.of(0, 20, Sort.by("name").ascending()));
page.getContent(); page.getTotalElements(); page.getTotalPages();

Return Page (includes total count — extra COUNT query) or Slice (knows only if there's a next page — no COUNT, cheaper). Controllers can accept Pageable directly.

findById hits the DB and returns an Optional (fully loaded entity or empty). getReferenceById returns a lazy proxy without a DB query — useful when you only need the reference to set a foreign key (e.g. order.setUser(repo.getReferenceById(id))) and avoid loading the whole entity. Accessing the proxy's fields later triggers a query (or EntityNotFoundException if it doesn't exist).

@Modifying
@Query("UPDATE User u SET u.active = false WHERE u.lastLogin < :cutoff")
int deactivateInactive(@Param("cutoff") LocalDate cutoff);

Required for UPDATE/DELETE/INSERT JPQL. Returns affected row count. Caveat: bulk modifying queries bypass the persistence context — the first-level cache may hold stale entities, so add clearAutomatically=true / flushAutomatically=true or clear the context.

Optional<T> (single, may be absent — preferred over null), T (single or null), List<T>/Set<T>, Page<T>/Slice<T>, Stream<T> (must close it), long/boolean (count/exists), and projections (interfaces/DTOs). For single-result queries returning more than one row, you get NonUniqueResultException.

spring.jpa.show-sql=true
spring.jpa.properties.hibernate.format_sql=true
logging.level.org.hibernate.SQL=DEBUG
logging.level.org.hibernate.orm.jdbc.bind=TRACE   # see bound parameters

Essential for spotting N+1 problems and verifying that JOIN FETCH/projections behave as expected. For production diagnostics, use Hibernate statistics or datasource-proxy instead of always-on logging.

CrudRepository provides basic CRUD and is store-agnostic (works for any Spring Data module). JpaRepository extends it (via PagingAndSortingRepository) with JPA-specific methods: flush(), saveAndFlush(), deleteAllInBatch(), getReferenceById(), and returns List instead of Iterable for findAll. Use JpaRepository for JPA-backed apps; CrudRepository if you want a leaner, portable contract.

@EnableJpaAuditing   // on a config class
@EntityListeners(AuditingEntityListener.class)
class BaseEntity {
    @CreatedDate     LocalDateTime createdAt;
    @LastModifiedDate LocalDateTime updatedAt;
    @CreatedBy       String createdBy;   // needs AuditorAware bean
}

Spring auto-populates these on insert/update — no manual timestamp code, consistent audit columns across entities.

@Embeddable marks a value object (e.g. Address) whose fields are stored inline in the owner's table (no separate table/identity). The owner uses @Embedded. It models composition and value objects cleanly.

@Embeddable record Address(String street, String city, String zip) {}
@Entity class Customer { @Embedded Address address; }
  • SINGLE_TABLE (default) — all subclasses in one table with a discriminator column; fast, but nullable columns for subclass-specific fields.
  • JOINED — a table per class joined by FK; normalized, no nulls, but joins on read.
  • TABLE_PER_CLASS — a full table per concrete class; avoids joins but unions on polymorphic queries.

SINGLE_TABLE is usually the pragmatic default.

It enables optimistic locking: a version column (int/timestamp) is incremented on each update; if two transactions read the same version and both try to update, the second's UPDATE (with WHERE version = old) affects 0 rows, and Hibernate throws OptimisticLockException. This prevents lost updates without holding DB locks — ideal for low-contention, read-heavy scenarios.

Returning a subset of fields instead of the full entity — for performance and clean APIs.

interface UserView { String getName(); String getEmail(); }   // interface projection
List<UserView> findByActiveTrue();

// DTO/class projection (constructor)
@Query("SELECT new com.app.UserDto(u.name, u.email) FROM User u")
List<UserDto> findAllDtos();

Closed projections fetch only the needed columns — a key N+1 and over-fetch remedy.

Loading N parent rows then firing one extra query per parent to load a lazy association = 1 + N queries. Detect: enable SQL logging / Hibernate statistics — you'll see the same query repeated. Fix:

  • JOIN FETCH in JPQL or an @EntityGraph to load associations in one query.
  • DTO projections (fetch only needed columns).
  • @BatchSize / hibernate.default_batch_fetch_size to load associations in batches (1 + N/batch).

The single most common JPA performance bug in interviews and production.

SELECT u FROM User u JOIN FETCH u.roles loads the association eagerly in one query. Pitfall: fetch-joining a collection with pagination causes Hibernate to fetch all rows into memory and paginate in Java (warning: "firstResult/maxResults specified with collection fetch; applying in memory") — disastrous for large datasets. Fix: paginate IDs first, then fetch the collection for those IDs (two-query approach), or use @BatchSize / entity graph with batch fetching.

@EntityGraph(attributePaths = {"roles", "department"})
@Query("SELECT u FROM User u WHERE u.active = true")
List<User> findActiveWithRoles();

A declarative way to specify which associations to fetch eagerly for a specific query — overriding the default LAZY without writing JOIN FETCH. It solves N+1 per use-case (entity stays LAZY by default; you opt in to fetching only where needed).

Accessing a LAZY association after the persistence context (session) has closed — typically when serializing an entity in the controller after the transaction ended. The proxy can't load data without an open session. Fixes: fetch the association within the transaction (JOIN FETCH / entity graph / DTO mapping inside the service), or map to a DTO before returning. Anti-fix: Open-Session-In-View (keeps session open through view rendering) — it hides the problem and harms performance; prefer explicit fetching.

The EntityManager's working memory for one transaction. It guarantees identity (one managed instance per entity ID — repeated findById returns the same object, no second query), tracks changes for dirty checking (auto-UPDATE on flush), and batches writes. It's always on (you can't disable it) and is cleared when the context closes. Bulk JPQL updates bypass it, causing staleness.

Dirty checking: Hibernate snapshots managed entities; at flush, it compares current state to the snapshot and auto-generates UPDATEs for changed fields — you never call update(), just mutate the managed entity inside a transaction. Flush synchronizes the persistence context to the DB (sends pending SQL). Flush triggers: before query execution (to ensure consistency), before commit, or explicit flush(). FlushMode AUTO is default; understanding flush timing explains "why did my SQL run there".

Optimistic (@Version): assume conflicts are rare; detect them at commit (version mismatch → retry). No DB locks held → high concurrency. Use for read-heavy, low-contention data (most web apps). Pessimistic (SELECT ... FOR UPDATE via @Lock(PESSIMISTIC_WRITE)): lock the row up front so others wait. Use for high-contention, must-not-fail operations (inventory decrement, financial balances) where retrying optimistically would thrash. Pessimistic risks deadlocks and reduces throughput.

@Lock(LockModeType.PESSIMISTIC_WRITE)
@Query("SELECT a FROM Account a WHERE a.id = :id")
Optional<Account> findByIdForUpdate(@Param("id") Long id);
// Optionally set a timeout:
@QueryHints(@QueryHint(name = "jakarta.persistence.lock.timeout", value = "3000"))

Must run inside a transaction. The DB issues SELECT ... FOR UPDATE, blocking other writers until commit. Always set a lock timeout to avoid indefinite waits/deadlocks.

LevelPrevents
READ_UNCOMMITTEDnothing (allows dirty reads)
READ_COMMITTEDdirty reads
REPEATABLE_READ+ non-repeatable reads
SERIALIZABLE+ phantom reads (full isolation)

Higher isolation = more consistency, less concurrency. Most apps use READ_COMMITTED (Postgres/Oracle default) or REPEATABLE_READ (MySQL default). Set via @Transactional(isolation=...).

saveAll just loops save — it does NOT automatically batch at the JDBC level. For true batch inserts you must enable hibernate.jdbc.batch_size, order_inserts, order_updates, and crucially avoid GenerationType.IDENTITY (it disables batching because each ID needs an immediate insert) — use SEQUENCE with allocationSize. Then Hibernate groups inserts into batched JDBC statements, dramatically faster for bulk loads.

An optional, SessionFactory-wide cache shared across transactions/sessions (vs the per-transaction first-level cache). Backed by Ehcache/Infinispan/Hazelcast; enabled per entity with @Cacheable + a cache concurrency strategy (READ_ONLY, READ_WRITE, etc.). Good for rarely-changing reference data. Risks: stale data, complexity, and it doesn't help write-heavy or highly dynamic entities. Often an application-level cache (Redis) on DTOs is simpler and more predictable.

OSIV keeps the Hibernate session open for the entire request (through view/JSON rendering), so lazy associations load during serialization — avoiding LazyInitializationException. Boot enables it by default. Controversy: it holds DB connections longer, hides N+1 problems (lazy loads happen in the view layer, outside transactions), and couples persistence to presentation. Best practice: disable it (spring.jpa.open-in-view=false) and fetch explicitly in the service layer — fail fast and control queries.

  • Criteria API — type-safe, programmatic JPQL; verbose.
  • Spring Data Specifications — composable predicates (and/or) built on Criteria; great for optional filters.
  • QueryDSL — fluent, type-safe DSL with generated Q-classes; the most readable for complex dynamic queries.
Specification<Product> spec = Specification.where(null);
if (minPrice != null) spec = spec.and((r,q,cb) -> cb.ge(r.get("price"), minPrice));
repo.findAll(spec, pageable);

Page includes total element/page counts — requiring an extra COUNT query on every fetch (expensive on large tables). Slice only knows whether a next page exists (fetches size+1, no COUNT) — cheaper, ideal for infinite-scroll UIs that don't need a total. Use Slice when you don't display total counts; use keyset pagination for very large datasets where even Slice's offset gets slow.

Offset (LIMIT 20 OFFSET 100000) makes the DB scan and discard 100k rows to reach the page — O(offset), and results shift if rows are inserted/deleted. Keyset (seek/cursor) pagination uses a WHERE clause on the last seen sorted key: WHERE (created_at, id) < (:lastTs, :lastId) ORDER BY created_at DESC, id DESC LIMIT 20 — O(limit), index-friendly, stable. Trade-off: no random page jumps (only next/prev), but essential for deep pagination at scale.

Use a migration tool, never ddl-auto=update in production. Flyway — versioned SQL scripts (V1__init.sql), simple, SQL-first. Liquibase — changelogs in XML/YAML/JSON/SQL, database-agnostic, supports rollback. Both track applied migrations in a metadata table and run pending ones at startup. They give reproducible, version-controlled, reviewable schema evolution across environments.

Controls Hibernate's schema generation: none, validate (check schema matches entities), update (alter to match — risky), create, create-drop (drop on shutdown — tests only). Production: use none or validate and manage schema with Flyway/Liquibase. update can't drop columns, doesn't version changes, and may make unintended/irreversible alterations — never trust it in prod.

@Transactional(readOnly = true) hints that no writes occur. Benefits: Hibernate sets FlushMode to MANUAL (skips dirty checking and auto-flush → less overhead and accidental writes), the JDBC connection can be marked read-only (some DBs optimize or route to replicas), and it documents intent. Use it on all query/read service methods — a cheap, common performance and safety win.

Inside any transaction (including readOnly), the persistence context is open, so accessing a lazy association triggers a SELECT and works fine. Outside a transaction (or after it closed — e.g. in the controller post-service), the context is gone → LazyInitializationException (unless OSIV is on). The lesson: do all entity-graph navigation/mapping within the transactional service method, returning DTOs to the controller.

Catch DataIntegrityViolationException (Spring's translation of the DB's constraint error) and map it to a meaningful 409 Conflict response. Pattern: attempt the insert and handle the exception (optimistic — avoids a TOCTOU race that a pre-check existsBy would have). For high contention, an INSERT ... ON CONFLICT (upsert) via native query is cleaner. Don't rely solely on a check-then-insert — two concurrent requests can both pass the check.

@ManyToMany with @JoinTable is convenient but the join table can hold only the two FKs — no extra columns, awkward to query, and updates can delete/re-insert all rows. The robust approach is to model the join table as its own entity (two @ManyToOnes) when you need extra attributes (e.g. enrolledAt, role, quantity) or fine-grained control. Most real schemas evolve toward the explicit join entity.

In a bidirectional association, both sides hold references but only the owning side (the one without mappedBy) controls the FK. If you set only the inverse side, the FK isn't persisted. Add helper methods to keep both sides consistent:

void addItem(Item i) { items.add(i); i.setOrder(this); }  // sync both sides

Forgetting this causes "saved but FK is null" bugs and stale in-memory object graphs.

Tricky: generated IDs are null before persistence, so ID-based equals/hashCode break in collections used before save, and Hibernate proxies complicate getClass() checks. Recommended approach: use a business/natural key if one exists; otherwise use the ID for equals but a constant hashCode (so the object's hash doesn't change when the ID is assigned). Never use all fields (mutable, and lazy associations trigger loads). Records are unsuitable as entities partly for this reason.

Hibernate get() hits the DB immediately and returns null if absent. load() returns a proxy without a query, throwing ObjectNotFoundException when the proxy is first accessed if the row doesn't exist. Spring Data mirrors this: findById ≈ get (real fetch, Optional), getReferenceById ≈ load (lazy proxy, no query) — use the latter only to set FK references without loading the full entity.

long deleteByStatus(String status);   // derived delete
@Modifying @Query("DELETE FROM Order o WHERE o.status = :s")
int bulkDelete(@Param("s") String s); // bulk delete

Caveat: a derived delete first loads the entities then deletes them one by one (so lifecycle callbacks/cascades fire) — slow for many rows. A @Modifying bulk delete issues a single SQL DELETE (fast) but skips cascade/orphan removal and bypasses the persistence context (clear it to avoid staleness). Choose based on whether you need entity lifecycle behavior.

  • Use DTO projections (constructor or interface) — fetch only needed columns, skip entity overhead.
  • Mark the method @Transactional(readOnly=true).
  • For complex aggregations, use native SQL (window functions, CTEs) — JPQL is limited.
  • Add proper indexes; verify with EXPLAIN.
  • Consider a read replica, materialized views, or a denormalized read model (CQRS) for heavy reporting.
  • Cache results if data is slowly changing.

By default Hibernate generates UPDATE statements including all columns (cached SQL). @DynamicUpdate generates SQL with only the changed columns at runtime — useful for entities with many columns where only a few change, or to avoid overwriting columns updated concurrently. Trade-off: SQL can't be precompiled/cached, adding per-update overhead. Use selectively for wide tables or optimistic-locking-sensitive partial updates.

Don't load millions of rows into a List. Options: repository method returning Stream<T> (with @QueryHints for fetch size, inside a transaction, and close the stream), Hibernate's ScrollableResults, or process in pages/keyset batches. Also clear the persistence context periodically (entityManager.clear() every N rows) so managed entities don't accumulate and exhaust memory. Set the JDBC fetch size to avoid loading all rows at once.

You called persist() on an entity that already has an ID (it's detached/existing), or you cascade-persisted a graph containing an already-persisted child. persist is only for transient (new) entities. Fix: use merge() (or Spring Data save() which chooses correctly) for detached entities, or ensure cascades target only new children. Often surfaces when re-saving an object that came from a previous transaction/request.

@OneToMany(mappedBy="order", cascade=ALL, orphanRemoval=true)
List<Item> items;

order.getItems().remove(item);  // orphanRemoval → DELETEs that item
repo.delete(order);             // cascade REMOVE → DELETEs order + all items

orphanRemoval fires on disassociation; cascade REMOVE fires on parent deletion. Together they model a parent that fully owns its children's lifecycle. Without orphanRemoval, removing an item from the list just nulls/orphans it (left in DB).

Enum: always @Enumerated(EnumType.STRING) — never ORDINAL (reordering enum constants corrupts existing data). JSON: Hibernate 6 supports @JdbcTypeCode(SqlTypes.JSON) on a POJO/Map field to map a native JSON/JSONB column; pre-6 used the hibernate-types library (@Type(JsonType.class)). For custom value types, write an AttributeConverter (@Converter) to translate between the field and a column value.

@Converter(autoApply = true)
class MoneyConverter implements AttributeConverter<Money, BigDecimal> {
    public BigDecimal convertToDatabaseColumn(Money m) { return m.amount(); }
    public Money convertToEntityAttribute(BigDecimal d) { return new Money(d); }
}

It transparently converts between a Java type and a DB column type — for value objects, encrypted fields, custom enums, or comma-joined lists. autoApply applies it to all fields of that type; or annotate a field with @Convert.

With REQUIRED, the inner method joins the caller's transaction — if it throws, the whole outer transaction rolls back (one physical transaction). With REQUIRES_NEW, the inner runs in a separate transaction (the outer is suspended); the inner can commit independently even if the outer later rolls back — used for audit logs/notifications that must persist regardless. Caveat: REQUIRES_NEW uses a second connection (risk of connection-pool exhaustion / self-deadlock).

Spring follows EJB convention: roll back only on unchecked (RuntimeException) and Error; checked exceptions are treated as recoverable business outcomes that don't auto-rollback. This surprises people — a checked exception leaves the transaction to commit. Override with @Transactional(rollbackFor = Exception.class) (or noRollbackFor for exceptions that should commit). Many teams standardize on unchecked exceptions partly to avoid this footgun.

Use @DataJpaTest — it loads only JPA components, configures an embedded/real DB, and rolls back each test. For fidelity, test against the real database engine (Postgres) via Testcontainers rather than H2, since H2's SQL dialect differs and hides bugs. Verify generated SQL/N+1 with statistics, seed data with @Sql scripts or builders, and use TestEntityManager for setup. Don't mock the repository to "test" it — test the actual query behavior.

Testcontainers spins up a real database (Postgres, MySQL) in a Docker container for integration tests, then tears it down. Advantage over H2: tests run against the exact engine/dialect/features used in production — catching issues H2 masks (JSONB, window functions, specific constraints, sequence behavior, vendor SQL). Boot 3.1+ integrates it via @ServiceConnection. Slight startup cost, but far higher confidence; H2 in-memory remains fine for fast, dialect-agnostic checks.

Flush sends pending SQL (INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE) to the database within the transaction, synchronizing the persistence context — but the transaction is still open and changes are not durable/visible to others. Commit ends the transaction, making all flushed changes permanent and visible. Flush happens automatically before queries and before commit; you rarely call it explicitly except to force ID generation or to surface constraint violations early.

Add a deleted/deletedAt flag instead of physically removing rows. Hibernate 6 supports @SoftDelete; older approach uses @SQLDelete (override DELETE with an UPDATE) + @Where("deleted = false") (filter all queries) or @SQLRestriction. Caveats: unique constraints must account for deleted rows (partial indexes), and the global filter can surprise you in joins/native queries. Soft deletes preserve audit history and enable "undelete".

Spring Data JPA uses Hibernate's full ORM — lazy loading, dirty checking, caching, complex mappings; powerful but with hidden behavior (proxies, flush timing, N+1). Spring Data JDBC is a simpler, no-magic alternative: no lazy loading, no persistence context, aggregates loaded fully, you control SQL more directly — closer to the DDD aggregate model and easier to reason about. Choose JDBC for simpler domains where ORM complexity isn't worth it; JPA for rich object graphs.

spring.jpa.properties.hibernate.jdbc.batch_size=50
spring.jpa.properties.hibernate.order_inserts=true
spring.jpa.properties.hibernate.order_updates=true

And in code: flush + clear the context every batch_size rows to bound memory, and use SEQUENCE (not IDENTITY) for IDs so inserts can batch. For very large bulk loads, consider raw JDBC batchUpdate (JdbcTemplate) — bypassing the persistence context entirely is often the fastest path.

A JOIN FETCH on a collection returns the parent row once per child (Cartesian-style duplication) — so a parent with 3 items appears 3 times in the result list. Historically you added DISTINCT in JPQL to deduplicate in memory. In Hibernate 6 this is handled automatically (and the old hibernate.query.passDistinctThrough=false hint avoided sending DISTINCT to the DB). Be aware of the duplication when mapping fetch-join results manually.

Two ways: @EmbeddedId — a single @Embeddable key object with the key fields (cleaner, key is one object). @IdClass — a separate class listing the key fields, with each field annotated @Id in the entity. The embeddable must implement equals/hashCode. Composite keys complicate relationships and Spring Data method naming; many teams prefer a surrogate single-column key + a unique constraint on the business key.

Spring Data findById returns Optional<T> — making absence explicit and preventing NPEs. Best practice: handle it functionally — repo.findById(id).orElseThrow(() -> new EntityNotFoundException(id)). Avoid .get() without checking. Custom finders should also return Optional for single results. This pushes the "not found" decision to the caller and produces clean 404 handling in controllers.

Options by complexity: DB-native full-text (Postgres tsvector / MySQL FULLTEXT) via native queries; Hibernate Search (integrates Lucene/Elasticsearch with entities, keeps an index in sync); or a dedicated search engine (Elasticsearch via Spring Data Elasticsearch) for advanced relevance/faceting at scale. For simple cases, LIKE/Specifications suffice. Rule: don't bend JPA into a search engine — use the right tool when search becomes a first-class feature.

A unidirectional @OneToMany (no mappedBy) defaults to a join table (extra table + extra DELETE/INSERT churn on collection changes) — generally inefficient. A bidirectional @OneToMany with mappedBy uses the FK on the many side (no join table, the many side owns the relationship) — the efficient, recommended mapping. If you must keep it unidirectional, use @JoinColumn on the @OneToMany to force an FK instead of a join table.

Use optimistic locking (@Version): both users read version=5; the first saves (version→6); the second's UPDATE with WHERE version=5 matches 0 rows → OptimisticLockException, prompting a refresh/retry rather than silently overwriting the first edit. For UI, surface a "data changed, please reload" conflict (409). For must-succeed serialized writes (inventory), use pessimistic locking instead. The anti-pattern is read-modify-write with no version, which loses one update silently.

EAGER-fetching two or more collections in one query produces a Cartesian product — if an entity has 10 items and 10 tags, the join returns 100 rows (MultipleBagFetchException in Hibernate for two bags). It bloats memory and SQL. Fixes: make associations LAZY and fetch them in separate queries / with @BatchSize / multiple entity graphs, or use Set instead of List for one. Never EAGER-fetch multiple collections together.

Hibernate Envers (@Audited) automatically creates audit/history tables capturing every revision of an entity, with a revision entity recording who/when — queryable to see an entity's state at any past revision. For lighter needs, Spring Data @CreatedBy/@LastModifiedBy (with an AuditorAware bean reading the security context) stamps who created/modified the current row. Envers gives full history; the auditing annotations give last-touched metadata. Choose by whether you need a full trail or just current provenance.

@NamedEntityGraph is defined once on the entity (reusable, named fetch plan) and referenced by name on repository methods (@EntityGraph("User.withRoles")). Ad-hoc @EntityGraph(attributePaths=...) is declared inline per method. Use named graphs for fetch plans reused across many queries (DRY); use ad-hoc for one-off fetch needs. Both override default lazy loading for that query without changing the entity's mappings.

@Procedure(procedureName = "calculate_bonus")
BigDecimal calculateBonus(@Param("emp_id") Long id);

// or on the entity:
@NamedStoredProcedureQuery(name="...", procedureName="...",
   parameters = {@StoredProcedureParameter(...)})

Use stored procedures for DB-side logic that's far cheaper to run near the data (bulk computations, set operations). Trade-off: business logic in the DB is harder to version/test and couples you to the vendor — keep procedures focused on data-intensive work, not core domain rules.

Classic N+1. Diagnosis: enable Hibernate statistics / SQL logging → see one main query + one per row for a lazy association. Fixes in order: (1) replace entity serialization with a DTO projection via a single JPQL/native query (best — fetch exactly what the API needs); (2) if entities are needed, use @EntityGraph/JOIN FETCH (mind pagination + collection fetch); (3) for multiple collections, hibernate.default_batch_fetch_size; (4) disable OSIV so such problems fail fast in tests. Verify the query count drops to 1–2.

Lost update. Add @Version for optimistic locking: the second save fails with OptimisticLockException instead of overwriting. In the API, catch it and return 409 Conflict with the current state so the client reloads/merges. For a smoother UX, send the version (ETag) to the client and validate via If-Match. If the operation is high-contention and must serialize (e.g. wallet balance), switch to pessimistic locking or an atomic UPDATE (SET balance = balance - :amt WHERE balance >= :amt).

Bulk JPQL updates execute directly in the DB and bypass the persistence context (L1 cache) — already-loaded managed entities still hold the old values. Fixes: add @Modifying(clearAutomatically = true, flushAutomatically = true) so Hibernate flushes pending changes before and clears the context after, forcing fresh reads; or call entityManager.refresh(entity) on specific entities; or restructure so you don't read those entities again in the same transaction. Understand that bulk operations and the cache are decoupled.

  • Enable JDBC batching (batch_size, order_inserts); use SEQUENCE with allocationSize (IDENTITY disables batching).
  • flush() + clear() the persistence context every batch to prevent it from accumulating a million managed entities (the OOM cause).
  • Disable the second-level cache for the load; consider StatelessSession (no L1 cache, no dirty checking).
  • For extreme volume, bypass JPA: JdbcTemplate batchUpdate or the DB's native bulk loader (COPY/LOAD DATA).
  • Tune the DB (drop/rebuild indexes around the load, larger commit batches).

Holding a DB connection while waiting on a remote call ties up a pooled connection for the entire latency — under load this exhausts the connection pool and stalls the app. Fix: keep transactions short and DB-only — do the remote call before or after the transaction, not inside it. If the API result must be persisted, structure as: call API (no tx) → open a short tx to save. For "do it only if committed", use @TransactionalEventListener(AFTER_COMMIT). Never block on I/O inside a transaction.

The dual-write problem. Use the Transactional Outbox: in the same transaction that saves the business data, insert an outbox row describing the side effect. A separate poller/CDC (Debezium) reads committed outbox rows and performs the side effect (send email/publish event), marking them done. This guarantees the side effect happens iff the commit succeeded, and survives crashes. Combine with idempotency on the consumer (email provider dedup key) for effectively-once. @TransactionalEventListener(AFTER_COMMIT) is a lighter in-process variant but loses events on crash.

Offset pagination scans and discards all prior rows — O(offset). Redesign with keyset (seek) pagination: order by an indexed unique key and use WHERE (sort_col, id) < (:lastSeenSort, :lastSeenId) ORDER BY sort_col DESC, id DESC LIMIT n. This is O(limit), uses the index, and is stable under concurrent inserts. Also avoid the COUNT query (use Slice). Trade-off: only forward/backward navigation, no arbitrary page jumps — acceptable for feeds/infinite scroll. Add a covering index on the sort columns.

EAGER associations are fetched in every query of that entity (extra joins or selects), even when unneeded. Fix: change the mapping to fetch = LAZY (for @ManyToOne, set optional=false when the FK is non-null to help Hibernate avoid a proxy/extra select). Then fetch it explicitly where needed via JOIN FETCH / entity graph. Risk to manage: code that relied on EAGER may now hit LazyInitializationException — audit usages and ensure fetching happens within the transaction. The principle: LAZY by default, fetch on demand.

Hibernate doesn't execute SQL immediately on persist/setter — it queues actions and flushes them in a fixed order (inserts, updates, then deletes) at flush time. This causes surprises: a delete-then-insert of the same unique key fails because inserts run before deletes (constraint violation); or your "delete then save" doesn't behave sequentially. Fixes: explicit flush() between conflicting operations, or restructure. Also, flush-before-query means a query can trigger pending writes earlier than you expect. Knowing the action queue explains "why did this SQL run in that order".

  • Database per tenant — strongest isolation, easy backup/restore per tenant, but many connection pools and ops overhead; route the datasource per request (AbstractRoutingDataSource).
  • Schema per tenant — one DB, schema switched per tenant (Hibernate SCHEMA multitenancy); good balance.
