  • Shared schema + discriminator column (tenant_id) — cheapest, but every query must filter by tenant (use a Hibernate filter / @TenantId) — a single missed filter = data leak.

Resolve tenant from request context; never trust client-supplied tenant without auth. Trade isolation vs cost vs operational complexity.

Streaming repository methods (Stream<T>) keep a DB cursor and connection open for the stream's lifetime, so: (1) the call must be inside an open transaction for the whole consumption; (2) you must close the stream (try-with-resources) or the connection leaks; (3) set the JDBC fetch size and periodically clear the persistence context to bound memory. If you collect the whole stream into a list, you gained nothing — stream only to process incrementally. Misuse manifests as connection-pool exhaustion under load.

High contention on one row makes optimistic retries thrash (everyone collides). Options: (1) switch that operation to pessimistic locking or a single atomic SQL UPDATE (e.g. UPDATE stock SET qty = qty - 1 WHERE id=? AND qty > 0) so the DB serializes it without app-level retries; (2) reduce contention by sharding the counter (N sub-rows summed) for write-heavy counters; (3) move the hot counter out of the relational DB (Redis atomic ops). The lesson: optimistic locking suits low contention; hot rows need serialization or sharding.

Use an AbstractRoutingDataSource that picks the writer or a reader based on a context flag set per transaction — typically driven by @Transactional(readOnly = true) (route readOnly tx to replicas, read-write to the primary) via a TransactionSynchronization/AOP aspect. Caveats: replication lag means read-after-write may not see just-committed data (route critical read-after-write to the primary), and the routing decision must be made before the connection is acquired. Some setups use separate repositories/datasources per role for clarity.

  • LazyInitializationException during serialization (session closed).
  • Over-fetching / accidental exposure of sensitive fields and entire object graphs.
  • Infinite recursion on bidirectional relationships (needs @JsonIgnore/@JsonManagedReference hacks).
  • Coupling the API contract to the DB schema (every schema change risks breaking clients).
  • Mass-assignment vulnerabilities on input binding.

Discipline: dedicated request/response DTOs mapped (MapStruct) in the service layer; entities never cross the web boundary.

Almost always a bidirectional relationship sync bug: you set the child into the parent's collection but didn't set the owning side (the child's @ManyToOne back-reference that actually maps the FK). The inverse (mappedBy) side doesn't control the FK, so it persists as null. Fix: set both sides (helper method addChild), or ensure the owning side is set before save. Tests may pass if they re-read within the same context (cache hides it) — production with a fresh read exposes the null FK.

JPA/Hibernate bind the EntityManager and connection to the current thread via a ThreadLocal. So a managed entity loaded in one thread is not usable in an @Async thread (different/no persistence context → LazyInitializationException), and you can't safely pass managed entities across threads. For async, pass IDs/DTOs and re-load inside the async method's own transaction. Reactive (WebFlux) is incompatible with blocking JPA entirely — use R2DBC for reactive data access. Mixing thread-bound JPA with async/reactive is a common source of subtle bugs.

Persist an append-only events table (aggregateId, sequenceNo, type, payload JSON, timestamp) with a unique constraint on (aggregateId, sequenceNo) for optimistic concurrency. Rebuild state by replaying events; add snapshots periodically to bound replay cost. Publish events via the Outbox for downstream consumers (CQRS read models). Considerations: event schema evolution (versioned payloads + upcasters), idempotent projections, and that you query read models, not the event store, for current state. Spring Data JDBC or a purpose-built event store often fits better than full JPA here.

Don't write 2^10 query methods or concatenate JPQL strings (injection + maintenance nightmare). Use Spring Data Specifications or QueryDSL to compose predicates only for the filters that are present, combined with Pageable for sorting/paging:

Specification<Product> spec = Specification.where(null);
if (name != null)  spec = spec.and(ProductSpecs.nameLike(name));
if (min != null)   spec = spec.and(ProductSpecs.priceGte(min));
return repo.findAll(spec, pageable);

Type-safe, composable, paginated, and each predicate is unit-testable. Add indexes for commonly filtered columns.

Enable formatted SQL + parameter binding logging and Hibernate statistics to see the exact generated SQL. Common causes: an EAGER association adding a join; @SecondaryTable/inheritance strategy; a @Formula field; an implicit join from navigating an association in JPQL; or a wrong @Column/mappedBy mapping. Cross-check the entity mappings and the JPQL/Criteria. For the definitive picture, run the SQL with EXPLAIN. Tools like datasource-proxy or the Hibernate query plan cache help. Fix the mapping or fetch plan, not the symptom.

Benefits: fewer DB hits for hot, rarely-changing entities/reference data. Backfires when: data changes frequently (constant invalidation/staleness), the app is clustered (cache coherence across nodes needs a distributed cache + invalidation, adding latency and complexity), bulk/native updates bypass it (stale entries), and query cache requires careful tuning. It also adds a subtle correctness surface. Pragmatic alternative: cache immutable DTOs in an application cache (Redis/Caffeine) with explicit TTL/invalidation, which is easier to reason about than L2 entity caching.

Diagnose from the DB's deadlock log (Postgres logs the two queries; MySQL SHOW ENGINE INNODB STATUS) — it shows which locks/rows collided. Common cause: transactions acquiring locks in different orders (T1 locks A then B, T2 locks B then A). Prevent: acquire locks/update rows in a consistent order (e.g. sort IDs before batch updates), keep transactions short, lower isolation if safe, add indexes (so locks are row-level not range/table), set lock timeouts, and retry on deadlock (DBs pick a victim — retry the failed transaction with backoff).

Do it in backward-compatible steps (expand-contract): (1) add the column as nullable (fast, no rewrite); (2) deploy code that writes the new column; (3) backfill existing rows in batches (avoid one giant locking UPDATE); (4) once populated, add the NOT NULL constraint (or a validated check) in a later migration; (5) remove old code paths. Each Flyway migration is small and online; avoid blocking DDL on huge tables (use tools like pg_repack / online DDL). The key principle: never deploy a migration that's incompatible with the currently running app version.

A StatelessSession has no persistence context: no first-level cache, no dirty checking, no cascades, no lazy loading — it's a thin layer over JDBC with entity mapping. Use it for bulk batch processing (mass insert/update/export) where the L1 cache would otherwise accumulate millions of entities and cause OOM/slow dirty-check scans. Trade-off: you lose ORM conveniences (must explicitly insert/update, no auto-flush of changes, no cascade). It's the JPA way to get near-JDBC bulk performance while keeping mappings.

Pre-checking with existsBy then inserting has a TOCTOU race — both pass the check, then one violates the unique constraint. Robust design: rely on the DB unique constraint as the source of truth, attempt the insert, and catch DataIntegrityViolationException → translate to a clean 409 Conflict (or fetch-and-return the existing resource for idempotent create). For upsert semantics, use INSERT ... ON CONFLICT DO NOTHING/UPDATE. Never depend on application-level checks for uniqueness under concurrency.

  • Modeling: aggregates with surrogate keys (SEQUENCE), LAZY associations, value objects as @Embeddable, enums as STRING, @Version for optimistic locking, audit columns.
  • Transactions: short, service-layer boundaries, readOnly for queries, no remote calls inside; appropriate isolation; atomic SQL for hot counters (stock).
  • Performance: DTO projections (no entity-over-REST, no N+1), keyset pagination, batched writes with SEQUENCE, proper indexes, read replicas for queries, app-level caching of reference data.
  • Consistency across services: Transactional Outbox for events, idempotent consumers, Saga for multi-service workflows.
  • Resilience & ops: Hikari sized to DB capacity, query/lock timeouts, Flyway migrations (expand-contract), Testcontainers in CI, metrics on pool/queries/locks, retry on deadlock.

🔐 Spring Security — Top 100

ℹ️ Authentication, authorization, filter chain, JWT/OAuth2, CSRF/CORS, password handling, method security, and real-world scenarios.

Authentication (AuthN) — verifying who you are (login, credentials). Authorization (AuthZ) — determining what you're allowed to do (roles/permissions). AuthN happens first and produces a principal; AuthZ then checks that principal's access to a resource. Mnemonic: AuthN = identity, AuthZ = permission.

A comprehensive security framework for authentication, authorization, and protection against common attacks (CSRF, session fixation, clickjacking). It integrates as a servlet filter chain, supports many auth mechanisms (form login, basic, JWT, OAuth2, LDAP, SAML), method-level security, and password encoding. Add spring-boot-starter-security and it secures everything by default (HTTP Basic + a generated password).

Auto-configuration secures all endpoints by default — every request requires authentication via HTTP Basic and a form login page. It generates a random password (printed in the logs) for the default user. CSRF protection, security headers, and session management are enabled. You then customize via a SecurityFilterChain bean to define which endpoints are public, auth mechanisms, etc. "Secure by default" is the philosophy.

@Bean
SecurityFilterChain filterChain(HttpSecurity http) throws Exception {
    return http
        .authorizeHttpRequests(auth -> auth
            .requestMatchers("/public/**").permitAll()
            .requestMatchers("/admin/**").hasRole("ADMIN")
            .anyRequest().authenticated())
        .formLogin(Customizer.withDefaults())
        .build();
}

Since Spring Security 5.7/6, you define a SecurityFilterChain bean (the old WebSecurityConfigurerAdapter is removed) using the lambda DSL.

The SecurityContext holds the current Authentication (the authenticated principal + authorities). SecurityContextHolder stores it — by default in a ThreadLocal, so any code in the request thread can access the current user: SecurityContextHolder.getContext().getAuthentication(). After authentication, Spring populates it; it's cleared at request end. For async, the strategy must propagate it.

Authentication represents the auth token/result: the principal (the user identity, often a UserDetails or username), credentials (password/token, often erased after auth), and authorities (granted roles/permissions). Before authentication it carries the request's credentials; after, it's the authenticated result stored in the SecurityContext. Controllers can inject it via @AuthenticationPrincipal or a Principal parameter.

UserDetails is Spring's user model (username, password, authorities, account status flags). UserDetailsService is the interface with one method, loadUserByUsername(String), that loads a user from your store (DB, LDAP) for authentication.

@Service
class MyUserDetailsService implements UserDetailsService {
    public UserDetails loadUserByUsername(String u) {
        User user = repo.findByUsername(u)
            .orElseThrow(() -> new UsernameNotFoundException(u));
        return org.springframework.security.core.userdetails.User
            .withUsername(user.getUsername()).password(user.getPassword())
            .roles(user.getRoles()).build();
    }
}

Never store plaintext passwords. A PasswordEncoder hashes them with a slow, salted one-way function so a DB leak doesn't expose passwords. BCrypt is the common default (adaptive cost factor); Argon2 and SCrypt are also supported.

@Bean PasswordEncoder encoder() { return new BCryptPasswordEncoder(); }
// store: encoder.encode(rawPassword)
// verify: encoder.matches(rawPassword, storedHash)

Use DelegatingPasswordEncoder (the default) to support multiple algorithms with a {bcrypt} prefix for future migration.

Roles are just authorities with a ROLE_ prefix convention. hasRole("ADMIN") checks for the authority ROLE_ADMIN (Spring adds the prefix for you). hasAuthority("ROLE_ADMIN") checks the exact string. Use roles for coarse categories (ADMIN, USER) and fine-grained authorities/permissions (order:read, user:delete) for granular access. Be consistent about the prefix.

Cross-Site Request Forgery — a malicious site tricks a logged-in user's browser into submitting a state-changing request using their session cookie. Spring Security defends with a CSRF token: a random token tied to the session that must accompany unsafe requests (POST/PUT/DELETE); the attacker's site can't read it (same-origin policy). Enabled by default for session-based apps. For stateless JWT APIs (no cookies), CSRF is typically disabled.

Disable it only for stateless APIs that don't use cookies for auth — e.g. a REST API authenticated by a Bearer JWT in the Authorization header. Since the browser doesn't auto-attach a Bearer token (unlike cookies), CSRF doesn't apply. Keep CSRF enabled for any session/cookie-based app (server-rendered or SPA using session cookies). Blindly disabling CSRF "to make POST work" is a common security mistake.

JSON Web Token — a compact, self-contained, signed token carrying claims. Three Base64URL parts separated by dots: Header (algorithm, type), Payload (claims: sub, exp, roles, custom data), Signature (header+payload signed with a secret/private key). The signature lets the server verify integrity without a DB lookup. Note: payload is encoded, not encrypted — never put secrets in it.

  1. User logs in with credentials → server validates and returns a signed JWT.
  2. Client stores it and sends it on each request: Authorization: Bearer <token>.
  3. A filter extracts and validates the signature + expiry (no DB/session lookup) and populates the SecurityContext.
  4. Request proceeds with the user's authorities.

"Stateless" = the server stores no session; the token carries the identity. Scales horizontally easily.

SessionJWT
StateServer stores sessionStateless (token self-contained)
ScalingNeeds sticky sessions / shared storeScales horizontally easily
RevocationEasy (delete session)Hard (valid until expiry)
TransportCookie (CSRF risk)Header (XSS risk if stored badly)

OAuth2 is an authorization framework that lets a user grant a third-party app limited access to their resources without sharing their password. Roles: Resource Owner (the user), Client (the app requesting access), Authorization Server (issues tokens, e.g. Google/Keycloak), Resource Server (hosts protected APIs, validates tokens). It deals in access tokens, not identity — that's OIDC's job.

OAuth2 = authorization (delegated access via access tokens) — it does NOT define how to identify the user. OIDC is a thin identity layer on top of OAuth2 that adds an ID token (a JWT with user identity claims) and a standard userinfo endpoint. Use OAuth2 for "let this app call that API on my behalf"; use OIDC for "log in with Google/Okta" (authentication). "Login with X" buttons are OIDC.

All do method-level authorization (enable with @EnableMethodSecurity). @Secured("ROLE_ADMIN") — Spring's simple role check. @RolesAllowed — the JSR-250 standard equivalent. @PreAuthorize("hasRole('ADMIN') and #id == authentication.principal.id") — the most powerful, supporting SpEL expressions (combine roles, method args, principal data). Prefer @PreAuthorize for anything beyond a plain role check.

@EnableMethodSecurity   // on a config class (replaces @EnableGlobalMethodSecurity)
@Service
class OrderService {
    @PreAuthorize("hasRole('ADMIN')")
    void deleteAll() {...}
    @PostAuthorize("returnObject.ownerId == authentication.principal.id")
    Order get(Long id) {...}
}

It secures service methods (not just URLs) — useful when the same method is reachable from multiple entry points. Implemented via AOP proxies (same self-invocation caveat applies).

  • X-Content-Type-Options: nosniff — prevent MIME sniffing.
  • X-Frame-Options: DENY — prevent clickjacking (framing).
  • Cache-Control — prevent caching of sensitive data.
  • Strict-Transport-Security (HSTS) — enforce HTTPS (when over HTTPS).

You can add Content-Security-Policy and customize via the headers DSL. These mitigate common browser-based attacks out of the box.

http.authorizeHttpRequests(auth -> auth
    .requestMatchers("/", "/login", "/public/**", "/actuator/health").permitAll()
    .requestMatchers(HttpMethod.GET, "/api/products/**").permitAll()
    .anyRequest().authenticated());

Order matters — more specific matchers first, anyRequest() last. Note permitAll() still runs the filter chain; to fully bypass security (e.g. static assets) use web-level ignoring sparingly.

formLogin presents an HTML login form, authenticates against a POST, and maintains a session (good for browser apps). httpBasic uses the Authorization: Basic header (base64 username:password) on each request — simple for APIs/testing but credentials sent every request (must be over HTTPS). Boot enables both by default. For REST APIs you typically replace both with token-based auth.

@GetMapping("/me")
UserDto me(@AuthenticationPrincipal UserDetails user) {
    return toDto(user);
}
// or
Authentication auth = SecurityContextHolder.getContext().getAuthentication();
String username = auth.getName();

@AuthenticationPrincipal is cleanest. You can also inject java.security.Principal or Authentication directly as a controller parameter.

401 Unauthorized — you're not authenticated (missing/invalid credentials or token); the server doesn't know who you are. 403 Forbidden — you are authenticated but lack permission for this resource. In Spring Security, AuthenticationEntryPoint handles 401, AccessDeniedHandler handles 403. The naming is historically confusing (401 says "Unauthorized" but means "Unauthenticated").

CORS controls whether a browser permits a page from origin A to call your API at origin B. It's a browser-enforced mechanism (not server-side security per se) — it doesn't protect the server, it relaxes the same-origin policy. In Spring Security, configure CORS in the security config (the security filter runs before MVC) via http.cors(...) + a CorsConfigurationSource. Never use wildcard origins with credentials in production.

@Bean
UserDetailsService users(PasswordEncoder enc) {
    UserDetails user = User.withUsername("user")
        .password(enc.encode("password")).roles("USER").build();
    UserDetails admin = User.withUsername("admin")
        .password(enc.encode("admin")).roles("ADMIN").build();
    return new InMemoryUserDetailsManager(user, admin);
}

Fine for demos/tests; for real apps use a UserDetailsService backed by a database.

Spring Security is an ordered chain of servlet filters fronted by FilterChainProxy (a Chain of Responsibility). Key filters in order: SecurityContextPersistenceFilter (load/save context) → CsrfFilter → authentication filters (UsernamePasswordAuthenticationFilter, BearerTokenAuthenticationFilter) → ExceptionTranslationFilter (turns auth exceptions into 401/403) → FilterSecurityInterceptor/AuthorizationFilter (the final authorization decision). Each request flows through them; understanding the order is key to debugging.

  1. A filter builds an unauthenticated Authentication token from the request.
  2. It calls the AuthenticationManager (default ProviderManager).
  3. ProviderManager delegates to a list of AuthenticationProviders; each declares what it supports.
  4. The matching provider (e.g. DaoAuthenticationProvider) loads the user via UserDetailsService and verifies credentials with the PasswordEncoder.
  5. On success it returns a fully authenticated token (with authorities); the filter stores it in the SecurityContext.

Add a custom OncePerRequestFilter before UsernamePasswordAuthenticationFilter:

protected void doFilterInternal(req, res, chain) {
    String token = extractBearer(req);
    if (token != null && jwtService.isValid(token)) {
        var auth = new UsernamePasswordAuthenticationToken(
            jwtService.getUsername(token), null, jwtService.getAuthorities(token));
        SecurityContextHolder.getContext().setAuthentication(auth);
    }
    chain.doFilter(req, res);
}

Configure the chain as stateless (SessionCreationPolicy.STATELESS), disable CSRF, and register the filter. For standard OAuth2/OIDC, prefer the built-in oauth2ResourceServer().jwt() instead of hand-rolling.

http.oauth2ResourceServer(oauth -> oauth.jwt(Customizer.withDefaults()));
// properties:
// spring.security.oauth2.resourceserver.jwt.issuer-uri=https://auth.example.com

Spring fetches the issuer's JWKS (public keys) and validates signature, issuer, and expiry automatically — no custom filter needed. Map claims to authorities via a JwtAuthenticationConverter. This is the recommended approach over hand-written JWT filters: standards-compliant, key rotation handled, less code.

Access token — short-lived (minutes), sent on every API call; if stolen, the exposure window is small. Refresh token — long-lived, stored securely, used only to obtain new access tokens when they expire (without re-login). This balances security (short access token) and UX (no frequent logins). Refresh tokens should be revocable (stored server-side), rotated on use, and bound to the client/device. If a refresh token is compromised, revoke it to cut off access.

Pure JWTs are stateless and self-validating, so a valid token works until it expires — you can't "delete" it server-side. Solutions: (1) short expiry + refresh tokens (limit the window); (2) a token blocklist/denylist in Redis (checked per request — but this reintroduces state, partly defeating JWT's purpose); (3) a token version per user in the DB (bump it on logout/password change → invalidate all their tokens); (4) rotate signing keys. Trade-off: true revocation requires some server state.

localStorage — easy, but readable by JavaScript → vulnerable to XSS (a script can steal the token). httpOnly + Secure + SameSite cookie — not readable by JS (XSS-safe) but susceptible to CSRF (mitigate with SameSite=Strict/Lax + CSRF tokens). Best practice for SPAs: store the token in an httpOnly cookie (or keep the access token in memory + refresh token in an httpOnly cookie). There's no perfect option — defend against both XSS (CSP, sanitization) and CSRF.

Map users → roles → fine-grained permissions/authorities (e.g. ROLE_MANAGER grants order:approve, report:view). Grant permissions as authorities and check those in @PreAuthorize("hasAuthority('order:approve')") rather than hardcoding roles. This decouples code from role names — you can reassign permissions to roles in config/DB without code changes. URL rules guard endpoints; method security guards business operations; combine both (defense in depth).

@PreAuthorize("hasRole('ADMIN') or #userId == authentication.principal.id")
User getUser(@P("userId") Long userId) {...}

@PreAuthorize("@orderSecurity.isOwner(#orderId, authentication)")
Order getOrder(Long orderId) {...}   // delegate to a bean for complex logic

SpEL can reference method args (#arg), the principal, and even call Spring beans (@beanName.method(...)) for complex rules — the cleanest pattern for non-trivial authorization, keeping logic testable in a dedicated bean.

  • @PreAuthorize — check before the method runs (using args/principal).
  • @PostAuthorize — check after, using the returnObject (e.g. verify the returned entity belongs to the user) — note the method still executes.
  • @PreFilter — filter elements of an input collection before the method.
  • @PostFilter — filter elements of the returned collection (drop items the user can't see).

PostFilter is convenient but inefficient for large collections (filters in memory) — prefer filtering in the query.

http.exceptionHandling(ex -> ex
    .authenticationEntryPoint((req, res, e) ->    // 401
        res.sendError(401, "Authentication required"))
    .accessDeniedHandler((req, res, e) ->         // 403
        res.sendError(403, "Access denied")));

The AuthenticationEntryPoint handles unauthenticated access (commence auth / return 401); the AccessDeniedHandler handles authenticated-but-forbidden (403). For REST APIs, return a consistent JSON error body instead of HTML.

Session fixation: an attacker sets a known session ID on the victim (e.g. via a link), then after the victim logs in, the attacker uses that same ID to hijack the authenticated session. Spring Security prevents it by creating a new session ID on authentication (the default changeSessionId / migrateSession strategy) — the pre-login ID becomes useless. Configurable via http.sessionManagement().sessionFixation(). Enabled by default for session-based apps.

http.sessionManagement(s -> s.sessionCreationPolicy(SessionCreationPolicy.STATELESS))
    .csrf(csrf -> csrf.disable())   // no cookies → CSRF N/A
    .authorizeHttpRequests(...)
    .oauth2ResourceServer(o -> o.jwt(withDefaults()));

STATELESS tells Spring never to create or use an HttpSession — each request must carry its own credentials (token). Essential for scalable, horizontally-distributed REST APIs where you don't want server-side session affinity.

Layer multiple independent controls so one failure doesn't breach the system: network (firewall, TLS), gateway (rate limiting, WAF), URL-level authorization, method-level security, data-level checks (row ownership, tenant filter), input validation and output encoding, least-privilege DB accounts, and secrets management. Never rely solely on the UI hiding a button or a single auth check — assume each layer can be bypassed and validate at every trust boundary.

Always use parameterized queries / prepared statements — JPA/Hibernate with bind parameters (:param, ?1), Spring Data derived queries, and JdbcTemplate with ? placeholders all parameterize automatically. Never concatenate user input into JPQL/SQL strings. For dynamic queries use Criteria/Specifications/QueryDSL (which parameterize) rather than string building. Validate/whitelist any dynamic identifiers (column/table names) since those can't be bind parameters.

Cross-Site Scripting: attacker injects script that runs in other users' browsers. Defenses: contextual output encoding (Thymeleaf escapes by default with th:text; avoid th:utext for untrusted data), a strict Content-Security-Policy header, input validation/sanitization (OWASP Java HTML Sanitizer for rich text), httpOnly cookies (so stolen-token XSS can't read auth cookies), and never inserting untrusted data into HTML/JS contexts unescaped. For REST+SPA, the frontend framework must also escape — XSS is primarily an output-encoding problem.

A widely-referenced list of the most critical web application security risks (e.g. Broken Access Control, Cryptographic Failures, Injection, Insecure Design, Security Misconfiguration, Vulnerable Components, Auth Failures, SSRF). It matters because it focuses effort on the highest-impact, most-common vulnerabilities and is a common interview/security-review checklist. Broken Access Control (e.g. IDOR — accessing another user's resource by changing an ID) has topped recent lists — always enforce ownership checks server-side.

Insecure Direct Object Reference: a user changes an ID in the request (/orders/123 → /orders/124) and accesses someone else's data because the server checks authentication but not ownership. Fix: always verify the authenticated user owns/may access the specific object — @PostAuthorize("returnObject.userId == authentication.principal.id") or an explicit check/query filter (findByIdAndUserId). Never trust client-supplied IDs to be the user's own. This is the #1 access-control bug.

http.authorizeHttpRequests(auth -> auth
    .requestMatchers(EndpointRequest.to("health", "info")).permitAll()
    .requestMatchers(EndpointRequest.toAnyEndpoint()).hasRole("ADMIN"));

Expose only needed endpoints (management.endpoints.web.exposure.include), require an admin role for sensitive ones (env, heapdump, loggers), optionally run management on a separate non-public port, and hide health details from anonymous users. Leaking /env or /heapdump publicly is a serious misconfiguration.

Spring Security's rememberMe() issues a persistent cookie so users stay logged in across sessions. Two strategies: hash-based token (signed cookie containing username+expiry+hash — simple, no storage) and persistent token (a series+token stored in the DB, rotated on each use — more secure, supports revocation and detects token theft). Always use Secure+httpOnly cookies and a reasonable expiry. The persistent approach is preferred for security-sensitive apps.

DaoAuthenticationProvider is the standard provider: it uses a UserDetailsService + PasswordEncoder for username/password auth — sufficient for most apps. A custom AuthenticationProvider is needed for non-standard auth: API keys, OTP/2FA, LDAP-with-custom-logic, multi-step authentication, or integrating an external identity system. You implement authenticate() and supports(), then register it with the AuthenticationManager. Use the standard provider unless your auth flow genuinely differs.

After verifying the first factor (password), require a second factor before granting full access. Common: TOTP (time-based codes from Google Authenticator — server stores a shared secret per user, validates the 6-digit code), SMS/email OTP, or WebAuthn/passkeys. In Spring, model it as a two-step flow: first auth produces a "partially authenticated" state (limited authority); a second filter/endpoint validates the OTP and elevates to fully authenticated. Store secrets encrypted; provide backup codes.

  • Authorization Code (+ PKCE) — web/mobile/SPA user login; the secure default.
  • Client Credentials — machine-to-machine (no user), service-to-service auth.
  • Refresh Token — obtain new access tokens without re-login.
  • Password (ROPC) — deprecated; only legacy first-party clients.
  • Implicit — deprecated (insecure); replaced by Auth Code + PKCE for SPAs.

Modern guidance: Authorization Code with PKCE for user flows, Client Credentials for service flows.

Proof Key for Code Exchange — an extension to the Authorization Code flow protecting public clients (SPAs, mobile apps that can't keep a secret). The client generates a random code_verifier, sends its hash (code_challenge) when requesting the auth code, then sends the original verifier when exchanging the code for tokens. An attacker who intercepts the auth code can't exchange it without the verifier. PKCE is now recommended for all Authorization Code flows, not just public clients.

Forward the user's token (or a service token) on outbound calls so the downstream can authorize. Patterns: pass the JWT in the Authorization header via an interceptor (each service validates it independently — stateless); use OAuth2 token relay (Spring Security's ServerOAuth2AuthorizedClientExchangeFilterFunction); or the gateway authenticates and injects identity headers into a trusted internal network. For service-to-service without a user, use Client Credentials tokens (mutual TLS adds transport-level identity). Always validate tokens at each service — don't trust the network.

http.addFilterBefore(jwtFilter, UsernamePasswordAuthenticationFilter.class);
// also: addFilterAfter, addFilterAt

Position matters — a JWT filter must run before the username/password filter so the SecurityContext is populated before authorization. Extend OncePerRequestFilter (guarantees one execution per request, even on forwards/includes). Don't register security filters as ordinary Boot @Component filters too, or they run twice — keep them inside the security chain.

permitAll() — anyone, authenticated or not, may access (filter chain still runs). authenticated() — requires a logged-in (non-anonymous) user. anonymous — Spring assigns an AnonymousAuthenticationToken to unauthenticated requests so the SecurityContext is never null; anonymous() matcher allows only anonymous users (rare). Subtlety: with anonymous auth enabled, a request is always "authenticated" as anonymous, so use isAuthenticated() (which excludes anonymous) when you mean a real user.

@Bean JwtAuthenticationConverter converter() {
    var roles = new JwtGrantedAuthoritiesConverter();
    roles.setAuthoritiesClaimName("roles");     // claim holding roles
    roles.setAuthorityPrefix("ROLE_");
    var conv = new JwtAuthenticationConverter();
    conv.setJwtGrantedAuthoritiesConverter(roles);
    return conv;
}

Identity providers put roles/scopes in custom claims (e.g. Keycloak's realm_access.roles). A converter extracts them into GrantedAuthoritys so hasRole/hasAuthority work. Without this, your roles never reach the authorization checks.

Scopes describe what the client application is permitted to do on the user's behalf (read:orders, write:profile) — consented at authorization time. Roles describe what the user is (ADMIN, USER) — about identity/permissions. A token can carry both: scopes limit the delegated app's reach, roles drive user-level authorization. In Spring, scopes map to SCOPE_ authorities by default; check with hasAuthority('SCOPE_read:orders').

Symmetric (HMAC, HS256) — the same secret signs and verifies; both issuer and verifier must share it (risky to distribute; fine when one service does both). Asymmetric (RSA/EC, RS256) — the authorization server signs with a private key; resource servers verify with the public key (fetched from the JWKS endpoint, auto-rotated). Asymmetric is preferred in distributed systems: resource servers never hold a signing secret, and key rotation is centralized. Spring's resource server handles JWKS fetching automatically via issuer-uri.

If a SPA authenticates via session/JWT in a cookie, disabling CSRF exposes it to forged cross-site requests. Mitigations without fully relying on CSRF tokens: SameSite=Strict/Lax cookies (browser won't send the cookie on cross-site requests), verify Origin/Referer headers, and use the cookie-based CSRF token pattern (CookieCsrfTokenRepository.withHttpOnlyFalse()) so the SPA reads the token and echoes it in a header. Only disable CSRF when auth is via a non-cookie Bearer header.

Limit requests per client/IP/key over a window to prevent abuse, brute-force, credential-stuffing, and DoS. Implement at the gateway (Spring Cloud Gateway RequestRateLimiter + Redis) or app level (Bucket4j, Resilience4j RateLimiter) using token-bucket/sliding-window algorithms with a distributed store for multi-instance correctness. Return 429 Too Many Requests with Retry-After. It's a security control because it caps the rate of attacks (e.g. password guessing) and protects availability.

  • Account lockout / throttling after N failed attempts (with exponential backoff or temporary lock).
  • Rate limit the login endpoint per IP/username.
  • CAPTCHA after suspicious activity.
  • Slow password hashing (BCrypt cost factor) so each guess is expensive.
  • Monitoring/alerting on spikes; 2FA as a strong backstop.

Spring Security exposes failure/success events (AuthenticationFailureBadCredentialsEvent) you can listen to for counting and locking. Don't reveal whether the username or password was wrong (avoid user enumeration).

Leaking whether an account exists — via different login error messages ("user not found" vs "wrong password"), registration responses, password-reset messages, or response-time differences. Attackers harvest valid usernames/emails for targeted attacks. Prevent: use generic messages ("invalid credentials"), return the same response for existing and non-existing accounts on password reset ("if the email exists, we sent a link"), and normalize response times. Spring's UsernameNotFoundException is hidden as BadCredentialsException by default for this reason.

Never hardcode or commit secrets. Use a secrets manager (Vault, AWS Secrets Manager, Azure Key Vault) via Spring Cloud integrations, or Kubernetes Secrets injected as env vars/files. Encrypt config at rest, rotate keys regularly, and scope access (least privilege). For JWT, prefer asymmetric keys (only public keys distributed). Audit access to secrets. Avoid logging secrets and exclude them from Actuator /env. Local dev: git-ignored profile files or a local Vault.

http.oauth2Login(Customizer.withDefaults());
// properties:
// spring.security.oauth2.client.registration.google.client-id=...
//                                            .client-secret=...

Spring handles the full OIDC Authorization Code flow: redirect to the provider, handle the callback, exchange the code for tokens, fetch userinfo, and create an authenticated session with an OAuth2User principal. You map provider attributes to your app's user (often a custom OAuth2UserService that creates/links a local account). Minimal code for "Login with Google/GitHub".

You can register multiple SecurityFilterChain beans with @Order and securityMatcher to apply different rules per path. Typical setup: one chain for /api/** (stateless, JWT/Bearer, CSRF off) and another for browser routes (/** form login, session, CSRF on). The first matching chain handles the request. This cleanly separates API and web security concerns within one application.

@Test @WithMockUser(roles = "ADMIN")
void adminCanDelete() throws Exception {
    mvc.perform(delete("/admin/users/1")).andExpect(status().isNoContent());
}
@Test void anonymousIs401() throws Exception {
    mvc.perform(get("/api/secure")).andExpect(status().isUnauthorized());
}
// JWT: .with(jwt().authorities(...)) ; OAuth2: .with(oauth2Login())

Use spring-security-test: @WithMockUser/@WithUserDetails for principals, and MockMvc request post-processors (jwt(), oauth2Login(), csrf()) to simulate auth and CSRF tokens.

@WithMockUser(username="x", roles={"ADMIN"}) fabricates a fake principal with the given authorities — fast, no DB. @WithUserDetails("realUser") loads an actual user through your UserDetailsService — exercises your real user-loading logic and gives an authentic principal (custom UserDetails fields). Use mock for simple role checks; use UserDetails when your authorization depends on real user attributes/custom principal types.

Session-based: Spring's logout handler invalidates the HttpSession, clears the SecurityContext, and deletes the session/remember-me cookies — straightforward. JWT (stateless): there's no server session to kill, so "logout" means the client discards the token; for real invalidation you need a denylist (until expiry) or a per-user token version bumped on logout. For OIDC, also call the provider's end-session/RP-initiated logout endpoint. Always clear client storage and revoke refresh tokens.

Clickjacking: an attacker embeds your site in a transparent iframe over their page, tricking users into clicking actions they didn't intend. Spring Security mitigates it by default with the X-Frame-Options: DENY header (your pages can't be framed). For modern, granular control use the Content-Security-Policy frame-ancestors directive. If you legitimately need framing (e.g. a trusted partner), set SAMEORIGIN or allow specific ancestors — never disable the protection blindly.

  • Validate type by content (magic bytes), not just extension/Content-Type (both spoofable).
  • Limit size (spring.servlet.multipart.max-file-size) to prevent DoS.
  • Store outside the web root with generated names (avoid path traversal — sanitize filenames, never trust user-supplied paths).
  • Serve downloads with correct Content-Disposition and Content-Type (avoid executing as HTML).
  • Scan for malware; restrict permissions; consider a separate domain/CDN for user content.
  • Encoding (Base64, URL) — reversible, no secret, for data representation/transport. Not security.
  • Encryption (AES, RSA) — reversible with a key, for confidentiality (you can decrypt).
  • Hashing (SHA-256, BCrypt) — one-way, not reversible, for integrity/passwords.

Common interview trap: passwords are hashed (with salt, slow algorithm), not encrypted; JWT payloads are encoded (Base64), not encrypted — so don't put secrets in them.

  1. User requests reset by email; respond identically whether or not the account exists (anti-enumeration).
  2. Generate a cryptographically random, single-use, short-lived token; store its hash (not the token) with an expiry.
  3. Email a link containing the token (over HTTPS).
  4. On submission, validate the token hash + expiry, let the user set a new password, then invalidate the token and any existing sessions/refresh tokens.
  5. Rate-limit requests; never email the password.
http.sessionManagement(s -> s
    .maximumSessions(1)                 // one active session per user
    .maxSessionsPreventsLogin(false));  // new login kicks out the old session

Useful to enforce "one device at a time" or limit account sharing. maxSessionsPreventsLogin(true) instead rejects the new login until the old expires. Requires session registry support (and a shared store like Spring Session + Redis in clustered deployments). Not applicable to stateless JWT setups.

Spring Session externalizes the HttpSession to a shared store (Redis, JDBC, Hazelcast) instead of the in-memory servlet container session. Benefits: sessions survive restarts, work across a cluster without sticky sessions (any instance can serve any request), and enable concurrent-session control in distributed apps. Add spring-session-data-redis and it transparently backs HttpSession. Essential for scaling stateful (session-based) apps horizontally.

When access depends on runtime attributes (resource ownership, department, time, data classification) rather than fixed roles, use ABAC: evaluate a policy combining subject + resource + action + context. In Spring, implement via @PreAuthorize calling a policy bean (@authz.canAccess(#resource, authentication)), a custom AuthorizationManager (Spring Security 6), or a dedicated policy engine (OPA/Open Policy Agent) for complex/externalized rules. ABAC scales better than role explosion when permissions are data-dependent.

In standard TLS only the server presents a certificate; in mutual TLS both sides authenticate with certificates. The server verifies the client's cert, establishing transport-level identity without passwords/tokens. Used for service-to-service auth in zero-trust networks (service meshes like Istio automate it via sidecars), high-security B2B APIs, and IoT. In Spring, configure client-auth on the connector and map the client cert's subject to a principal (x509()). Strong identity, but cert lifecycle/rotation is operationally heavy.

Spring Security publishes authentication and authorization events (AuthenticationSuccessEvent, AuthenticationFailureBadCredentialsEvent, AuthorizationDeniedEvent). Listen with @EventListener to write structured audit logs (who, what, when, from where, outcome). Log security-relevant actions (login, logout, permission changes, sensitive data access) to a tamper-resistant store, include correlation IDs, and never log credentials/tokens/PII. Audit logs support incident response, compliance (GDPR/SOX), and intrusion detection.

Gateway-level auth (coarse: authenticate the token, basic route-level checks) centralizes the entry point and offloads work. But relying on it alone violates defense in depth — a compromised/internal caller bypasses it. Each service should still validate the token and enforce its own fine-grained, resource-level authorization (zero trust — never trust the network). Best practice: gateway does authentication + coarse filtering; each service independently validates and authorizes business operations and data ownership.

  • Identity: a central authorization server (Keycloak/Okta/Cognito) issues OIDC tokens via Authorization Code + PKCE for users.
  • Gateway: authenticates the token, does coarse routing/rate limiting, relays the JWT downstream.
  • Each service: a stateless resource server validating the JWT against the issuer's JWKS (asymmetric), enforcing fine-grained + ownership authorization (zero trust).
  • Service-to-service: Client Credentials tokens or mTLS (service mesh); never trust the network.
  • Cross-cutting: short-lived access + rotating refresh tokens, token version/denylist for revocation, audit logging, secrets in a vault, propagate correlation IDs.

Classic IDOR / broken object-level authorization — the endpoint authenticates but doesn't verify ownership. Immediate fix: enforce ownership on that endpoint (findByIdAndUserId or @PostAuthorize("returnObject.ownerId == authentication.principal.id")). System-wide: audit every endpoint that takes a resource ID, add ownership/tenant checks as a standard (a reusable AuthorizationManager or aspect), prefer query-level scoping (always filter by owner) over post-checks, add automated tests for cross-user access, and treat object-level authz as a checklist item in code review. Don't rely on unguessable IDs (UUIDs) as the only defense.

Pure stateless JWTs stay valid until expiry. Options: (1) Short access tokens (1–5 min) + refresh tokens — limit the window; revoke the refresh token to cut renewal (delayed but simple). (2) Token denylist in Redis keyed by JWT id (jti) until expiry — instant revocation but reintroduces a per-request lookup (partial statefulness). (3) Per-user token version in a fast store, embedded as a claim; bump on logout/ban → all old tokens invalid (one cheap lookup, cache-friendly). Trade-off: instant revocation requires some shared state — choose the cheapest store and shortest token lifetime that meets your security SLA.

Use one-time-use refresh tokens with rotation: each refresh issues a new refresh token and invalidates the old one (stored server-side, hashed, with a family/lineage id). Theft detection: if an already-used (old) refresh token is presented again, it means two parties hold the lineage → revoke the entire token family and force re-login. Bind tokens to a device/client fingerprint, set absolute + idle expiry, store only hashes, and audit refresh events. This is the OAuth2 recommended pattern for public clients.

  • Rate limiting per IP and per account (distributed, e.g. Redis token bucket); 429 with backoff.
  • Account lockout / progressive delays after repeated failures (with anti-enumeration).
  • CAPTCHA / proof-of-work triggered on risk signals.
  • Breached-password checks (HaveIBeenPwned k-anonymity) to reject known-leaked passwords.
  • MFA as the strong backstop; device/IP reputation and anomaly detection.
  • WAF/bot management at the edge; alerting on failure spikes; never reveal which field was wrong.

No single control suffices — layer them and monitor.

Two causes: (1) extending OncePerRequestFilter but the filter also got registered as a Boot bean (auto-detected by the servlet container) and added to the security chain → registered twice. Fix: declare it with @Bean but wrap in a FilterRegistrationBean with setEnabled(false) to stop the container auto-registration, and add it only inside the security chain. (2) Forwards/includes re-trigger plain filters — which is exactly why OncePerRequestFilter exists (it guards against multiple executions within one request). Always extend it for security filters.

  • Method security not enabled — missing @EnableMethodSecurity.
  • Self-invocation — the method is called from within the same bean, bypassing the AOP proxy (same caveat as @Transactional). Fix: call via another bean or self-inject the proxy.
  • Not public / not a Spring bean — private/package methods or new-created objects aren't proxied.
  • final method/class — CGLIB can't override it.
  • Wrong import or expression always evaluating true.

Confirm the bean is proxied and the call crosses a proxy boundary.

Defense in depth on the tenant boundary: (1) Authenticate and derive tenantId from the token (a trusted claim — never from a client-supplied parameter). (2) Scope every query by tenantId automatically (Hibernate filter / @TenantId / a mandatory repository predicate) so a forgotten filter can't leak data. (3) Stronger isolation options: schema-per-tenant or DB-per-tenant routing. (4) Enforce at multiple layers (API authz + data filter + DB row-level security). (5) Test cross-tenant access negatively. The cardinal rule: tenant context comes from the verified identity, and data access is filtered by it unconditionally.

Zero trust = authenticate and authorize every call regardless of network location. Mechanisms: mTLS (mutual cert auth, automated by a service mesh like Istio with short-lived rotating certs) for transport identity; OAuth2 Client Credentials tokens for application-level identity/scopes; combine both for strong + granular control. Each service validates the caller's identity and checks it's authorized for the specific operation. Add network policies, encrypt in transit, rotate credentials automatically, and audit. Never grant access based solely on being "inside the VPC".

Use multiple SecurityFilterChain beans with @Order and path/header matchers: a high-priority chain for /api/v2/** or requests bearing an Authorization: Bearer header (stateless JWT), and a lower-priority chain for legacy session routes (form login, CSRF, session). Both resolve to the same authorities model so downstream authorization is uniform. Migrate clients gradually (Strangler-style), monitor usage of the legacy chain, and retire it once traffic drops to zero. This avoids a risky big-bang auth switch.

JWT payloads are Base64-encoded, not encrypted — anyone with the token can read all claims, and tokens often sit in logs, browser storage, and proxies. So never put passwords, PII, or secrets in claims. For truly sensitive data: keep the token minimal (subject + roles), fetch sensitive details server-side per request; or use JWE (encrypted JWT) when claims must be confidential in transit/storage. Also keep tokens small (they're sent on every request), validate aud/iss/exp, and prefer opaque tokens + introspection when you need full server-side control.

JWT (self-contained): validated locally (signature + claims) → fast, no network call, scales statelessly; but hard to revoke and can grow large. Opaque token (reference): a random string the resource server introspects against the auth server (/introspect) each request → instant revocation and central control, but adds a network round-trip and load on the auth server (mitigated by caching). Choice: JWT for high-throughput, short-lived-token systems; opaque tokens when revocation/centralized control matters more than latency. Spring supports both (oauth2ResourceServer().jwt() vs .opaqueToken()).

  • Headers: ensure HSTS, X-Content-Type-Options, a strict Content-Security-Policy, X-Frame-Options/frame-ancestors, Referrer-Policy, and remove Server/version banners.
  • Errors: return generic error bodies (no stack traces, SQL, or framework versions) via a global @RestControllerAdvice / ProblemDetail; disable detailed error pages (server.error.include-stacktrace=never); hide Actuator details.
  • Disable debug/dev profiles in prod; ensure consistent generic auth messages (anti-enumeration); log details server-side only.

Server-Side Request Forgery: an attacker makes your server request internal/metadata endpoints (e.g. cloud metadata 169.254.169.254) by supplying a malicious URL. Defenses: allowlist permitted hosts/schemes (no file://, gopher://); resolve and validate the IP after DNS, blocking private/link-local/loopback ranges (and re-validate to prevent DNS rebinding); disable redirects or re-validate each hop; run egress through a controlled proxy with network policies; never reflect raw responses. Also remove cloud metadata access from the workload's network. SSRF is a top cloud-era risk.

Move from hardcoded roles to a policy-based (ABAC) model: store role→permission and resource-access rules in a DB/config, load them into a fast cache, and evaluate per request. In Spring, implement a custom AuthorizationManager or a @PreAuthorize("@policy.permit(#resource, authentication, 'edit')") bean that consults the rules. For complex/externalized policy, integrate OPA (Open Policy Agent) with Rego policies, decoupling authorization logic from code and enabling runtime updates without redeploy. Cache decisions, audit them, and fail closed on policy errors.

The default SecurityContextHolder uses a ThreadLocal, so async/pooled threads lose the context (and could leak a previous request's context). For @Async: set SecurityContextHolder.setStrategyName(MODE_INHERITABLETHREADLOCAL) (limited with pools) or wrap the executor with DelegatingSecurityContextExecutor / a TaskDecorator that copies and clears the context. For reactive (WebFlux), the context lives in the Reactor Context (not ThreadLocal) — accessed via ReactiveSecurityContextHolder. Always clear the context after the task to avoid bleed between requests on reused threads.

Don't rely on network obscurity. Layer controls: (1) require a service identity — a Client Credentials token with a specific scope/audience, or mTLS client cert — and authorize on that (hasAuthority('SCOPE_internal')), explicitly rejecting user tokens. (2) Bind the endpoint to a separate internal listener/port not exposed by the gateway/ingress. (3) Network policies / firewall rules restricting source. (4) Validate the token's aud claim equals this service. Combining service-identity authz + network restriction + audience validation enforces "services only" robustly.

Use Spring's DelegatingPasswordEncoder with prefixed hashes ({sha256}, {bcrypt}). Migration strategy: on the next successful login, you have the plaintext momentarily — re-hash it with BCrypt and update the stored value (transparent rehash). For accounts that never log in, you can't re-hash without plaintext, so optionally wrap existing hashes (bcrypt(sha256(pw))) to immediately strengthen them, or force a password reset. Meanwhile rotate to a strong algorithm, add salt, and treat the old scheme as compromised (assume the hashes may be cracked).

  • Generate high-entropy keys; store only a hash (treat like passwords) — show the key once.
  • Use a prefix (pk_live_...) for identification/secret-scanning detection.
  • Scope each key (permissions, allowed IPs/origins), support multiple keys + rotation without downtime, and easy revocation.
  • Per-key rate limiting and usage analytics; expiry dates.
  • Transmit only over HTTPS in a header (never URL — logged); audit usage; alert on anomalies.

For richer needs, prefer OAuth2 Client Credentials over raw API keys.

A "confused deputy" is a privileged component tricked into misusing its authority on behalf of an attacker. In token terms: Service A receives a token meant for it, then forwards that same token to Service B, which accepts it — even though it was never intended for B. Defense: validate the aud (audience) claim — each service accepts only tokens minted for it. For delegated calls, use token exchange (RFC 8693) to mint a new, correctly-scoped token for the downstream audience rather than blindly relaying. Always check aud, iss, and scopes.

Step-up requires stronger auth for high-risk operations (transfer money, change email) even if already logged in. Design: track the authentication strength/recency (e.g. an acr claim or a "last MFA at" timestamp). When a sensitive endpoint is hit, check whether the current auth meets the required assurance level; if not, challenge for MFA/re-auth and only then elevate the session/token (a short-lived elevated scope). In Spring, gate the endpoint with a custom AuthorizationManager/@PreAuthorize that verifies the assurance claim, returning a 401 with a step-up challenge otherwise.

  1. Assess exposure — is the vulnerable lib/version actually present and reachable? (SBOM, dependency scan).
  2. Mitigate immediately — apply vendor mitigations/flags, WAF rules, or disable the vulnerable feature while patching.
  3. Patch — upgrade to a fixed version; rebuild and redeploy across all services.
  4. Verify — scan again, test exploitation is blocked.
  5. Prevent recurrence — continuous dependency scanning (OWASP Dependency-Check, Snyk, Dependabot) in CI, an SBOM, and patching SLAs.

Vulnerable/outdated components are an OWASP Top 10 item — automate detection.

Centralize authorization in AuthorizationManagers / a policy bean and emit a structured audit event on every decision (subject, resource, action, decision, reason, timestamp, request/trace id) — Spring Security 6 publishes AuthorizationGrantedEvent/AuthorizationDeniedEvent you can subscribe to. Ship audit logs to an append-only, access-controlled store with retention for compliance; correlate with tracing. Ensure decisions fail closed, are consistent across layers, and never log sensitive payloads. This supports forensics, anomaly detection, and regulatory requirements (who accessed PII when).

Walk the system with STRIDE: Spoofing (strong auth, mTLS, token validation), Tampering (signatures, integrity checks, input validation), Repudiation (audit logs), Information disclosure (encryption in transit/at rest, least data, no verbose errors), Denial of service (rate limiting, timeouts, bulkheads, resource caps), Elevation of privilege (least privilege, authorization at every boundary, no IDOR). Diagram trust boundaries and data flows, enumerate threats per element, rank by risk, and map each to a concrete control + test. Do it early (design phase) and revisit on changes.

  • AuthN: OAuth2/OIDC or JWT resource server (validate sig/iss/aud/exp), strong password hashing (BCrypt/Argon2), MFA, short-lived + rotating tokens.
  • AuthZ: URL + method security, object-level ownership checks (no IDOR), least privilege, tenant isolation, fail closed.
  • Transport/headers: HTTPS/HSTS, CSP, frame/MIME protections, CORS locked down, CSRF correct for the auth model.
  • Input/Output: validation, parameterized queries (no injection), output encoding (no XSS), SSRF protections, file-upload hardening.
  • Data: encryption at rest/in transit, secrets in a vault, no PII/secrets in logs or JWTs.
  • Ops: rate limiting, dependency/CVE scanning + SBOM, Actuator locked down, audit logging, generic error messages, security tests in CI, threat model reviewed.

📨 Apache Kafka — Top 100

ℹ️ Architecture, producers/consumers, partitions, delivery semantics, ordering, Spring Kafka, and real-world scenarios.

Kafka is a distributed, durable, high-throughput event-streaming platform — a append-only commit log you publish events to and many consumers read from independently. It decouples producers from consumers, buffers spikes, retains events for replay, and scales horizontally. Solves: async communication, event-driven architectures, log/metrics pipelines, stream processing, and integrating many systems without point-to-point coupling.

  • Producer — publishes records to topics.
  • Consumer — reads records, usually within a consumer group.
  • Broker — a Kafka server storing data and serving requests; a cluster has many.
  • Topic — a named stream of records, split into partitions.
  • Partition — an ordered, immutable log; the unit of parallelism.
  • ZooKeeper/KRaft — cluster metadata/coordination (KRaft replaces ZooKeeper).

A topic is a logical category of events (e.g. orders). It's physically divided into partitions — each an ordered, append-only log. Partitions enable parallelism (different consumers read different partitions) and scalability (spread across brokers). Order is guaranteed within a partition, not across the whole topic. Each record in a partition has a monotonically increasing offset.

A unique, sequential integer identifying a record's position within a partition. Consumers track which offset they've processed (their "committed offset") so they can resume after a restart. Offsets are per-partition (not global). Kafka itself doesn't delete records when read — it retains them by time/size, and consumers can re-read from any offset (replay).

A set of consumers that cooperatively consume a topic — Kafka assigns each partition to exactly one consumer in the group, so the group as a whole processes all partitions in parallel without duplication. Multiple groups can read the same topic independently (each gets all messages — pub/sub). Scaling a group = adding consumers (up to the partition count). The group id identifies the group; Kafka tracks committed offsets per group.

Ordering is guaranteed only within a single partition. Records with the same key always go to the same partition (via key hashing), so all events for a given key (e.g. an order id) are ordered. Across partitions there's no global order. To preserve per-entity ordering, key by that entity. If you need strict total ordering, you'd need a single partition (sacrificing parallelism).

The key determines the target partition (default: hash(key) % partitions), which controls (a) ordering — same key → same partition → ordered, and (b) co-location — related events land together. With a null key, records are distributed round-robin/sticky across partitions (no ordering guarantee). Choose a key that reflects your ordering/grouping requirement (userId, orderId) — but beware hot keys causing partition skew.

Each partition is replicated across multiple brokers for fault tolerance. The replication factor (e.g. 3) = number of copies. One replica is the leader (handles all reads/writes); the others are followers that replicate it. If the leader's broker dies, a follower is promoted. RF=3 tolerates 2 broker failures. Replication is the foundation of Kafka's durability and availability guarantees.

The leader replica serves all produce/consume for a partition; followers passively replicate it. The ISR (In-Sync Replicas) is the set of replicas (including the leader) that are fully caught up. Only ISR members are eligible to become leader (preventing data loss). Producer durability (acks=all) and min.insync.replicas are defined relative to the ISR — writes succeed only when enough in-sync replicas acknowledge.

Historically ZooKeeper stored cluster metadata (brokers, topics, partition leadership, ACLs) and handled controller election. KRaft (Kafka Raft, KIP-500) removes the ZooKeeper dependency by managing metadata internally via a Raft-based quorum of controllers — simpler ops, faster failover, and better scalability (millions of partitions). KRaft is production-ready and the default going forward; ZooKeeper is being phased out.

KafkaRabbitMQ
ModelDistributed log (pull, retained)Broker queues (push, consume-and-delete)
RetentionKeeps messages (replay)Removed after ack
ThroughputVery high (sequential disk)Lower, lower latency per msg
UseStreaming, event sourcing, logsTask queues, complex routing

Kafka excels at high-throughput streaming + replay; RabbitMQ at flexible routing and per-message workflows.

  • acks=0 — fire and forget; no ack, possible data loss, fastest.
  • acks=1 — leader acknowledges after writing to its log; data lost if the leader dies before followers replicate.
  • acks=all (-1) — leader waits for all in-sync replicas; strongest durability (with min.insync.replicas), slightly higher latency.

For important data use acks=all + min.insync.replicas=2. It's the core durability vs latency knob.

  • At-most-once — messages may be lost, never duplicated (commit offset before processing). Rare.
  • At-least-once — never lost, may be duplicated (process then commit; a crash before commit re-delivers). The common default — requires idempotent consumers.
  • Exactly-once — no loss, no duplicates; achieved with idempotent producer + transactions (within Kafka) or idempotent consumers (to external systems).

Committing an offset records "I've processed up to here" so a restart resumes correctly. Auto-commit (enable.auto.commit=true) periodically commits in the background — simple but risks data loss or reprocessing (it may commit messages you haven't finished processing). Manual commit (commitSync/commitAsync after processing) gives precise control and at-least-once guarantees. Production consumers usually disable auto-commit and commit after successful processing.

// Produce
kafkaTemplate.send("orders", order.getId(), orderJson);

// Consume
@KafkaListener(topics = "orders", groupId = "order-service")
public void handle(ConsumerRecord<String, String> record) {
    process(record.value());
}

KafkaTemplate wraps the producer; @KafkaListener wires a method to a consumer in a group. Spring Boot auto-configures both from spring.kafka.* properties.

A broker is a single Kafka server that stores partition data and handles client requests. A cluster is multiple brokers working together — partitions and their replicas are distributed across brokers for scalability and fault tolerance. One broker acts as the controller (manages partition leadership and cluster state). Clients connect via bootstrap servers and are routed to the right broker for each partition leader.

Unlike queues, Kafka retains messages for a configured period regardless of consumption, enabling replay. Controlled by retention.ms (time, e.g. 7 days) and/or retention.bytes (size) per topic. After the limit, old segments are deleted. Alternatively, log compaction retains the latest value per key indefinitely. Consumers can re-read anytime within the retention window — a key differentiator from traditional MQs.

A retention policy (cleanup.policy=compact) that keeps only the latest value for each key, deleting older values for that key over time — so the log becomes a snapshot of current state per key. Ideal for changelog/state topics (e.g. "current user profile", database CDC) where you only need the most recent state, not full history. A null value (tombstone) marks a key for deletion. Used heavily by Kafka Streams state stores.

Partitions are the unit of parallelism: within a consumer group, each partition is consumed by exactly one consumer. So max useful consumers = partition count. If consumers > partitions, the extras sit idle. If consumers < partitions, some consumers handle multiple partitions. To scale a consumer group's throughput, you must have enough partitions — choose partition count up front with future scaling in mind (increasing it later breaks key→partition stability).

Kafka stores bytes; you choose a serializer/deserializer (serde). Common: JSON (human-readable, no schema enforcement), Avro (compact binary + schema, the classic choice with Schema Registry), Protobuf, and plain String/byte[]. Binary formats with a Schema Registry are preferred for production: smaller payloads and enforced schema compatibility across producers/consumers. The producer and consumer must agree on the serde.

A service (Confluent) that stores and versions message schemas (Avro/Protobuf/JSON Schema) and enforces compatibility rules (backward/forward/full) when schemas evolve. Producers register/validate schemas; messages carry a small schema ID instead of the full schema (compact). Consumers fetch the schema by ID to deserialize. It prevents a producer from breaking consumers with an incompatible change — essential governance for evolving event contracts.

A separate topic where messages that repeatedly fail processing are sent, so the main consumer isn't blocked and bad messages aren't lost. After N retries, the consumer publishes the failed record (with error metadata) to <topic>.DLT for later inspection, fixing, or reprocessing. Spring Kafka provides DeadLetterPublishingRecoverer + DefaultErrorHandler to automate this. Prevents "poison pill" messages from halting the pipeline.

The difference between the latest offset produced to a partition and the consumer group's committed offset — i.e. how many messages the consumer is behind. Growing lag means consumers can't keep up with producers (under-provisioned, slow processing, or stuck). It's the #1 health metric for Kafka consumers; monitor it (Burrow, Kafka exporter + Prometheus) and alert. Fixes: add consumers/partitions, speed up processing, or batch.

Kafka consumers pull (poll) messages at their own pace, rather than the broker pushing. Benefits: consumers control the rate (natural backpressure — a slow consumer just polls less, no overwhelming), can batch efficiently, and can replay by seeking offsets. The broker stays simple (it doesn't track per-consumer delivery state beyond committed offsets). RabbitMQ, by contrast, pushes to consumers. Pull is why Kafka handles slow/variable consumers gracefully.

spring.kafka:
  bootstrap-servers: localhost:9092
  consumer:
    group-id: order-service
    auto-offset-reset: earliest
    enable-auto-commit: false
  producer:
    acks: all

With spring-kafka on the classpath, Boot creates KafkaTemplate, producer/consumer factories, and listener containers from these properties. You then just use @KafkaListener and KafkaTemplate — wiring is automatic.

Rebalancing reassigns partitions among consumers in a group. Triggered when a consumer joins or leaves (scaling, crash, or missing heartbeats), partitions are added, or the consumer is too slow (exceeds max.poll.interval.ms). During a "stop-the-world" rebalance, consumption pauses while partitions are redistributed — causing latency spikes. Frequent rebalances (from slow processing or long GC) are a common production pain; mitigated by tuning poll intervals and using cooperative/incremental rebalancing.

Eager (old default): on any membership change, all consumers revoke all partitions and reassign from scratch — a full stop-the-world pause. Cooperative (CooperativeStickyAssignor): only the partitions that actually need to move are revoked; unaffected consumers keep processing — much shorter pauses. It's the modern recommended strategy, especially for large groups. Configure via partition.assignment.strategy. Reduces the cost of scaling and transient failures.

  • Producer: acks=all, retries high, enable.idempotence=true (avoids dup-on-retry), handle send failures.
  • Broker/topic: replication factor ≥ 3, min.insync.replicas=2, disable unclean leader election.
  • Consumer: disable auto-commit, process then commit (at-least-once), make processing idempotent.

Loss happens at the weakest link — e.g. acks=1 + leader crash, or committing offsets before processing. The chain must be durable at every hop.

enable.idempotence=true makes the producer assign each message a sequence number per partition; the broker deduplicates retries so a network retry doesn't write the same record twice. It prevents duplicates caused by producer retries (without it, acks=all + retry can double-write). It also guarantees ordering per partition even with retries (max.in.flight ≤ 5). It's the foundation of exactly-once and is now enabled by default in recent Kafka versions.

Transactions let a producer atomically write to multiple partitions/topics and commit consumer offsets together — so a "consume-process-produce" loop is exactly-once within Kafka. Enabled via transactional.id + initTransactions/beginTransaction/commitTransaction; consumers read with isolation.level=read_committed (skip aborted records). Crucial caveat: EOS holds for Kafka-to-Kafka flows. Writing to an external system (DB, email) still needs idempotency/outbox — Kafka transactions don't extend across non-Kafka systems.

Since at-least-once delivery can re-deliver, the consumer must make reprocessing harmless. Techniques: track processed message IDs in a dedup store (DB unique constraint on a business/message key) and skip seen ones; design operations to be naturally idempotent (upsert by key, set-state-to-X instead of increment); or use the message's (topic, partition, offset) or a producer-supplied unique key. The principle: at-least-once + idempotent consumer ≈ effectively-once, far simpler than full Kafka transactions to external systems.

min.insync.replicas (a topic/broker config) sets the minimum number of in-sync replicas that must acknowledge a write when the producer uses acks=all. With RF=3 and min.insync.replicas=2, a write needs at least 2 replicas (leader + 1 follower) — so you can lose 1 broker and still write/never lose data. If too few replicas are in sync, the producer gets NotEnoughReplicasException (fail fast rather than risk loss). It's the durability counterpart to acks=all.

If all in-sync replicas are down, unclean leader election (unclean.leader.election.enable=true) allows an out-of-sync replica to become leader — restoring availability but losing the unreplicated messages (data loss). Disabling it (the safe default) means the partition stays unavailable until an ISR member returns — choosing consistency over availability. For data you can't afford to lose (financial), keep it disabled; enable only if availability strictly outweighs occasional loss.

Order is per-partition, and each partition goes to one consumer in a group — so key by the entity that needs ordering (e.g. accountId), guaranteeing all its events land in one partition and are processed in order. You still scale by having many partitions (many keys spread across them) and many consumers. Trade-off: a single key's throughput is bounded by one partition/consumer; very hot keys can't be parallelized without breaking their ordering. Choose partition count and keys deliberately.

It enables more parallelism going forward, but changes the key→partition mapping (hash(key) % N with a new N) — so a given key may now route to a different partition than its historical records. This breaks per-key ordering across the change boundary and can disrupt log-compacted/state topics and stream-processing assumptions. You also can't decrease partitions. Best practice: over-provision partitions at creation; avoid changing it on keyed/ordered topics in production.

@Bean
DefaultErrorHandler errorHandler(KafkaTemplate<?,?> template) {
    var recoverer = new DeadLetterPublishingRecoverer(template); // → topic.DLT
    return new DefaultErrorHandler(recoverer,
        new ExponentialBackOff(1000L, 2.0)); // retry with backoff, then DLT
}

DefaultErrorHandler retries failed records (configurable backoff/attempts), then hands persistent failures to a recoverer (commonly publishing to a DLT). You can classify exceptions as retryable vs non-retryable (don't retry deserialization errors — send straight to DLT). Prevents poison pills from blocking the partition.

A message that always fails processing (e.g. malformed/undeserializable) — without handling, the consumer retries it forever and blocks the whole partition (offset never advances, lag grows). Handling: catch deserialization errors with an ErrorHandlingDeserializer (wraps the real deserializer, turns failures into a recoverable record), classify it as non-retryable, and route it to a DLT so the consumer moves on. Then inspect/fix the bad messages offline. Never let one bad record halt a partition.

Time/size retention (cleanup.policy=delete) keeps a rolling window of all events — use for event streams/logs where history matters but old data can expire (e.g. page-views, last 7 days). Compaction (cleanup.policy=compact) keeps the latest value per key forever — use for state/changelog topics where you need the current snapshot per entity (user profiles, config, CDC). You can combine both (compact,delete). Choose based on whether you need history (delete) or current state (compact).

Balance throughput vs overhead. Estimate from target throughput ÷ per-partition throughput (and the max consumers you'll want, since consumers ≤ partitions). More partitions = more parallelism but also more open files, more rebalance cost, higher end-to-end latency, and more memory on brokers/clients. Rule of thumb: provision for peak/future load (you can't easily reduce later, and increasing breaks key mapping), but avoid thousands of tiny partitions. Consider per-broker partition limits.

The producer buffers records and sends them in batches per partition for efficiency. batch.size (max bytes per batch) and linger.ms (wait time to fill a batch) trade latency for throughput: higher linger.ms = bigger batches = better throughput/compression but added latency. buffer.memory caps total buffering; compression.type (lz4/zstd) shrinks network/disk use. Tune for your workload: low-latency apps use small linger; high-throughput pipelines batch aggressively.

Kafka Streams is a client library for stream processing — transformations, aggregations, joins, windowing — directly on Kafka topics, with built-in stateful processing (local state stores backed by compacted changelog topics) and exactly-once support. Unlike a plain consumer (which just reads and you write all logic), Streams gives a high-level DSL (KStream/KTable), automatic scaling/fault tolerance via partitions, and managed state. Use it for real-time analytics, enrichment, and CEP without a separate cluster (no Spark/Flink needed for many cases).

KStream = an unbounded stream of events (each record is an independent fact; "append" semantics) — e.g. clicks, transactions. KTable = a changelog representing the latest value per key (a continuously-updated table; "upsert" semantics) — e.g. current account balance. Stream = facts that happened; Table = current state. They interconvert (aggregate a stream → table; a table's changes → stream) and join in different ways (stream-table enrichment, table-table joins). The duality is central to Kafka Streams.

Services communicate by publishing and reacting to events rather than direct synchronous calls — decoupling producers from consumers in time and knowledge. Kafka enables it as the durable event backbone: a service emits "OrderPlaced" without knowing who consumes it; multiple services (inventory, notification, analytics) react independently. Benefits: loose coupling, independent scaling/deployment, resilience (events buffered if a consumer is down), replay, and easy addition of new consumers. Trade-off: eventual consistency and harder end-to-end tracing.

Kafka is the event transport for choreography Sagas — each service completes a local transaction and publishes an event that triggers the next step (and compensating events on failure). The Outbox pattern solves the dual-write problem feeding Kafka: write the event to an outbox table in the same DB transaction as the business change, then a relay/CDC (Debezium) publishes it to Kafka reliably. Together they give reliable, consistent event-driven workflows across microservices without distributed transactions.

A framework for streaming data between Kafka and external systems without custom code, using reusable connectors. Source connectors pull data into Kafka (e.g. Debezium CDC from a database, files, APIs); sink connectors push Kafka data out (to Elasticsearch, S3, JDBC, data warehouses). It handles scaling, offset tracking, and fault tolerance in a distributed worker cluster. Use it instead of hand-writing producers/consumers for standard integrations — config over code.

  • Consumer lag (the top signal — are consumers keeping up?).
  • Under-replicated partitions & offline partitions (broker health/durability).
  • Broker request/produce/fetch latency, disk usage, network throughput.
  • ISR shrink/expand rate, leader election rate, controller health.
  • Producer error/retry rate, batch size; consumer rebalance frequency.

Tools: JMX metrics → Prometheus/Grafana, Burrow/Kafka-exporter for lag, Confluent Control Center / Conduktor. Alert on lag growth and under-replicated partitions.
