At-least-once: process, then commit the offset — a crash between produce and commit re-delivers, so downstream may see duplicates (handle with idempotency). Exactly-once: use Kafka transactions to atomically produce the output and commit the input offset, with read_committed consumers downstream — no duplicates, no loss, entirely within Kafka. EOS adds latency/complexity and only covers Kafka-internal flows; many systems choose at-least-once + idempotent consumers as a simpler, sufficient guarantee.

Because consumers pull, back-pressure is natural — a slow consumer simply polls less, and unconsumed messages stay durably in the topic (bounded by retention). Tuning: max.poll.records (batch size per poll) and max.poll.interval.ms (max time to process a batch before being considered dead → rebalance). If processing is slow, reduce max.poll.records or pause/resume the consumer. The danger is processing taking longer than max.poll.interval.ms, triggering repeated rebalances — offload slow work or increase the interval.

  • Encryption in transit — TLS/SSL between clients and brokers (and inter-broker).
  • Authentication — SASL (SCRAM, GSSAPI/Kerberos, OAUTHBEARER) or mTLS client certs.
  • Authorization — ACLs per topic/group/operation (who can produce/consume/admin).
  • Encryption at rest — disk-level/volume encryption; and network isolation.

Apply least privilege (per-service credentials and ACLs), rotate credentials, and audit. Cloud-managed Kafka (MSK, Confluent Cloud) handles much of this.

The group coordinator (a broker) manages a consumer group: tracks membership/heartbeats, triggers rebalances, and assigns partitions. Committed offsets are stored in an internal compacted topic, __consumer_offsets (not ZooKeeper since modern versions) — keyed by (group, topic, partition), so the latest committed offset per key is retained. On restart/rebalance, consumers read their last committed offset from here to resume. This makes offset management durable and replicated like any other topic.

Log End Offset (LEO) — the offset of the next record to be written to a replica's log. High Water Mark (HW) — the highest offset that has been replicated to all in-sync replicas; consumers can only read up to the HW (committed records). Records between HW and the leader's LEO exist but aren't yet "safe" (not fully replicated), so they're invisible to consumers — this prevents reading data that could be lost if the leader fails before replication.

  • Sequential disk writes/reads — appending to a log is near memory-speed, far faster than random I/O.
  • OS page cache — Kafka relies on the OS cache rather than a JVM heap cache (no GC pressure, warm reads served from memory).
  • Zero-copy (sendfile) — data goes from page cache straight to the network socket, bypassing user space.
  • Batching + compression on produce/consume; partitioning for horizontal scale.

These design choices let a single broker handle very high throughput cheaply.

subscribe(topics) joins a consumer group — Kafka dynamically assigns partitions and rebalances automatically (the normal mode for scalable groups). assign(partitions) manually pins the consumer to specific partitions with no group management or rebalancing — used when you need precise control (e.g. exactly-once with manual offset control, reading a specific partition, or a single-consumer replay tool). Most apps use subscribe; assign is for specialized low-level scenarios.

Because Kafka retains messages, you can re-read by resetting offsets: use seek(partition, offset) / seekToBeginning() in the consumer, or reset a group's committed offsets with kafka-consumer-groups --reset-offsets --to-earliest/--to-datetime ... --execute (group must be inactive). Use cases: reprocess after a bug fix, backfill a new consumer/read model, or recover from bad downstream writes. Ensure processing is idempotent, since replay re-delivers. This replay capability is a core Kafka advantage over queues.

Determines where a consumer starts when there's no committed offset for its group (new group) or the committed offset is out of range (expired). Options: earliest (start from the beginning — process all retained history), latest (only new messages from now — skip backlog; the default), none (throw an error if no offset found). Choose earliest for analytics/full processing, latest for "only care about new events". A common surprise: a new consumer group with latest misses existing messages.

commitSync() blocks until the broker confirms the commit and retries on transient failure — reliable but adds latency. commitAsync() fires the commit without blocking (with an optional callback) — higher throughput but won't retry (a later commit supersedes it). Common pattern: use commitAsync during normal processing for speed, and a final commitSync on shutdown/rebalance to guarantee the last offsets are committed. With manual commits, commit after successful processing for at-least-once.

A consumer sends heartbeats (on a background thread, every heartbeat.interval.ms) to the group coordinator. If no heartbeat arrives within session.timeout.ms, the consumer is considered dead and a rebalance reassigns its partitions. Separately, max.poll.interval.ms bounds the time between poll() calls — if processing a batch takes too long, the consumer is removed even though heartbeats continue (a common cause of unexpected rebalances). Tune these so slow-but-alive consumers aren't wrongly evicted.

Use a Schema Registry with a compatibility policy. Backward compatible (new schema reads old data — add optional fields with defaults, don't remove required fields) lets consumers upgrade first. Forward compatible (old schema reads new data) lets producers upgrade first. Full = both. Rules of thumb: add fields with defaults, never rename/retype, deprecate before removing. The registry rejects incompatible schemas at publish time, preventing a producer from breaking consumers. Plan rollout order based on the compatibility mode.

To reliably publish events without the dual-write problem: the service writes business data and an outbox row in one DB transaction. Debezium (a Kafka Connect source connector) reads the database's transaction log (CDC) and streams committed outbox rows to Kafka — guaranteeing the event is published iff the transaction committed, with no application polling and no lost events. Consumers (idempotent) react. This is the production-standard way to bridge a relational DB and Kafka consistently.

Windowing groups stream records into time buckets for aggregation. Types: tumbling (fixed, non-overlapping, e.g. count per 1-min bucket), hopping (fixed size, overlapping by a hop interval), sliding (windows defined by record timestamp proximity), and session (dynamic windows separated by inactivity gaps — e.g. user sessions). Combined with grace periods for late events and event-time semantics, windowing powers real-time analytics (rolling counts, rate calculations) directly in Kafka Streams.

Event time — when the event actually occurred (embedded in the record); the correct basis for analytics, but events can arrive late/out of order. Ingestion time — when Kafka received it (broker timestamp). Processing time — when the stream app processes it (clock at compute) — simplest but skewed by delays/replays. Stream processing prefers event time with watermarks/grace periods to handle late data correctly. Choosing the wrong time semantics produces wrong aggregations (e.g. counting replayed events as "now").

High throughput: increase batch.size, set linger.ms (e.g. 10–100ms to fill batches), enable compression (lz4/zstd), raise buffer.memory, allow more in-flight requests. Low latency: linger.ms=0 (send immediately), smaller batches, possibly acks=1 (if durability allows). They trade off — bigger batches amortize overhead but add delay. Keep enable.idempotence=true for safety. Always measure under realistic load rather than guessing.

  • Scale out — add consumers (up to partition count); if at the limit, add partitions.
  • Speed up processing — batch DB writes, async I/O, remove blocking calls, optimize the hot path.
  • Parallelize within a consumer carefully (worker pool) while preserving per-key order and offset-commit correctness.
  • Decouple — offload slow work to another topic/stage so the consumer just hands off.

Monitor lag and tune max.poll.records. Persistent lag means under-provisioning or a too-slow processing design.

A record with a non-null key and a null value, used in log-compacted topics to signal deletion of that key. During compaction, the tombstone removes all prior values for the key, and after a retention period the tombstone itself is removed — so the key disappears entirely from the compacted log. Essential for CDC/changelog topics to represent "this entity was deleted" (e.g. GDPR erasure). Consumers must handle null-value records as deletions, not errors.

Kafka transactions don't extend to external systems, so use one of: (1) idempotent writes — upsert keyed by a business id, so reprocessing is harmless (effectively-once); (2) store the processed offset in the same DB transaction as the data write (transactional offset storage) — on restart, resume from the DB-stored offset, guaranteeing no double-apply; (3) a dedup table keyed by message id with a unique constraint. The pattern is: make the external write and the offset/dedup atomic or idempotent. Pure Kafka EOS alone won't cover the DB side.

MirrorMaker 2 (built on Kafka Connect) replicates topics between Kafka clusters across data centers/regions for disaster recovery, geo-distribution, aggregation, or migration. It replicates data, consumer offsets, and topic configs, and handles offset translation between clusters. Patterns: active-passive (DR failover), active-active (each region serves locally), hub-and-spoke (aggregate to a central cluster). Considerations: replication lag, avoiding loops (topic renaming/prefixing), and offset mapping during failover.

Within one topic, key by the entity to keep its events ordered in one partition. Across multiple topics/stages, order isn't automatically preserved, so: keep the same partitioning key throughout the pipeline (consistent keying so each stage processes a given entity on one partition/consumer), or carry a sequence number in the event and have consumers detect/handle out-of-order (buffer, reorder, or reject). For strict cross-stage ordering, use Kafka Streams with the same key or a single ordered topic. Often, designing for causal rather than total ordering is more practical.

An event log (delete retention) stores the full history of what happened — append-only facts, ideal for event sourcing, audit, and deriving multiple read models. A compacted topic stores only the current state per key — a materialized snapshot, ideal for rebuilding caches/state stores quickly and for "latest value" lookups. Many systems use both: an event-sourced history topic (delete) plus compacted state/changelog topics for fast state recovery. Choose by whether you need history or current state.

@KafkaListener(topics = "events", batch = "true")
public void handle(List<ConsumerRecord<String,String>> records, Acknowledgment ack) {
    bulkProcess(records);   // process whole poll batch (efficient DB writes)
    ack.acknowledge();      // manual commit after success (AckMode.MANUAL)
}

Batch listeners hand you the whole poll at once (fewer DB round-trips, higher throughput). With AckMode.MANUAL/MANUAL_IMMEDIATE you control exactly when offsets commit — commit only after the batch is durably processed for at-least-once safety.

Many topics: clear separation of concerns, fine-grained ACLs/retention, but more metadata overhead, more partitions to manage, and harder to maintain ordering across related events. Few topics (with keys/headers to distinguish): simpler operations and easier cross-event ordering, but coarser access control and mixed concerns. Guideline: one topic per event type/bounded context, sized partitions for throughput. Avoid both extremes — topic-per-entity-instance (explosion) and one-topic-for-everything (no isolation).

  • Unit: mock KafkaTemplate/listener logic; use MockProducer/MockConsumer from the Kafka client.
  • Integration: EmbeddedKafka (spring-kafka-test) or Testcontainers Kafka for a real broker — verify end-to-end produce/consume, serialization, and error handling.
  • Streams: TopologyTestDriver tests topologies without a broker (fast, deterministic).

Testcontainers gives the highest fidelity (real broker behavior, rebalancing); embedded/test-driver are faster for logic tests. Use Awaitility for async assertions.

max.poll.records caps how many records one poll() returns; max.poll.interval.ms is the max time allowed between polls before the consumer is deemed dead (triggering a rebalance). The trap: if processing a large batch takes longer than the interval, the consumer is evicted mid-processing → rebalance loop and potential reprocessing. Fix by lowering max.poll.records (smaller batches per cycle) or raising max.poll.interval.ms for legitimately slow processing — balance batch efficiency against liveness detection.

Kafka records can carry headers — key/value metadata separate from key and value. Uses: tracing/correlation IDs for distributed tracing, schema/version info, message type for routing, retry count and original-topic info for DLT handling, tenant/source identifiers, and content-type for polyglot consumers. They let consumers make decisions without deserializing the payload and enable cross-cutting concerns (observability, security context propagation) — Spring Kafka maps them to/from message headers automatically.

Kafka is an append-only event log optimized for sequential streaming, replay, and decoupling — not for arbitrary queries, updates, or point lookups (it has no indexes/joins/ad-hoc queries). A database excels at random access, updates, transactions, and querying current state. Pattern: use Kafka as the source of truth/event backbone and project events into databases/caches (materialized views) for querying (CQRS). Don't try to "query Kafka" like a DB — derive read stores from it. Compacted topics give "latest per key" but not rich queries.

Kafka is tuned for many small messages; large payloads strain memory, replication, and the default message.max.bytes (~1MB). Options: claim-check pattern (store the blob in object storage/S3 and put only a reference/URL in the Kafka message — recommended); chunking (split + reassemble); or raising the size limits (message.max.bytes, max.request.size, fetch.max.bytes) for moderately larger messages, accepting throughput/latency costs. Compression also helps. Prefer the claim-check approach for genuinely large data.

  • Ordering: key events by orderId so all events for one order land in one partition (per-key order guaranteed).
  • Producer: acks=all, enable.idempotence=true; publish via the Outbox + Debezium to avoid dual-write.
  • Consumer: at-least-once (process then commit) + idempotent processing (dedup by event id / upsert), or Kafka transactions for Kafka-to-Kafka stages.
  • External writes: store offset in the same DB transaction as the state change (effectively-once).
  • Resilience: retries with backoff → DLT for poison pills; monitor lag.

Usual cause: processing a poll batch takes longer than max.poll.interval.ms, so the consumer is evicted → rebalance → reprocessing → more slowness (a loop). Also check long GC pauses, network blips, or session.timeout.ms too low. Fixes: reduce max.poll.records (smaller batches), increase max.poll.interval.ms for genuinely slow work, move slow processing off the poll thread (worker pool with careful offset management), enable cooperative rebalancing (CooperativeStickyAssignor) to shrink pause impact, and fix GC/blocking calls. Verify with consumer/group metrics.

The offset never advances because the bad record always fails. Robust design: wrap deserialization with ErrorHandlingDeserializer (so malformed bytes don't crash the loop); use a DefaultErrorHandler with bounded retries + exponential backoff; classify exceptions — non-retryable (deserialization, validation) go straight to a DLT, retryable (transient downstream) get retried; on exhausting retries, publish to the DLT with error headers (original topic/offset/exception) and commit past the bad record so the partition proceeds. Monitor the DLT and reprocess fixed messages. Never infinite-retry in place.

This is the dual-write problem (DB + Kafka can't be atomic). Solution: Transactional Outbox + CDC. In the same DB transaction as the row change, insert an outbox record (event payload + unique id). Debezium tails the DB transaction log and streams committed outbox rows to Kafka — guaranteeing the event is published iff the transaction committed (no loss), with at-least-once delivery. Make consumers idempotent (dedup by the event's unique id) to neutralize the "at-least-once" duplicates. Result: reliable, effectively-once event publishing without distributed transactions.

A hot key concentrates load on one partition/consumer (skew), and you can't parallelize it without breaking its ordering. Options: (1) if strict per-key order isn't required, use a composite/sub-key (e.g. userId + bucket) to spread the hot key across several partitions, processing with relaxed ordering; (2) two-tier processing — fan the hot key's events to a dedicated topic/partition set with more parallelism; (3) batch/aggregate the hot key's events before processing; (4) accept the bound and scale that single partition's consumer vertically. The core trade-off: ordering vs parallelism for a single key.

The Kafka-to-Kafka part is solved by transactions (atomic produce + offset commit, read_committed downstream). The hard part is the database side — Kafka transactions can't span an external DB. Approaches: (a) make the DB write idempotent (upsert by key) so at-least-once is effectively-once; (b) store the consumed offset in the DB within the same transaction as the data write, and on restart seek to that offset (DB is the source of truth for progress); (c) outbox the produce side from the DB. You generally cannot get true atomic exactly-once across Kafka + DB without idempotency or 2PC (which is avoided in practice).

A rename is a breaking change, so do it in compatible steps with the Schema Registry (backward/forward compatibility): (1) add the new field alongside the old (both populated by producers) — backward compatible. (2) Migrate consumers to read the new field. (3) Once all consumers are updated, stop populating the old field, then remove it in a later version. Never rename in place. For larger breaks, publish a new event version/topic and run both during a deprecation window. Compatibility mode + staged rollout (producer-first vs consumer-first depends on backward vs forward compat) prevents breakage.

Because Kafka retains messages and consumers pull, nothing is lost — the consumer simply lags; when the service recovers it resumes from its committed offset and catches up (back-pressure is natural). Design for it: ensure retention > max expected outage (so messages don't expire before processing), size partitions/consumers to catch up faster than real-time after recovery, make processing idempotent (in case of retries), and add circuit breakers/timeouts so the consumer fails fast rather than hanging. Monitor lag and alert. This buffering is a key advantage over synchronous calls.

  • Ingest transactions into a Kafka topic, keyed by accountId (per-account ordering + co-location of an account's history).
  • Kafka Streams for stateful, windowed analysis: maintain per-account state (recent spend, locations) in state stores; compute features over session/sliding windows using event time + grace for late events.
  • Enrich via stream-table joins (account profile KTable, blocklists).
  • Score (rules or an ML model) → emit alerts to a fraud-alerts topic for downstream action.
  • EOS for accurate counts; DLT for bad records; monitor lag (fraud detection is latency-sensitive). Replay capability lets you re-run with improved models.

Under-replicated partitions mean followers can't keep up with the leader (ISR shrinking) — durability and latency risk. Likely causes: a slow/overloaded broker (disk I/O saturation, CPU), network bottleneck between brokers, a broker GC pause, uneven partition/leader distribution (one broker hot), or a disk filling up. Investigate: per-broker disk/network/CPU metrics, ISR shrink/expand rate, leader skew (rebalance leadership), and GC logs. Fixes: rebalance partitions/leaders, add brokers/capacity, faster disks, tune replication threads (num.replica.fetchers), and ensure min.insync.replicas protects writes meanwhile.

Increments aren't naturally idempotent, so re-delivery double-counts. Solutions: (1) dedup by message id — store processed event ids (unique constraint); skip duplicates, then increment — making the net effect once. (2) Store the increment as an event and compute the counter from the deduplicated event log (event sourcing — replays are safe). (3) Atomic upsert that records which offsets/ids contributed (idempotency key per increment). (4) Use Kafka transactions if the counter lives in Kafka (compacted topic + Streams with EOS). The general technique: turn a non-idempotent operation into an idempotent one via a dedup key or by storing facts instead of mutations.

  • REST (sync): immediate request/response needed, simple point-to-point, caller needs the result now, low fan-out. Tight coupling; downstream outage fails the caller.
  • RabbitMQ: task/work queues, complex routing, per-message workflows, lower volume, need consume-and-delete + priorities/TTL.
  • Kafka: high-throughput event streaming, multiple independent consumers, replay/audit, event sourcing, decoupling many services, buffering spikes.

Decide by: coupling tolerance (sync vs async), throughput, fan-out, replay needs, ordering, and operational maturity. Often a mix — REST for queries, Kafka for events.

Strict global order requires a single partition (one consumer) — which caps throughput. So challenge the requirement: usually you need per-entity order, not global. Reframe: key by entity → many partitions → ordered per key + parallel across keys (high throughput). If global order is truly mandatory (rare), accept the single-partition bottleneck, make processing as fast as possible, and batch. Alternatively, process in parallel but reorder downstream using sequence numbers/timestamps (buffer and emit in order). The senior move is questioning whether global order is genuinely required.

Layered retry: (1) in-memory retries with exponential backoff for transient errors (bounded, e.g. 3 attempts) — don't block the partition too long. (2) For longer/delayed retries, use retry topics (a tiered set: retry-5s, retry-1m, retry-10m) so failed messages are re-attempted later without blocking the main flow (Spring Kafka's @RetryableTopic automates this). (3) After exhausting retries, send to a DLT with error metadata (exception, stack, original topic/offset, retry count) in headers. Observability: metrics on retry/DLT rates, alerting on DLT growth, and tooling to inspect/replay DLT messages after fixing the root cause. Classify retryable vs non-retryable to avoid wasting retries.

Synchronous slow calls make per-message processing exceed max.poll.interval.ms → rebalances and lag. Options: (1) increase parallelism — more partitions/consumers, and within a consumer use a worker pool to call the API concurrently (carefully managing offset commits so you only commit contiguously-processed offsets, and preserve per-key order if needed). (2) pause/resume the consumer to control in-flight work. (3) batch API calls if the API supports it. (4) bulkhead + circuit breaker + timeouts on the API so a slow dependency fails fast. (5) tune max.poll.records down. The key is decoupling Kafka's liveness (poll cadence) from the slow I/O.

The write side emits domain events to Kafka (via Outbox). A projector consumer materializes a denormalized read model (DB/Elasticsearch/cache) by applying events idempotently (upsert by key, track last applied offset/version). For rebuilds (schema change, new view, bug fix): reset the projector's offsets to the beginning (or read a compacted state topic) and replay all events into a fresh read store, then atomically switch over. Consistency: it's eventually consistent — surface a version/timestamp, and for read-after-write UX, read from the write side or wait for the projection. Idempotency + replay are what make this safe.

Use separate consumer groups — each group gets all messages independently and tracks its own offsets, so one can be at-least-once with aggressive retries while another is at-most-once (commit-before-process) or uses different batch sizes, error handling, and DLTs. Kafka's pub/sub-per-group model means consumers don't interfere. Each service tunes its own consumer config (acks awareness, commit strategy, idempotency) for its guarantee. This is a core benefit of Kafka's retained log + multiple independent groups.

Increasing partitions on a keyed topic changes hash(key) % N, scrambling future key→partition mapping and breaking per-key ordering across the boundary. Safer approach: create a new topic with the desired partition count (and a partitioning scheme designed for future growth), then dual-write or replay from the old topic to the new one (preserving keys), migrate consumers to the new topic, and retire the old. For state/compacted topics, rebuild carefully. Avoid in-place partition increases on ordered topics in production — plan partition count up front.

Use MirrorMaker 2 (or Confluent Cluster Linking) to replicate topics between regional clusters. Active-active: producers write locally (low latency), and topics are mirrored both ways with prefixing/renaming to prevent replication loops; consumers may read local + remote. Trade-offs: replication lag means non-zero RPO (some in-flight data can be lost on a regional failure), no global ordering across regions, potential duplicate/conflicting writes (need conflict resolution or region-partitioned keys), and offset translation complexity on failover. For strict consistency, prefer active-passive (one writer) accepting failover RTO. Decide based on the consistency-vs-availability-vs-latency trade-off.

Producer idempotence dedups retries within a producer session per partition — it does NOT prevent duplicates from: producer restarts (new producer id, sequence resets) unless using transactions with a stable transactional.id; application-level re-sends (retrying a failed send() at the app layer); or consumer-side reprocessing after a crash before offset commit (at-least-once). So "duplicates downstream" usually means the consumer reprocessed, or events were produced twice by app logic. Fix at the consumer with idempotent processing (dedup by business key), and use transactions for a stable producer identity across restarts. Idempotent producer ≠ end-to-end exactly-once.

This is causal ordering. Simplest: put both events on the same topic keyed by accountId, so they're in the same partition and delivered in production order (created before closed) to a single consumer — ordering preserved. If they originate from different sources/topics, options: (1) merge into one keyed topic upstream; (2) carry a sequence/version number and have the consumer buffer out-of-order events (hold "closed" until "created" with the lower sequence is seen); (3) make "closed" handling tolerant (if the account doesn't exist yet, retry/park the event). Per-key single-partition ordering is the clean solution; sequence-aware buffering handles cross-topic cases.

Kafka's pull model gives natural backpressure — slow consumers just lag; messages buffer durably in the topic (no consumer is overwhelmed). Manage it by: ensuring retention covers worst-case lag, scaling consumers/partitions to drain faster, and on the producer side limiting rate if business requires near-real-time (rate limiting, or buffer.memory/max.block.ms to throttle a runaway producer). For end-to-end flow control across stages, size each stage's consumer capacity and monitor lag per stage as the signal. The architectural win: decoupling means the producer needn't slow down for the consumer — the log absorbs the burst.

Kafka's immutable, append-only log with retention (or infinite retention/tiered storage) is a natural audit/event store: every change is an event, ordered per key, never modified in place, and replayable to rebuild state or feed new consumers. For tamper-evidence, add integrity measures Kafka doesn't provide natively: include a hash chain in events (each event hashes the previous), sign events cryptographically, and restrict ACLs (no delete/produce by unauthorized parties). Use compaction off (keep full history) and tiered storage for long-term retention. Consumers project the audit log into queryable stores. Replay + immutability are exactly what audit requires.

Kafka Streams keeps local state stores (RocksDB) backed by compacted changelog topics. On restart/reassignment, it restores state by replaying the changelog — correct but potentially slow for large state. Speed-ups: (1) standby replicas (num.standby.replicas) keep warm copies on other instances for fast failover; (2) persistent volumes so RocksDB state survives restarts (no full replay); (3) static group membership + sticky assignment to avoid reassigning partitions on transient restarts; (4) tune changelog compaction. The trade-off: standbys/persistent disks cost resources but cut recovery time dramatically.

  • Topic design: one topic per event type/bounded context; partition by entity key for ordering + parallelism; sized for peak throughput; retention/compaction chosen per topic (event log vs state).
  • Delivery: idempotent producer + acks=all + min.insync.replicas=2; Outbox + CDC for reliable publishing; at-least-once consumers with idempotent processing (or EOS for Kafka-internal flows).
  • Ordering: per-key partitioning; sequence numbers for cross-topic causal ordering.
  • Schema: Schema Registry with backward compatibility; evolve additively; Avro/Protobuf.
  • Resilience: retries + retry topics + DLT, circuit breakers on external calls, replay capability, Saga for workflows.
  • Ops & security: monitor consumer lag & under-replicated partitions, TLS + SASL + ACLs, multi-region DR (MirrorMaker), Testcontainers in CI, and CQRS read models projected from events.

🌐 Microservices & REST API — Top 100

ℹ️ REST design, microservice patterns, resilience, data consistency, communication, and heavily scenario-based questions.

REST (Representational State Transfer) is an architectural style for APIs over HTTP. Core principles: client-server separation, statelessness (each request self-contained), cacheability, uniform interface (resources identified by URIs, manipulated via standard HTTP methods), layered system, and resources represented in formats like JSON. It leverages HTTP semantics rather than inventing its own protocol.

MethodPurposeSafeIdempotent
GETReadYesYes
POSTCreateNoNo
PUTReplace/upsertNoYes
PATCHPartial updateNoNo*
DELETERemoveNoYes

Safe = no state change; idempotent = same result if repeated. PATCH can be idempotent if designed so.

  • Use nouns, not verbs (/orders, not /getOrders) — the method conveys the action.
  • Plural collection names; hierarchy for relationships: /users/42/orders.
  • Lowercase, hyphenated, no trailing slash.
  • Query params for filtering/sorting/pagination: /orders?status=PAID&page=2.
  • Avoid deep nesting beyond 2 levels; use IDs.

Each request carries all the information needed to process it; the server stores no client session state between requests. This matters because any server instance can handle any request — enabling horizontal scaling, load balancing without sticky sessions, easy failover, and simpler caching. State (if needed) lives in the request (token), the database, or a shared store (Redis), not in server memory.

  • 2xx: 200 OK, 201 Created, 202 Accepted (async), 204 No Content.
  • 3xx: 301/304 (redirect/not-modified, caching).
  • 4xx: 400 Bad Request, 401 Unauthorized, 403 Forbidden, 404 Not Found, 405 Method Not Allowed, 409 Conflict, 422 Unprocessable, 429 Too Many Requests.
  • 5xx: 500 Internal, 502 Bad Gateway, 503 Service Unavailable, 504 Gateway Timeout.

A microservice is a small, independently deployable service owning a single business capability and its data, communicating over the network. A monolith is one deployable unit containing all functionality. Microservices offer independent scaling/deployment, technology diversity, and team autonomy — at the cost of distributed-system complexity (network failures, data consistency, observability, ops overhead). Monoliths are simpler to build/debug but harder to scale and evolve at large team/codebase size.

Benefits: independent deployment & scaling, fault isolation, team autonomy, technology flexibility, easier to understand each small service. Drawbacks: distributed complexity (network latency/failures), data consistency without distributed transactions, harder debugging/tracing, operational overhead (many services, CI/CD, monitoring), and the risk of a "distributed monolith" if boundaries are wrong. Rule: don't start with microservices unless the scale/team justifies it — a well-structured monolith is often the right first step.

The mechanism for services to find each other's network locations dynamically (since instances scale up/down and change IPs). Approaches: client-side discovery (client queries a registry like Eureka/Consul and load-balances) or server-side discovery (a load balancer/proxy routes — e.g. Kubernetes Services + DNS). It removes hardcoded hostnames and supports elastic scaling and failover. In Kubernetes, service discovery is built in via DNS.

A single entry point in front of microservices handling cross-cutting concerns: routing, authentication, rate limiting, request aggregation, protocol translation, and caching. Clients call one endpoint instead of many services, and the gateway shields them from the internal topology. Tools: Spring Cloud Gateway, Kong, AWS API Gateway. Risk: it can become a bottleneck or accumulate business logic — keep it focused on edge concerns.

Synchronous (REST/gRPC) — request/response; simple, immediate result, but creates temporal coupling (caller waits, downstream outage propagates). Asynchronous (messaging/events via Kafka/RabbitMQ) — fire-and-react; decoupled, resilient to outages, scalable, but eventually consistent and harder to trace. Rule of thumb: use sync for queries needing an immediate answer, async events for state-change notifications and workflows. Most systems use both.

Each microservice owns its private database — no other service accesses it directly (only via the owning service's API/events). This enforces loose coupling and lets each service choose the right datastore and evolve its schema independently. Trade-off: you lose cross-service joins and ACID transactions, so consistency must be handled with Sagas/events (eventual consistency). The anti-pattern is a shared database, which couples services and defeats independent deployment.

Prevents cascading failures by stopping calls to a failing dependency. States: Closed (calls pass, failures counted) → Open (threshold exceeded → fail fast with a fallback, don't call) → Half-Open (after a cooldown, allow trial calls; success closes it, failure reopens). Implemented by Resilience4j. It protects the caller from wasting threads on timeouts and gives the failing service time to recover.

An operation is idempotent if performing it multiple times has the same effect as once. It's critical because networks are unreliable — clients retry, messages get redelivered (at-least-once), and load balancers re-route. Without idempotency, retries cause duplicates (double charges, duplicate orders). Achieve it via idempotency keys, upserts, or dedup tables. GET/PUT/DELETE are idempotent by HTTP semantics; make POST safe to retry with an Idempotency-Key header.

A consistency model where, after an update, replicas/services converge to the same state over time rather than instantly. In microservices, since each service owns its data and they sync via events, there's a window where different services have different views (e.g. order placed but inventory not yet decremented). The system is correct eventually. You trade immediate (strong) consistency for availability and scalability — and must design UX/logic to tolerate the lag (status fields, retries, compensations).

A way to manage data consistency across services without distributed transactions: a business transaction becomes a sequence of local transactions, each publishing an event that triggers the next; if a step fails, compensating transactions undo the prior steps. Two styles: choreography (services react to each other's events) and orchestration (a coordinator directs the steps). Trades ACID for eventual consistency with explicit rollback logic.

Providing multiple versions of an API so you can evolve it without breaking existing clients. Strategies: URI (/v1/orders), header (Accept: application/vnd.app.v2+json), or query param. You version because clients integrate at a point in time and can't all upgrade simultaneously; breaking changes (removing/renaming fields, changing types) require a new version, with old versions maintained during a deprecation window. Prefer additive, backward-compatible changes to minimize version bumps.

Orchestration — a central coordinator explicitly commands each service in sequence (like a conductor). Clear flow and monitoring, but the orchestrator is a coupling/complexity point. Choreography — services react to events independently with no central brain (like dancers responding to cues). Loosely coupled and scalable, but the overall flow is implicit and harder to trace/debug. Use orchestration for complex, branching workflows needing visibility; choreography for simple, loosely-coupled event flows.

REST — HTTP/JSON, human-readable, universally supported, browser-friendly, flexible. gRPC — HTTP/2 + Protobuf (binary), strongly-typed contracts, code generation, streaming, much faster/compact. Use REST for public APIs and browser clients; use gRPC for high-performance internal service-to-service communication where efficiency and typed contracts matter. gRPC isn't natively browser-callable (needs gRPC-Web). Many systems use REST at the edge, gRPC internally.

Isolate resources so a failure in one part doesn't sink the whole system — like watertight compartments in a ship. In microservices: dedicate separate thread pools/connection pools per downstream dependency, so if service A is slow and exhausts its pool, service B's calls still have their own pool and keep working. Implemented via Resilience4j Bulkhead (thread-pool or semaphore-based). Pairs with circuit breakers and timeouts for resilient inter-service calls.

Endpoints reporting whether a service is functioning, used by orchestrators/load balancers to route traffic and restart unhealthy instances. Liveness — is the app alive? (failing → restart the pod). Readiness — is it ready to serve traffic? (failing → remove from load balancer, e.g. while warming up or a dependency is down). Spring Boot Actuator provides /actuator/health with liveness/readiness groups for Kubernetes probes.

Tracking a single request as it flows through multiple services, correlating all the work via a shared trace ID (and per-hop span IDs). Needed because in microservices one user action touches many services — without tracing, debugging latency or errors is guesswork. Tools: Micrometer Tracing + OpenTelemetry exporting to Jaeger/Zipkin/Tempo. It produces an end-to-end timeline showing where time is spent and where failures originate.

Vertical scaling (scale up) — add more resources (CPU/RAM) to a single instance; simple but has a ceiling and a single point of failure. Horizontal scaling (scale out) — add more instances and distribute load; near-unlimited, fault-tolerant, the cloud-native approach — but requires statelessness, load balancing, and handling distributed concerns. Microservices favor horizontal scaling; that's why services must be stateless.

Restricting how many requests a client can make in a time window to protect against abuse, ensure fair usage, and prevent overload/DoS. Apply at the API gateway (global edge protection) and/or per-service. Algorithms: token bucket, sliding window. Use a distributed store (Redis) for correctness across instances. Return 429 Too Many Requests with a Retry-After header. It's both a reliability and security control.

The gateway can authenticate (validate the token) once at the edge and route, offloading work. But relying on it alone is unsafe — internal/compromised callers could bypass it. Following zero trust, each service should also validate the token and enforce its own authorization (defense in depth). Best practice: gateway does authentication + coarse checks; each service independently validates the token and enforces fine-grained, resource-level authorization. Never trust the network.

A unique identifier attached to a request at the entry point and propagated through every service and log it touches (via a header like X-Correlation-ID, stored in SLF4J MDC). It lets you trace and correlate all logs for one user request across services — essential for debugging distributed systems. Generate it at the gateway (or accept a client-provided one), forward it on every outbound call, and include it in every log line. Distributed tracing automates this with trace IDs.

Decompose by business capability / bounded context (DDD), not by technical layer. Each service should own a cohesive domain and its data, with high internal cohesion and loose coupling to others. Heuristics: align with team ownership (Conway's law), separate things that change for different reasons and scale differently, and minimize cross-service transactions/chatter. Identify seams in the monolith, extract the least-coupled context first (Strangler Fig), and avoid splitting so fine that a single user action requires many synchronous hops (a "distributed monolith").

The worst of both worlds: services that are physically separate but tightly coupled — they must be deployed together, share a database, or chain many synchronous calls for one operation. You get microservice overhead (network, ops) without the benefits (independent deployment/scaling). Avoid by: proper bounded contexts, database-per-service, async events over chatty sync calls, versioned contracts, and ensuring each service can be deployed independently. If you can't deploy one service without the others, it's a distributed monolith.

Avoid 2PC (XA) — it's slow, locks resources, and doesn't scale. Instead use the Saga pattern: a sequence of local transactions with compensating actions for rollback, coordinated by choreography (events) or orchestration (a coordinator). Pair with the Transactional Outbox for reliable event publishing and idempotent handlers. Accept eventual consistency and design the domain to tolerate intermediate states (e.g. order status PENDING → CONFIRMED/CANCELLED). For true atomic needs, reconsider whether those operations should be in the same service.

They're layered resilience controls. Timeouts bound how long you wait (never block indefinitely). Retries handle transient failures (with exponential backoff + jitter, bounded attempts, only for idempotent ops). Circuit breakers stop retrying once a dependency is clearly down (fail fast, give it time to recover). Order matters: a circuit breaker prevents retry storms from hammering a dead service; retries handle blips while it's healthy. Without the breaker, retries amplify an outage. Add bulkheads to isolate the blast radius.

In a distributed system you can guarantee at most two of Consistency, Availability, Partition tolerance — and since network partitions are unavoidable, you really choose between CP (consistent but may reject requests during a partition) and AP (available but may return stale data). Microservices typically lean AP with eventual consistency (stay available, reconcile via events) for most flows, choosing CP only where correctness is non-negotiable (payments, inventory). The decision is per-operation, not system-wide.

Require the client to send an Idempotency-Key header (a unique token per logical operation). On the server: check a store (Redis/DB with unique constraint) for the key — if seen, return the stored response; otherwise process, persist (result + key) atomically, and return. Concurrent duplicates are handled by the unique constraint (one wins, others read the result). This makes client retries, double-clicks, and network re-sends safe — essential for create/payment endpoints. Set a reasonable TTL on stored keys.

For small/early pages, offset pagination (page/size) is fine. For large datasets/deep pages, use keyset (cursor) pagination: order by an indexed key and filter WHERE (sort, id) > (lastSort, lastId) LIMIT n — O(limit), index-friendly, stable under inserts. Return a cursor token, not a page number. Avoid expensive total-count queries when unnecessary (use "has next" / cursors). Document limits, cap page size, and keep responses consistent. Cursor pagination is the standard for high-volume, frequently-changing collections.

  • Encrypt in transit — TLS everywhere (mTLS for mutual identity, automated by a service mesh).
  • Authenticate services — OAuth2 Client Credentials tokens or mTLS certs; validate aud/scopes.
  • Authorize per call — each service checks the caller's permissions (zero trust, never trust the network).
  • Propagate user context securely (token relay) when acting on a user's behalf.
  • Network policies, secrets management, and audit logging.

Client-side — the calling service gets the list of instances from a registry (Eureka/Consul) and picks one itself (e.g. Spring Cloud LoadBalancer). No extra hop, but logic lives in each client. Server-side — a dedicated load balancer/proxy (NGINX, Kubernetes Service, AWS ELB) sits in front and routes; clients just call one address. Simpler clients, centralized control, but an extra network hop. Kubernetes uses server-side (Services + kube-proxy); Spring Cloud historically used client-side.

A service mesh offloads networking concerns to sidecar proxies (Envoy) deployed alongside each service, handling mTLS, retries, timeouts, circuit breaking, load balancing, traffic shifting (canary), and observability (metrics/tracing) — without changing application code. The control plane (Istio) configures all sidecars centrally. Benefits: language-agnostic resilience/security, consistent policy, rich telemetry. Cost: operational complexity and latency/resource overhead from the proxies. Useful at scale; often overkill for a handful of services.

A dedicated backend tailored to a specific frontend (web, mobile, IoT), aggregating and shaping data from multiple microservices for that client's exact needs. It avoids a one-size-fits-all gateway and reduces client-side orchestration and over-fetching (e.g. a mobile BFF returns slim payloads; a web BFF returns richer ones). Each BFF is owned by the frontend team. Trade-off: more services to maintain. GraphQL can serve a similar role (client-specified queries) as an alternative to multiple BFFs.

Externalize config (12-factor) — never bake it into images. Options: Spring Cloud Config Server (Git-backed, centralized, versioned, with encryption and dynamic refresh via @RefreshScope/Bus), Kubernetes ConfigMaps/Secrets (mounted as env/files), or a config service like Consul. Secrets go in a vault (HashiCorp Vault, cloud secret managers). Use profiles per environment. Goal: single source of truth, environment-specific overrides, auditable, and changeable without rebuilding.

The three pillars: Logs (structured, centralized — ELK/Loki — with correlation IDs), Metrics (Micrometer → Prometheus/Grafana: latency, error rate, throughput, saturation — the RED/USE methods), and Traces (distributed tracing across services — Jaeger/Tempo via OpenTelemetry). Plus health checks and alerting (on SLOs). Together they answer "is it working, how well, and where's the problem" — without observability, distributed systems are nearly impossible to operate.

Blue-green — run two identical environments; deploy to the idle one (green), test it, then switch all traffic from blue to green (instant rollback by switching back). Canary — release the new version to a small percentage of traffic, monitor metrics/errors, and gradually ramp up (or roll back if bad). Canary limits blast radius and validates in production with real traffic; blue-green gives fast, clean cutover. Both require backward-compatible changes (esp. DB) and good monitoring. Service meshes/gateways enable traffic splitting.

Treat events as long-lived contracts. Use a Schema Registry with compatibility rules (backward-compatible: add optional fields with defaults, never remove/rename/retype required ones). Consumers should be tolerant readers (ignore unknown fields). For breaking changes, publish a new event version (or new topic) and run both during a deprecation window. Avoid coupling consumers to fields they don't use. Contract testing catches breaks in CI. The principle: producers can evolve without forcing all consumers to upgrade simultaneously.

Contract testing verifies that a provider and consumer agree on the API/event contract — the consumer defines expectations, and the provider's build validates it still meets them (consumer-driven contracts). Tools: Pact, Spring Cloud Contract. Importance: in microservices, you can't run full end-to-end tests for every change cheaply; contract tests catch breaking changes in CI before deployment, without spinning up the whole system. They give confidence that independently-deployed services remain compatible.

When a dependency fails, return a reduced but useful response instead of failing entirely — via circuit-breaker fallbacks. Examples: serve cached/stale data, return default/empty values, hide a non-critical widget, or queue the work for later. E.g. if the recommendations service is down, show popular items instead of erroring the whole product page. The goal: core functionality survives partial failures. Design each call site to answer "what's the acceptable degraded behavior?" rather than propagating the failure to the user.

An aggregator (gateway or BFF) calls multiple services and combines their responses into one for the client — solving the "data spread across services" problem for queries. Pitfalls: latency (sum or max of all calls — parallelize them), partial failures (one service down — use fallbacks/timeouts per call), chattiness and tight coupling to many services, and the aggregator becoming a bottleneck. For complex queries spanning services, consider CQRS (a pre-built read model) instead of composing at request time.

Call services in parallel (CompletableFuture) with per-call timeouts and circuit breakers. On a sub-call failure, decide per field whether it's critical (fail the whole request) or optional (return a default/omit it and continue — graceful degradation). Return a partial response with indicators of what's missing rather than a 500. Never let one slow/failed dependency block or fail the entire aggregation. Log/trace the failure for observability. The key is classifying each dependency's criticality.

PUT replaces the entire resource with the provided representation (idempotent — sending it twice yields the same state); omitted fields are typically cleared. PATCH applies a partial update (only the provided fields change). Use PUT when the client has and sends the full resource; use PATCH for partial edits (e.g. update just the status). PATCH bodies can be JSON Merge Patch or JSON Patch. Be explicit about semantics — partial-update confusion is a common API bug.

Use ETags + conditional requests. The server returns an ETag (version/hash) with a resource; the client sends it back on update via If-Match. If the resource changed since (ETag mismatch), the server returns 412 Precondition Failed, preventing a lost update — the client must re-fetch and retry. This maps to JPA's @Version optimistic locking underneath. It lets concurrent edits be detected without server-side locks, and gives clients a clear conflict signal.

  • HTTP caching — Cache-Control, ETag/If-None-Match (304 Not Modified), Expires — let clients/CDNs cache responses.
  • Server-side cache — Redis/Caffeine for expensive computations/queries (cache-aside, read-through).
  • Gateway/CDN caching for public, cacheable GETs.

Cache only safe/idempotent reads; invalidate on writes (or short TTLs); never cache user-specific data in shared caches without keying by user. Caching is the highest-leverage performance lever — but stale data and invalidation are the hard parts.

Use a standard structure across all endpoints, ideally RFC 7807 Problem Details (Spring's ProblemDetail): type, title, status, detail, instance, plus a stable machine-readable error code and field-level validation errors. Handle it centrally (@RestControllerAdvice). Rules: use correct HTTP status codes, don't leak stack traces/internal details, include a correlation ID, and keep the format consistent so clients can parse errors uniformly. Good error contracts are part of the API design, not an afterthought.

Sync request-reply — the caller invokes each step and waits; simple and immediate, but temporally coupled (all services must be up), latency adds up, and failures cascade. Event-driven — each step emits an event the next reacts to; decoupled, resilient (services can be down and catch up), scalable, but eventually consistent, harder to trace, and the flow is implicit. Use sync for short, query-like interactions needing an immediate answer; events for multi-step, long-running, or cross-service workflows (Sagas).

  • Unit tests (most) — business logic, fast, mocked dependencies.
  • Component/slice tests — a service in isolation with its DB (Testcontainers) and mocked collaborators.
  • Contract tests — verify inter-service contracts (Pact) without full integration.
  • Integration tests — real dependencies (DB, broker) via Testcontainers.
  • End-to-end (fewest) — full system, slow/brittle, smoke-test critical paths only.

Favor lower levels; E2E is expensive and flaky in distributed systems — contract tests replace much of it.

No distributed transaction — use a Saga. Orchestration is cleaner here (clear flow, compensation): an Order Orchestrator drives: (1) reserve inventory → (2) charge payment → (3) confirm order. On failure at any step, fire compensating actions (release inventory, refund payment). Make each step idempotent (idempotency keys) and use the Outbox for reliable events. Order starts as PENDING and moves to CONFIRMED/CANCELLED (eventual consistency). Add timeouts and a Saga timeout to cancel stuck flows. Surface status to the user rather than blocking.

Root cause: the charge operation isn't idempotent, and the client (or gateway) retries on timeout/network error — so the same payment executes twice. Fix: require an idempotency key per payment attempt; the payment service records it (unique constraint) with the result, and a retry with the same key returns the original result instead of charging again. Also: distinguish "timed out but succeeded" from "failed" (query the provider before retrying), use the payment provider's own idempotency keys, and make the charge + record atomic. This is the canonical idempotency interview scenario.

A cascading failure: C's slowness makes B's threads block waiting, which makes A's threads block waiting on B — thread pools exhaust up the chain and everything stalls. Fix with layered resilience: timeouts at every hop (fail fast, don't wait on C forever), circuit breakers (once C is failing, B fails fast with a fallback), bulkheads (isolate C-calling threads so they can't starve B's other work), and graceful degradation (return partial/cached data). This stops one slow service from taking down the system — the core resilience scenario.

  • API composition (BFF/gateway calls all 4 in parallel) — simple, real-time, but latency = slowest call, partial-failure handling needed, chatty.
  • CQRS read model — services publish events; a projector pre-builds a denormalized view the screen queries in one hit — fast reads, but eventual consistency and extra infrastructure.
  • GraphQL — let the client request exactly the fields, federated across services.

Choose composition for occasional/simple needs; a materialized read model for hot, high-traffic screens where latency matters.

At-least-once delivery guarantees duplicates can happen, so the consumer must be idempotent. Options: dedup by a unique event/message ID stored with a unique constraint (skip if already processed); use upsert keyed by a business identifier instead of insert; or store the processed offset/ID in the same transaction as the write. Don't try to achieve "exactly-once delivery" — instead make processing idempotent so duplicate delivery is harmless ("effectively-once"). This is the standard answer for duplicate-event scenarios.

Use the Backend for Frontend (BFF) pattern: a dedicated backend per client type. The mobile BFF aggregates and trims data to minimal fields (bandwidth/battery-friendly); the web BFF returns richer composed responses. Each BFF owns its client's contract and evolves independently, avoiding a bloated one-size-fits-all API. Alternative: GraphQL — clients request exactly the fields they need from a single federated endpoint, avoiding multiple BFFs. Choose BFF for clear per-client divergence, GraphQL for flexible field selection.

Use the Strangler Fig pattern behind a facade/gateway: route specific features to new services while the monolith serves the rest, migrating context by context. Steps: identify bounded contexts (DDD), extract the least-coupled one first, add an anti-corruption layer to translate models, start with a shared DB then split data gradually (database-per-service goal), introduce async events to decouple, and add observability early. Deliver continuously, validate each slice in production, and retire monolith pieces as replaced. Avoid the big-bang rewrite.

A shared database couples the services: schema changes by one break the other, they can't be deployed/scaled independently, ownership blurs, and you've built a distributed monolith. Push back and instead: each service owns its data; share via APIs (synchronous query) or events (the owning service publishes changes, others maintain their own copy/read model). If they truly need the same data atomically, maybe they should be one service. The database-per-service principle is foundational to microservice independence.

  • Contract testing (Pact) — your expectations run in the provider's CI, catching breaks before deploy.
  • API versioning + backward-compatible evolution (additive changes; deprecation windows).
  • Tolerant reader — ignore unknown fields, don't fail on extras.
  • Defensive coding: validate responses, timeouts, fallbacks for missing data.
  • Clear ownership/communication and a schema registry for events.

The systemic fix is contract testing + versioning discipline, not just patching your client.

The dual-write problem (save order + send email can't be atomic) plus email being non-idempotent. Solution: Transactional Outbox — in the same transaction as the order, write an "OrderPlaced" outbox row; a relay/CDC publishes it to a queue/topic. The email consumer is idempotent — it records sent emails by a dedup key (orderId) so redelivery doesn't double-send, and uses the email provider's idempotency key. This gives at-least-once delivery made effectively-once via dedup. Sending email inside the DB transaction (and rolling back after sending) is the anti-pattern to avoid.

Apply rate limiting / throttling per client (API key/user/IP) at the gateway — token bucket with a distributed store (Redis), returning 429 with Retry-After. Add quotas (per-day limits) and tiered limits by plan. Use bulkheads so one client's load is isolated, and load shedding (reject low-priority requests under pressure). For abusive patterns, IP blocking/WAF. This protects fairness and availability — a noisy-neighbor problem solved primarily at the edge.

Don't hold an HTTP connection for 10 minutes. Use the async request-reply pattern: POST /reports returns 202 Accepted with a status URL (and a job ID); the work runs asynchronously (queue/worker). The client polls GET /reports/{id} (returns PENDING/DONE + result link) or you push completion via webhook/WebSocket/SSE. Store job state, make submission idempotent, handle retries and timeouts. This decouples request acceptance from processing and survives client disconnects.

You can't force all consumers to change at once. Strategy: introduce a new version (v2) alongside v1, keep both running, and announce a deprecation timeline with Deprecation/Sunset headers and docs. Prefer making the change additive/backward-compatible so a version bump isn't needed at all. Provide migration guides, monitor v1 usage, nudge laggards, and only retire v1 when usage approaches zero. Contract tests and clear communication minimize breakage. Never silently change behavior.

Use a Saga with eventual consistency. Order service creates the order as PENDING and emits "OrderCreated"; inventory service reserves stock and emits "StockReserved" (or "StockInsufficient"); order service then confirms or cancels accordingly. Each step is a local transaction with compensation (release stock if the order later fails). Use the Outbox for reliable events and idempotent handlers. For the critical "don't oversell" guarantee, the inventory reservation uses an atomic conditional update (UPDATE ... WHERE qty >= n). Accept the brief inconsistency window; design status to reflect it.

Likely eventual consistency or caching: the read came from a replica/cache/read model that hadn't yet received the update (replication lag or stale cache). Address per requirement: for read-after-write consistency, route the user's immediate reads to the primary (or the write side), or invalidate/update the cache synchronously on write. For read models, surface a version/timestamp and let the UI reflect "updating". Use cache TTLs/invalidation events. If strong consistency is truly required for that flow, don't cache/replicate it. The key is matching the consistency model to the use case.

REST/JSON at the edge (public API via the gateway — universal, browser-friendly, documented with OpenAPI, versioned, rate-limited, authenticated). gRPC internally between services for performance (binary Protobuf, HTTP/2, streaming, typed contracts, lower latency). The gateway can translate REST→gRPC. This hybrid gives external compatibility and internal efficiency. Keep one source of truth for schemas (Protobuf/OpenAPI) and generate clients. Many large systems adopt exactly this REST-outside/gRPC-inside split.

Partition by the entity that requires ordering (e.g. accountId) so all events for one entity go to one partition/consumer — ordered per key — while different keys are processed in parallel across partitions (scaling). Have enough partitions for the desired parallelism and consumers up to that count. A single hot key is bounded by one partition; if that's a problem and strict order isn't essential, sub-partition it. This per-key ordering + cross-key parallelism is the standard reconciliation of order and scale.

Per-instance counters would allow 10× the intended limit, so use a distributed, shared counter — typically Redis with an atomic token-bucket/sliding-window algorithm (e.g. a Lua script for atomic check-and-decrement, or Redis-backed Bucket4j/Spring Cloud Gateway RequestRateLimiter). All instances consult the same Redis state, so the global limit is enforced regardless of which instance handles the request. Consider key design (per-user/API-key), expiry, and Redis availability (fail-open vs fail-closed). Edge/gateway enforcement centralizes it further.

  • Scale out consumers (up to partition count); add partitions if at the limit.
  • Speed up processing — batch DB writes, async I/O, remove blocking calls.
  • Parallelize within the consumer (worker pool) while preserving per-key order and correct offset commits.
  • Decouple slow work — quickly hand off to another stage/topic.
  • Tune batch size / poll settings.

Monitor lag as the signal; persistent lag means under-provisioning or a too-slow design that needs re-architecture.

Use distributed tracing: a trace ID is generated at the entry point and propagated (W3C traceparent header) through all 6 services, each recording spans with timings. View the trace in Jaeger/Zipkin/Tempo to see the end-to-end timeline and pinpoint which service/operation consumed the time (DB query, downstream call, queue wait). Correlate with logs (same trace ID in MDC) and metrics. Without tracing this is guesswork; Micrometer Tracing + OpenTelemetry instruments it automatically. The flamegraph reveals the bottleneck instantly.

Require an idempotency key (transferId) from the client. Within a transaction (or Saga across account services): check if the transferId was already processed (unique constraint) → if so, return the prior result; else perform the debit and credit atomically (or via a Saga with compensation if accounts are separate services), recording the transferId. Use atomic conditional updates (UPDATE ... SET balance = balance - amt WHERE balance >= amt) to prevent overdraft and lost updates. Retries with the same key are safe; concurrent duplicates are blocked by the unique constraint. This combines idempotency + atomicity + concurrency control.

The decrement must be atomic and conditional. Best: a single SQL statement UPDATE inventory SET qty = qty - 1 WHERE sku = ? AND qty >= 1 — if it affects 0 rows, it's out of stock (the DB serializes concurrent updates; no oversell). Alternatives: pessimistic lock on the row, or an atomic counter in Redis (DECR with a floor check). Optimistic locking (@Version) works but thrashes on a hot item. Avoid read-then-write in app code (race → oversell). For multi-service flows, reserve stock as a Saga step with a reservation TTL.

  • Timeouts on every call (never block indefinitely).
  • Retries with exponential backoff + jitter, bounded, idempotent-only.
  • Circuit breaker to fail fast when it's down, with a fallback (cached/default response or queue for later).
  • Bulkhead so its slowness doesn't starve other work.
  • Async/queue the call if the result isn't needed immediately (retry from the queue).
  • Monitor its health; cache responses where possible; have a degraded mode.

Persist outbound webhook events (Outbox) and deliver via a worker. Key concerns: retries with exponential backoff on failure (customer endpoints are unreliable), a max-attempts + dead-letter for permanent failures, idempotency (include an event ID + delivery ID so customers can dedup, since you deliver at-least-once), signing payloads (HMAC) so customers verify authenticity, timeouts, and ordering caveats (document that order isn't guaranteed, or sequence per resource). Provide a dashboard and replay capability. This mirrors reliable messaging applied to outbound HTTP.

Too coarse → it's a mini-monolith (multiple unrelated concerns, can't scale parts independently). Too fine → a distributed monolith (excessive network hops, chatty calls, hard transactions, ops overhead). Right-size by bounded context / business capability: a service should own a cohesive domain and its data, be independently deployable, and not require synchronous chains with siblings for a single operation. Align with team ownership (Conway's law), data ownership, and differing scaling/change rates. Start coarser and split when real pain (coupling, scaling) appears — premature fine-graining is costly.

  • Services by capability: Rider, Driver, Matching/Dispatch, Trip, Pricing (surge), Payment, Location/Geo, Notification.
  • Data: DB-per-service; Geo service uses a spatial index (geohash/Redis Geo) for nearby-driver queries.
  • Communication: high-frequency driver location updates via streaming (Kafka); matching consumes location + requests; trip lifecycle via events (Saga: request → match → accept → start → end → charge); sync REST/gRPC for queries.
  • Consistency: eventual; idempotent payments; surge computed in a stream-processing layer.
  • Resilience: circuit breakers, regional partitioning, real-time tracing; scale Matching/Geo independently (hottest).

Compensations must be retryable and idempotent (retry with backoff until they succeed — they should be designed to eventually succeed). If a compensation can't succeed automatically (e.g. refund to a closed account), escalate: persist the failed compensation, alert/route to a manual intervention / dead-letter queue, and keep the Saga in a clearly FAILED-NEEDS-ATTENTION state. Design compensations to be "always possible" where feasible (reserve-then-confirm rather than do-then-undo). Use an orchestrator that tracks Saga state durably (Temporal/Camunda) so it can resume and retry compensations after crashes. Never silently swallow a failed compensation.

  • Idempotency: idempotency key per payment at the API and at the gateway; dedup store with unique constraint; provider idempotency keys.
  • Ordering: key events by accountId/paymentId (per-entity order in one partition); carry sequence numbers for cross-stage causal order.
  • Reliable publish: Transactional Outbox + CDC (no dual-write).
  • Effectively-once: at-least-once delivery + idempotent consumers; store consumed offset in the same DB tx as the write.
  • State machine: payment status (INITIATED→AUTHORIZED→CAPTURED/FAILED) with Saga compensations (refund/void).
  • Audit: immutable event log; reconciliation against the provider.

Detect via reconciliation jobs that periodically compare related data across services (e.g. orders vs payments vs inventory) and flag mismatches, plus Saga timeouts that catch stuck workflows. Reconcile by: re-driving the Saga from its last durable state (idempotent steps), firing missed compensations, or surfacing to a manual-resolution queue for irreconcilable cases. Prevent recurrence with the Outbox (no lost events), idempotency, durable Saga state, and audit logs. Eventual consistency means you plan for reconciliation as a first-class operational process, not an afterthought.

  • Ingest notification requests to Kafka (buffer spikes, decouple producers).
  • Outbox from the originating service so requests are never lost.
  • Channel workers (email/SMS/push) consume in parallel (partitioned), call providers with the provider's idempotency keys, and dedup by notification ID (at-least-once → effectively-once).
  • Retries + backoff + DLT for failures; rate limiting per provider; circuit breakers per channel.
  • Preferences/templating service; track delivery status; allow replay.
  • Scale by partitions/consumers; monitor lag and provider error rates.

Use backward-compatible, expand-contract migrations so the old and new app versions both work against the schema during rollout: (1) Expand — add new columns/tables (nullable, no breaking changes); deploy code that writes both old and new. (2) Migrate/backfill data in batches. (3) Switch reads to the new structure (canary/rolling). (4) Contract — remove old columns only after no version uses them. Never deploy a migration incompatible with the currently-running version. Combine with rolling/blue-green deploys, feature flags, and online DDL tools for large tables.

Deploy services in multiple regions, route users to the nearest via GeoDNS/global load balancer (latency-based). Data strategy is the crux: active-active with regional data ownership + async cross-region replication (eventual consistency, conflict resolution by region-partitioned keys), or active-passive (one writer region, others read-only/standby — simpler consistency, failover RTO). Replicate Kafka with MirrorMaker. Handle data residency regulations. On a region outage, failover traffic (accepting some RPO for in-flight data). Trade-offs: latency vs consistency vs complexity — decide per data type.

15 synchronous hops = a distributed monolith with summed latency and fragile failure propagation. Redesign: (1) Parallelize independent calls (CompletableFuture) instead of chaining. (2) Pre-compute a read model (CQRS) so the action reads one denormalized view instead of fanning out. (3) Make non-critical work asynchronous (fire events, return immediately, process in background). (4) Reconsider service boundaries — if 15 services are always called together, the granularity is wrong. (5) Add caching for stable data. Goal: minimize synchronous fan-out on the critical path.

  • Write side: commands validated against the aggregate; append immutable events (OrderCreated, ItemAdded, Paid) to an event store; optimistic concurrency via aggregate version; publish events (Outbox) to Kafka.
  • Read side: projectors consume events and build denormalized read models (order summary, search index) for fast queries — eventually consistent.
  • Rebuild: replay all events into a fresh projection (new view, schema change, bug fix); use snapshots to bound replay cost.
  • Trade-offs: full audit + temporal queries + flexible read models, but complexity, schema evolution (upcasters), and eventual consistency.

Apply consistency per operation, not system-wide. For the critical operation (e.g. final payment capture, inventory decrement): keep the data and the operation within a single service/database so a local ACID transaction guarantees strong consistency; use atomic conditional updates and pessimistic/optimistic locking. Avoid spreading that invariant across services (which forces Sagas/eventual consistency). If it must span services, use a synchronous orchestrated flow with compensation and accept it's a CP choice (may reject under partition). The principle: co-locate strongly-consistent invariants; reserve eventual consistency for the rest.

Use a token-bucket per client key (allows bursts up to bucket size, refills at the tier's sustained rate) backed by Redis for distributed correctness. Map API key → tier → (rate, burst, quota); enforce at the gateway. Add per-resource limits for expensive endpoints, daily quotas alongside per-second rates, and fairness (per-user limits so one user can't consume a shared pool). Return 429 with Retry-After and rate-limit headers (X-RateLimit-*). Consider sliding-window for smoother enforcement, and graceful soft-limits/warnings before hard blocks.

Combine: bounded queues + bounded thread pools (reject when full rather than queueing infinitely → fast failure), CallerRuns / backpressure so producers slow down, load shedding (drop or 503 low-priority requests when overloaded, protect critical paths), circuit breakers + timeouts to stop waiting on saturated dependencies, and autoscaling on the right signal. Adaptive concurrency limits (e.g. Netflix concurrency-limits) dynamically cap in-flight requests. The goal: degrade gracefully and stay up for as many requests as capacity allows, rather than collapsing entirely. Shed early, fail fast, prioritize.

Sync hops: propagate context via headers — Authorization (token relay), tenant ID, and W3C traceparent — using client interceptors that inject them on every outbound call; each service rebuilds the SecurityContext/MDC from headers. Async hops (messaging): put the context in message headers (trace ID, tenant, a service token), and the consumer restores it before processing. Use Micrometer context-propagation / OpenTelemetry to automate trace context across threads and brokers. Always clear context after processing (pooled threads) to prevent bleed. Never trust client-supplied tenant without validating it against the verified identity.

  • Ingest: Kafka with enough partitions for parallelism; producers batch + compress; acks=all with adequate replication.
  • Process: horizontally-scaled consumers / Kafka Streams or Flink for stateful work; partition by key for ordering + parallelism.
  • Backpressure: pull model buffers spikes; size retention for catch-up.
  • Storage: write to scalable stores (time-series/columnar/object storage); pre-aggregate; CQRS read models for queries.
  • Resilience & cost: idempotent processing, DLT, autoscaling, monitoring lag; consider tiered storage and sampling for non-critical data.

The crux: partitioning for parallelism, async buffering, and avoiding any single-threaded chokepoint.

When a hot key expires, thousands of concurrent requests miss simultaneously and all hit the database (stampede). Prevent with: request coalescing / single-flight (only one request recomputes; others wait for the result — e.g. Caffeine's loading cache, or a per-key lock), probabilistic early expiration (refresh before TTL ends, jittered, so not everything expires at once), stale-while-revalidate (serve stale data while one request refreshes in the background), and jittered TTLs (avoid synchronized expiry). Detect via DB load spikes correlated with cache TTL boundaries and cache hit-ratio dips. Bound DB concurrency as a backstop.

Use a centralized feature-flag service (LaunchDarkly/Unleash or config-backed) with flags evaluated per request using context (user %, region, tier). Roll out by ramping the percentage and monitoring metrics; a kill switch instantly disables a feature globally without redeploy. Keep flag-gated code behind a Strategy so removing a flag is a localized change. Ensure flags propagate consistently (cache with short TTL + push updates), default to safe-off on flag-service failure (fail closed for risky features), and clean up stale flags. Combine with canary deploys for infra + flags for behavior.

Unbounded growth: set a TTL/expiry on idempotency keys (e.g. 24–72h — long enough to cover retries) in Redis or a cleanup job for the DB table. Concurrency race: rely on a unique constraint / atomic insert — both requests try to insert the key; the DB lets exactly one succeed, the other gets a conflict and must wait for and return the first's result (store result keyed by the idempotency key; the loser polls/reads it). Don't use check-then-insert in app code (TOCTOU race). Atomic claim + stored result + TTL solves both correctness and growth.

Instrument all three pillars (logs+metrics+traces, correlation IDs) and define SLOs per service. Alert on symptoms, not causes (alert fatigue): the RED metrics (Rate, Errors, Duration) breaching SLO — e.g. error rate > X%, p99 latency > threshold, and SLO error-budget burn rate (page on fast burn, ticket on slow burn). Also: saturation (CPU/mem/queue/lag), health-check failures, and key business metrics (orders/min dropping). Avoid alerting on every CPU blip. Use multi-window burn-rate alerts, runbooks per alert, and dashboards for diagnosis. Page humans only for user-impacting, actionable issues.

This read-heavy/write-rare profile suits CQRS + replication. Writes go to a single consistent primary (strong consistency, ACID) — rare, so the bottleneck is acceptable. Reads are served from globally-distributed replicas / read models / CDN caches for low latency and high availability (eventual consistency, acceptable since writes are infrequent and reads tolerate slight staleness). Propagate changes via events/replication; invalidate caches on write. Use compacted topics or materialized views for the read state. For the rare read-after-write need, route those specific reads to the primary. Optimizes the common case (reads) without sacrificing write correctness.
